<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XIX.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>THE youth stared at the land in front of him.



<P>Its foliages now seemed to veil powers and hor-



<P>rors.&nbsp; He was unaware of the machinery of orders



<P>that started the charge, although from the cor-



<P>ners of his eyes he saw an officer, who looked



<P>like a boy a-horseback, come galloping, waving



<P>his hat.&nbsp; Suddenly he felt a straining and heaving



<P>among the men.&nbsp; The line fell slowly forward



<P>like a toppling wall, and, with a convulsive gasp



<P>that was intended for a cheer, the regiment began



<P>its journey.&nbsp; The youth was pushed and jostled



<P>for a moment before he understood the move-



<P>ment at all, but directly he lunged ahead and



<P>began to run.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He fixed his eye upon a distant and promi-



<P>nent clump of trees where he had concluded the



<P>enemy were to be met, and he ran toward it as



<P>toward a goal.&nbsp; He had believed throughout that



<P>it was a mere question of getting over an unpleas-



<P>ant matter as quickly as possible, and he ran

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>179



<P>desperately, as if pursued for a murder.&nbsp; His



<P>face was drawn hard and tight with the stress of



<P>his endeavor.&nbsp; His eyes were fixed in a lurid



<P>glare.&nbsp; And with his soiled and disordered dress,



<P>his red and inflamed features surmounted by the



<P>dingy rag with its spot of blood, his wildly



<P>swinging rifle and banging accouterments, he



<P>looked to be an insane soldier.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As the regiment swung from its position out



<P>into a cleared space the woods and thickets be-



<P>fore it awakened.&nbsp; Yellow flames leaped toward



<P>it from many directions.&nbsp; The forest made a tre-



<P>mendous objection.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The line lurched straight for a moment.&nbsp; Then



<P>the right wing swung forward; it in turn was



<P>surpassed by the left.&nbsp; Afterward the center



<P>careered to the front until the regiment was a



<P>wedge-shaped mass, but an instant later the



<P>opposition of the bushes, trees, and uneven places



<P>on the ground split the command and scattered



<P>it into detached clusters.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth, light-footed, was unconsciously in



<P>advance.&nbsp; His eyes still kept note of the clump of



<P>trees.&nbsp; From all places near it the clannish yell



<P>of the enemy could be heard.&nbsp; The little flames



<P>of rifles leaped from it.&nbsp; The song of the bullets



<P>was in the air and shells snarled among the tree-



<P>tops.&nbsp; One tumbled directly into the middle of a



<P>hurrying group and exploded in crimson fury.



<P>There was an instant's spectacle of a man, almost



<P>over it, throwing up his hands to shield his eyes.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Other men, punched by bullets, fell in gro-



<P>tesque agonies.&nbsp; The regiment left a coherent



<P>trail of bodies.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They had passed into a clearer atmosphere.



<P>There was an effect like a revelation in the new



<P>appearance of the landscape.&nbsp; Some men work-



<P>ing madly at a battery were plain to them, and



<P>the opposing infantry's lines were defined by the



<P>gray walls and fringes of smoke.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It seemed to the youth that he saw every-



<P>thing.&nbsp; Each blade of the green grass was bold



<P>and clear.&nbsp; He thought that he was aware of



<P>every change in the thin, transparent vapor that



<P>floated idly in sheets.&nbsp; The brown or gray trunks



<P>of the trees showed each roughness of their sur-



<P>faces.&nbsp; And the men of the regiment, with their



<P>starting eyes and sweating faces, running madly,



<P>or falling, as if thrown headlong, to queer,



<P>heaped-up corpses--all were comprehended.&nbsp; His



<P>mind took a mechanical but firm impression, so



<P>that afterward everything was pictured and ex-



<P>plained to him, save why he himself was there.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But there was a frenzy made from this furious



<P>rush.&nbsp; The men, pitching forward insanely, had



<P>burst into cheerings, moblike and barbaric, but



<P>tuned in strange keys that can arouse the dullard



<P>and the stoic.&nbsp; It made a mad enthusiasm that, it



<P>seemed, would be incapable of checking itself



<P>before granite and brass.&nbsp; There was the deli-



<P>rium that encounters despair and death, and is



<P>heedless and blind to the odds.&nbsp; It is a temporary



<P>but sublime absence of selfishness.&nbsp; And because



<P>it was of this order was the reason, perhaps, why



<P>the youth wondered, afterward, what reasons he



<P>could have had for being there.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Presently the straining pace ate up the ener-



<P>gies of the men.&nbsp; As if by agreement, the leaders



<P>began to slacken their speed.&nbsp; The volleys di-



<P>rected against them had had a seeming windlike



<P>effect.&nbsp; The regiment snorted and blew.&nbsp; Among



<P>some stolid trees it began to falter and hesitate.



<P>The men, staring intently, began to wait for some



<P>of the distant walls of smoke to move and dis-



<P>close to them the scene.&nbsp; Since much of their



<P>strength and their breath had vanished, they re-



<P>turned to caution.&nbsp; They were become men



<P>again.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth had a vague belief that he had run



<P>miles, and he thought, in a way, that he was now



<P>in some new and unknown land.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The moment the regiment ceased its advance



<P>the protesting splutter of musketry became a



<P>steadied roar.&nbsp; Long and accurate fringes of



<P>smoke spread out.&nbsp; From the top of a small hill



<P>came level belchings of yellow flame that caused



<P>an inhuman whistling in the air.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The men, halted, had opportunity to see some



<P>of their comrades dropping with moans and



<P>shrieks.&nbsp; A few lay under foot, still or wailing.



<P>And now for an instant the men stood, their rifles



<P>slack in their hands, and watched the regiment



<P>dwindle.&nbsp; They appeared dazed and stupid.&nbsp; This



<P>spectacle seemed to paralyze them, overcome



<P>them with a fatal fascination.&nbsp; They stared wood-



<P>enly at the sights, and, lowering their eyes, looked



<P>from face to face.&nbsp; It was a strange pause, and a



<P>strange silence.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Then, above the sounds of the outside commo-



<P>tion, arose the roar of the lieutenant.&nbsp; He strode



<P>suddenly forth, his infantile features black with



<P>rage.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Come on, yeh fools!" he bellowed.&nbsp; "Come



<P>on!&nbsp; Yeh can't stay here.&nbsp; Yeh must come on."



<P>He said more, but much of it could not be under-



<P>stood.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He started rapidly forward, with his head



<P>turned toward the men.&nbsp; "Come on," he was



<P>shouting.&nbsp; The men stared with blank and yokel-



<P>like eyes at him.&nbsp; He was obliged to halt and



<P>retrace his steps.&nbsp; He stood then with his back



<P>to the enemy and delivered gigantic curses into



<P>the faces of the men.&nbsp; His body vibrated from



<P>the weight and force of his imprecations.&nbsp; And



<P>he could string oaths with the facility of a maiden



<P>who strings beads.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The friend of the youth aroused.&nbsp; Lurching



<P>suddenly forward and dropping to his knees, he



<P>fired an angry shot at the persistent woods.&nbsp; This



<P>action awakened the men.&nbsp; They huddled no



<P>more like sheep.&nbsp; They seemed suddenly to be-



<P>think them of their weapons, and at once com-



<P>menced firing.&nbsp; Belabored by their officers, they



<P>began to move forward.&nbsp; The regiment, involved



<P>like a cart involved in mud and muddle, started



<P>unevenly with many jolts and jerks.&nbsp; The men



<P>stopped now every few paces to fire and load,



<P>and in this manner moved slowly on from trees



<P>to trees.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The flaming opposition in their front grew



<P>with their advance until it seemed that all for-



<P>ward ways were barred by the thin leaping



<P>tongues, and off to the right an ominous demon-



<P>stration could sometimes be dimly discerned.



<P>The smoke lately generated was in confusing



<P>clouds that made it difficult for the regiment to



<P>proceed with intelligence.&nbsp; As he passed through



<P>each curling mass the youth wondered what



<P>would confront him on the farther side.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The command went painfully forward until an



<P>open space interposed between them and the



<P>lurid lines.&nbsp; Here, crouching and cowering be-



<P>hind some trees, the men clung with desperation,



<P>as if threatened by a wave.&nbsp; They looked wild-



<P>eyed, and as if amazed at this furious disturbance



<P>they had stirred.&nbsp; In the storm there was an



<P>ironical expression of their importance.&nbsp; The



<P>faces of the men, too, showed a lack of a certain



<P>feeling of responsibility for being there.&nbsp; It was



<P>as if they had been driven.&nbsp; It was the dominant



<P>animal failing to remember in the supreme mo-



<P>ments the forceful causes of various superficial



<P>qualities.&nbsp; The whole affair seemed incompre-



<P>hensible to many of them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As they halted thus the lieutenant again be-



<P>gan to bellow profanely.&nbsp; Regardless of the vin-



<P>dictive threats of the bullets, he went about



<P>coaxing, berating, and bedamning.&nbsp; His lips,



<P>that were habitually in a soft and childlike curve,



<P>were now writhed into unholy contortions.&nbsp; He



<P>swore by all possible deities.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Once he grabbed the youth by the arm.



<P>"Come on, yeh lunkhead!" he roared.&nbsp; "Come



<P>on!&nbsp; We'll all git killed if we stay here.&nbsp; We've



<P>on'y got t' go across that lot.&nbsp; An' then"--the



<P>remainder of his idea disappeared in a blue haze



<P>of curses.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth stretched forth his arm.&nbsp; "Cross



<P>there?"&nbsp; His mouth was puckered in doubt and



<P>awe.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Certainly.&nbsp; Jest 'cross th' lot!&nbsp; We can't



<P>stay here," screamed the lieutenant.&nbsp; He poked



<P>his face close to the youth and waved his ban-



<P>daged hand.&nbsp; "Come on!"&nbsp; Presently he grap-



<P>pled with him as if for a wrestling bout.&nbsp; It was



<P>as if he planned to drag the youth by the ear on



<P>to the assault.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The private felt a sudden unspeakable indig-



<P>nation against his officer.&nbsp; He wrenched fiercely



<P>and shook him off.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Come on herself, then," he yelled.&nbsp; There



<P>was a bitter challenge in his voice.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They galloped together down the regimental



<P>front.&nbsp; The friend scrambled after them.&nbsp; In front



<P>of the colors the three men began to bawl:



<P>"Come on! come on!"&nbsp; They danced and gy-



<P>rated like tortured savages.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The flag, obedient to these appeals, bended its



<P>glittering form and swept toward them.&nbsp; The



<P>men wavered in indecision for a moment, and then



<P>with a long, wailful cry the dilapidated regiment



<P>surged forward and began its new journey.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Over the field went the scurrying mass.&nbsp; It



<P>was a handful of men splattered into the faces of



<P>the enemy.&nbsp; Toward it instantly sprang the yel-



<P>low tongues.&nbsp; A vast quantity of blue smoke



<P>hung before them.&nbsp; A mighty banging made ears



<P>valueless.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth ran like a madman to reach the



<P>woods before a bullet could discover him.&nbsp; He



<P>ducked his head low, like a football player.&nbsp; In



<P>his haste his eyes almost closed, and the scene was



<P>a wild blur.&nbsp; Pulsating saliva stood at the corners



<P>of his mouth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Within him, as he hurled himself forward, was



<P>born a love, a despairing fondness for this flag



<P>which was near him.&nbsp; It was a creation of beauty



<P>and invulnerability.&nbsp; It was a goddess, radiant,



<P>that bended its form with an imperious gesture to



<P>him.&nbsp; It was a woman, red and white, hating and



<P>loving, that called him with the voice of his



<P>hopes.&nbsp; Because no harm could come to it he en-



<P>dowed it with power.&nbsp; He kept near, as if it



<P>could be a saver of lives, and an imploring cry



<P>went from his mind.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In the mad scramble he was aware that the



<P>color sergeant flinched suddenly, as if struck by a



<P>bludgeon.&nbsp; He faltered, and then became motion-



<P>less, save for his quivering knees.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He made a spring and a clutch at the pole.



<P>At the same instant his friend grabbed it from the



<P>other side.&nbsp; They jerked at it, stout and furious,



<P>but the color sergeant was dead, and the corpse



<P>would not relinquish its trust.&nbsp; For a moment



<P>there was a grim encounter.&nbsp; The dead man,



<P>swinging with bended back, seemed to be obsti-



<P>nately tugging, in ludicrous and awful ways, for



<P>the possession of the flag.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It was past in an instant of time.&nbsp; They



<P>wrenched the flag furiously from the dead man,



<P>and, as they turned again, the corpse swayed for-



<P>ward with bowed head.&nbsp; One arm swung high,



<P>and the curved hand fell with heavy protest on



<P>the friend's unheeding shoulder.



<P>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XVIII.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>THE ragged line had respite for some min-



<P>utes, but during its pause the struggle in the



<P>forest became magnified until the trees seemed to



<P>quiver from the firing and the ground to shake



<P>from the rushing of the men.&nbsp; The voices of the



<P>cannon were mingled in a long and interminable



<P>row.&nbsp; It seemed difficult to live in such an atmos-



<P>phere.&nbsp; The chests of the men strained for a bit



<P>of freshness, and their throats craved water.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was one shot through the body, who



<P>raised a cry of bitter lamentation when came this



<P>lull.&nbsp; Perhaps he had been calling out during



<P>the fighting also, but at that time no one had



<P>heard him.&nbsp; But now the men turned at the woe-



<P>ful complaints of him upon the ground.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Who is it?&nbsp; Who is it?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"It's Jimmie Rogers.&nbsp; Jimmie Rogers."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When their eyes first encountered him there



<P>was a sudden halt, as if they feared to go near.



<P>He was thrashing about in the grass, twisting his

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>171



<P>shuddering body into many strange postures.



<P>He was screaming loudly.&nbsp; This instant's hesita-



<P>tion seemed to fill him with a tremendous, fantas-



<P>tic contempt, and he damned them in shrieked



<P>sentences.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth's friend had a geographical illusion



<P>concerning a stream, and he obtained permission



<P>to go for some water.&nbsp; Immediately canteens



<P>were showered upon him.&nbsp; "Fill mine, will



<P>yeh?"&nbsp; "Bring me some, too."&nbsp; "And me, too."



<P>He departed, ladened.&nbsp; The youth went with his



<P>friend, feeling a desire to throw his heated body



<P>onto the stream and, soaking there, drink quarts.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They made a hurried search for the supposed



<P>stream, but did not find it.&nbsp; "No water here,"



<P>said the youth.&nbsp; They turned without delay and



<P>began to retrace their steps.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>From their position as they again faced to-



<P>ward the place of the fighting, they could of



<P>course comprehend a greater amount of the bat-



<P>tle than when their visions had been blurred by



<P>the hurling smoke of the line.&nbsp; They could see



<P>dark stretches winding along the land, and on



<P>one cleared space there was a row of guns mak-



<P>ing gray clouds, which were filled with large



<P>flashes of orange-colored flame.&nbsp; Over some foli-



<P>age they could see the roof of a house.&nbsp; One win-



<P>dow, glowing a deep murder red, shone squarely



<P>through the leaves.&nbsp; From the edifice a tall lean-



<P>ing tower of smoke went far into the sky.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Looking over their own troops, they saw



<P>mixed masses slowly getting into regular form.



<P>The sunlight made twinkling points of the bright



<P>steel.&nbsp; To the rear there was a glimpse of a dis-



<P>tant roadway as it curved over a slope.&nbsp; It was



<P>crowded with retreating infantry.&nbsp; From all the



<P>interwoven forest arose the smoke and bluster



<P>of the battle.&nbsp; The air was always occupied by



<P>a blaring.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Near where they stood shells were flip-flap-



<P>ping and hooting.&nbsp; Occasional bullets buzzed in



<P>the air and spanged into tree trunks.&nbsp; Wounded



<P>men and other stragglers were slinking through



<P>the woods.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Looking down an aisle of the grove, the



<P>youth and his companion saw a jangling general



<P>and his staff almost ride upon a wounded man,



<P>who was crawling on his hands and knees.&nbsp; The



<P>general reined strongly at his charger's opened



<P>and foamy mouth and guided it with dexterous



<P>horsemanship past the man.&nbsp; The latter scram-



<P>bled in wild and torturing haste.&nbsp; His strength



<P>evidently failed him as he reached a place of



<P>safety.&nbsp; One of his arms suddenly weakened, and



<P>he fell, sliding over upon his back.&nbsp; He lay



<P>stretched out, breathing gently.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A moment later the small, creaking cavalcade



<P>was directly in front of the two soldiers.&nbsp; An-



<P>other officer, riding with the skillful abandon of a



<P>cowboy, galloped his horse to a position directly



<P>before the general.&nbsp; The two unnoticed foot sol-



<P>diers made a little show of going on, but they



<P>lingered near in the desire to overhear the con-



<P>versation.&nbsp; Perhaps, they thought, some great



<P>inner historical things would be said.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The general, whom the boys knew as the com-



<P>mander of their division, looked at the other



<P>officer and spoke coolly, as if he were criticising



<P>his clothes.&nbsp; "Th' enemy's formin' over there for



<P>another charge," he said.&nbsp; "It'll be directed



<P>against Whiterside, an' I fear they'll break



<P>through there unless we work like thunder t' stop



<P>them."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The other swore at his restive horse, and then



<P>cleared his throat.&nbsp; He made a gesture toward



<P>his cap.&nbsp; "It'll be hell t' pay stoppin' them," he



<P>said shortly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I presume so," remarked the general.&nbsp; Then



<P>he began to talk rapidly and in a lower tone.&nbsp; He



<P>frequently illustrated his words with a pointing



<P>finger.&nbsp; The two infantrymen could hear nothing



<P>until finally he asked: "What troops can you



<P>spare?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The officer who rode like a cowboy reflected



<P>for an instant.&nbsp; "Well," he said, "I had to order



<P>in th' 12th to help th' 76th, an' I haven't really got



<P>any.&nbsp; But there's th' 304th.&nbsp; They fight like a



<P>lot 'a mule drivers.&nbsp; I can spare them best



<P>of any."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth and his friend exchanged glances



<P>of astonishment.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The general spoke sharply.&nbsp; "Get 'em ready,



<P>then.&nbsp; I'll watch developments from here, an'



<P>send you word when t' start them.&nbsp; It'll happen



<P>in five minutes."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As the other officer tossed his fingers toward



<P>his cap and wheeling his horse, started away, the



<P>general called out to him in a sober voice: "I



<P>don't believe many of your mule drivers will get



<P>back."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The other shouted something in reply.&nbsp; He



<P>smiled.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>With scared faces, the youth and his compan-



<P>ion hurried back to the line.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>These happenings had occupied an incredibly



<P>short time, yet the youth felt that in them he had



<P>been made aged.&nbsp; New eyes were given to him.



<P>And the most startling thing was to learn sud-



<P>denly that he was very insignificant.&nbsp; The officer



<P>spoke of the regiment as if he referred to a



<P>broom.&nbsp; Some part of the woods needed sweep-



<P>ing, perhaps, and he merely indicated a broom in



<P>a tone properly indifferent to its fate.&nbsp; It was



<P>war, no doubt, but it appeared strange.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As the two boys approached the line, the lieu-



<P>tenant perceived them and swelled with wrath.



<P>"Fleming--Wilson--how long does it take yeh



<P>to git water, anyhow--where yeh been to."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But his oration ceased as he saw their eyes,



<P>which were large with great tales.&nbsp; "We're goin'



<P>t' charge--we're goin' t' charge!" cried the



<P>youth's friend, hastening with his news.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Charge?" said the lieutenant.&nbsp; "Charge?



<P>Well, b'Gawd!&nbsp; Now, this is real fightin'."&nbsp; Over



<P>his soiled countenance there went a boastful



<P>smile.&nbsp; "Charge?&nbsp; Well, b'Gawd!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A little group of soldiers surrounded the two



<P>youths.&nbsp; "Are we, sure 'nough?&nbsp; Well, I'll be



<P>derned!&nbsp; Charge?&nbsp; What fer?&nbsp; What at?&nbsp; Wil-



<P>son, you're lyin'."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I hope to die," said the youth, pitching his



<P>tones to the key of angry remonstrance.&nbsp; "Sure



<P>as shooting, I tell you."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>And his friend spoke in re-enforcement.&nbsp; "Not



<P>by a blame sight, he ain't lyin'.&nbsp; We heard 'em



<P>talkin'."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They caught sight of two mounted figures a



<P>short distance from them.&nbsp; One was the colonel



<P>of the regiment and the other was the officer who



<P>had received orders from the commander of the



<P>division.&nbsp; They were gesticulating at each other.



<P>The soldier, pointing at them, interpreted the



<P>scene.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>One man had a final objection: "How could



<P>yeh hear 'em talkin'?"&nbsp; But the men, for a large



<P>part, nodded, admitting that previously the two



<P>friends had spoken truth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They settled back into reposeful attitudes



<P>with airs of having accepted the matter.&nbsp; And



<P>they mused upon it, with a hundred varieties of



<P>expression.&nbsp; It was an engrossing thing to think



<P>about.&nbsp; Many tightened their belts carefully and



<P>hitched at their trousers.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A moment later the officers began to bustle



<P>among the men, pushing them into a more com-



<P>pact mass and into a better alignment.&nbsp; They



<P>chased those that straggled and fumed at a few



<P>men who seemed to show by their attitudes that



<P>they had decided to remain at that spot.&nbsp; They



<P>were like critical shepherds struggling with sheep.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Presently, the regiment seemed to draw itself



<P>up and heave a deep breath.&nbsp; None of the men's



<P>faces were mirrors of large thoughts.&nbsp; The sol-



<P>diers were bended and stooped like sprinters be-



<P>fore a signal.&nbsp; Many pairs of glinting eyes peered



<P>from the grimy faces toward the curtains of the



<P>deeper woods.&nbsp; They seemed to be engaged in



<P>deep calculations of time and distance.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They were surrounded by the noises of the



<P>monstrous altercation between the two armies.



<P>The world was fully interested in other matters.



<P>Apparently, the regiment had its small affair to



<P>itself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth, turning, shot a quick, inquiring



<P>glance at his friend.&nbsp; The latter returned to him



<P>the same manner of look.&nbsp; They were the only



<P>ones who possessed an inner knowledge.&nbsp; "Mule



<P>drivers--hell t' pay--don't believe many will get



<P>back."&nbsp; It was an ironical secret.&nbsp; Still, they saw



<P>no hesitation in each other's faces, and they nod-



<P>ded a mute and unprotesting assent when a shag-



<P>gy man near them said in a meek voice: "We'll



<P>git swallowed."



<P>

<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XXIV.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>THE roarings that had stretched in a long line



<P>of sound across the face of the forest began to



<P>grow intermittent and weaker.&nbsp; The stentorian



<P>speeches of the artillery continued in some dis-



<P>tant encounter, but the crashes of the musketry



<P>had almost ceased.&nbsp; The youth and his friend of



<P>a sudden looked up, feeling a deadened form of



<P>distress at the waning of these noises, which had



<P>become a part of life.&nbsp; They could see changes



<P>going on among the troops.&nbsp; There were march-



<P>ings this way and that way.&nbsp; A battery wheeled



<P>leisurely.&nbsp; On the crest of a small hill was the



<P>thick gleam of many departing muskets.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth arose.&nbsp; "Well, what now, I won-



<P>der?" he said.&nbsp; By his tone he seemed to be



<P>preparing to resent some new monstrosity in



<P>the way of dins and smashes.&nbsp; He shaded his



<P>eyes with his grimy hand and gazed over the



<P>field.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His friend also arose and stared.&nbsp; "I bet

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>226



<P>we're goin' t' git along out of this an' back over



<P>th' river," said he.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, I swan!" said the youth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They waited, watching.&nbsp; Within a little while



<P>the regiment received orders to retrace its way.



<P>The men got up grunting from the grass, regret-



<P>ting the soft repose.&nbsp; They jerked their stiffened



<P>legs, and stretched their arms over their heads.



<P>One man swore as he rubbed his eyes.&nbsp; They all



<P>groaned "O Lord!"&nbsp; They had as many objec-



<P>tions to this change as they would have had to a



<P>proposal for a new battle.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They trampled slowly back over the field



<P>across which they had run in a mad scamper.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The regiment marched until it had joined its



<P>fellows.&nbsp; The reformed brigade, in column, aimed



<P>through a wood at the road.&nbsp; Directly they were



<P>in a mass of dust-covered troops, and were



<P>trudging along in a way parallel to the enemy's



<P>lines as these had been defined by the previous



<P>turmoil.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They passed within view of a stolid white



<P>house, and saw in front of it groups of their com-



<P>rades lying in wait behind a neat breastwork.&nbsp; A



<P>row of guns were booming at a distant enemy.



<P>Shells thrown in reply were raising clouds of



<P>dust and splinters.&nbsp; Horsemen dashed along the



<P>line of intrenchments.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At this point of its march the division curved



<P>away from the field and went winding off in the



<P>direction of the river.&nbsp; When the significance of



<P>this movement had impressed itself upon the



<P>youth he turned his head and looked over his



<P>shoulder toward the trampled and debris-strewed



<P>ground.&nbsp; He breathed a breath of new satisfac-



<P>tion.&nbsp; He finally nudged his friend.&nbsp; "Well, it's



<P>all over," he said to him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His friend gazed backward.&nbsp; "B'Gawd, it



<P>is," he assented.&nbsp; They mused.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>For a time the youth was obliged to reflect



<P>in a puzzled and uncertain way.&nbsp; His mind was



<P>undergoing a subtle change.&nbsp; It took moments



<P>for it to cast off its battleful ways and resume



<P>its accustomed course of thought.&nbsp; Gradually his



<P>brain emerged from the clogged clouds, and at



<P>last he was enabled to more closely compre-



<P>hend himself and circumstance.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He understood then that the existence of shot



<P>and counter-shot was in the past.&nbsp; He had dwelt



<P>in a land of strange, squalling upheavals and had



<P>come forth.&nbsp; He had been where there was red



<P>of blood and black of passion, and he was es-



<P>caped.&nbsp; His first thoughts were given to rejoic-



<P>ings at this fact.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Later he began to study his deeds, his fail-



<P>ures, and his achievements.&nbsp; Thus, fresh from



<P>scenes where many of his usual machines of re-



<P>flection had been idle, from where he had pro-



<P>ceeded sheeplike, he struggled to marshal all his



<P>acts.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At last they marched before him clearly.



<P>From this present view point he was enabled



<P>to look upon them in spectator fashion and



<P>to criticise them with some correctness, for his



<P>new condition had already defeated certain sym-



<P>pathies.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Regarding his procession of memory he felt



<P>gleeful and unregretting, for in it his public deeds



<P>were paraded in great and shining prominence.



<P>Those performances which had been witnessed



<P>by his fellows marched now in wide purple and



<P>gold, having various deflections.&nbsp; They went



<P>gayly with music.&nbsp; It was pleasure to watch these



<P>things.&nbsp; He spent delightful minutes viewing the



<P>gilded images of memory.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He saw that he was good.&nbsp; He recalled with



<P>a thrill of joy the respectful comments of his fel-



<P>lows upon his conduct.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Nevertheless, the ghost of his flight from



<P>the first engagement appeared to him and



<P>danced.&nbsp; There were small shoutings in his



<P>brain about these matters.&nbsp; For a moment he



<P>blushed, and the light of his soul flickered with



<P>shame.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A specter of reproach came to him.&nbsp; There



<P>loomed the dogging memory of the tattered



<P>soldier--he who, gored by bullets and faint for



<P>blood, had fretted concerning an imagined wound



<P>in another; he who had loaned his last of strength



<P>and intellect for the tall soldier; he who, blind



<P>with weariness and pain, had been deserted in



<P>the field.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>For an instant a wretched chill of sweat was



<P>upon him at the thought that he might be



<P>detected in the thing.&nbsp; As he stood persistently



<P>before his vision, he gave vent to a cry of sharp



<P>irritation and agony.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His friend turned.&nbsp; "What's the matter,



<P>Henry?" he demanded.&nbsp; The youth's reply was



<P>an outburst of crimson oaths.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As he marched along the little branch-hung



<P>roadway among his prattling companions this



<P>vision of cruelty brooded over him.&nbsp; It clung



<P>near him always and darkened his view of these



<P>deeds in purple and gold.&nbsp; Whichever way his



<P>thoughts turned they were followed by the



<P>somber phantom of the desertion in the fields.



<P>He looked stealthily at his companions, feeling



<P>sure that they must discern in his face evidences



<P>of this pursuit.&nbsp; But they were plodding in



<P>ragged array, discussing with quick tongues the



<P>accomplishments of the late battle.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh, if a man should come up an' ask me, I'd



<P>say we got a dum good lickin'."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Lickin'--in yer eye!&nbsp; We ain't licked, sonny.



<P>We're goin' down here aways, swing aroun', an'



<P>come in behint 'em."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh, hush, with your comin' in behint 'em.



<P>I've seen all 'a that I wanta.&nbsp; Don't tell me about



<P>comin' in behint--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Bill Smithers, he ses he'd rather been in



<P>ten hundred battles than been in that heluva



<P>hospital.&nbsp; He ses they got shootin' in th' night-



<P>time, an' shells dropped plum among 'em in th'



<P>hospital.&nbsp; He ses sech hollerin' he never see."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Hasbrouck?&nbsp; He's th' best off'cer in this



<P>here reg'ment.&nbsp; He's a whale."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Didn't I tell yeh we'd come aroun' in behint



<P>'em?&nbsp; Didn't I tell yeh so?&nbsp; We--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh, shet yeh mouth!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>For a time this pursuing recollection of the



<P>tattered man took all elation from the youth's



<P>veins.&nbsp; He saw his vivid error, and he was afraid



<P>that it would stand before him all his life.&nbsp; He



<P>took no share in the chatter of his comrades, nor



<P>did he look at them or know them, save when he



<P>felt sudden suspicion that they were seeing his



<P>thoughts and scrutinizing each detail of the scene



<P>with the tattered soldier.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Yet gradually he mustered force to put the sin



<P>at a distance.&nbsp; And at last his eyes seemed to



<P>open to some new ways.&nbsp; He found that he could



<P>look back upon the brass and bombast of his



<P>earlier gospels and see them truly.&nbsp; He was



<P>gleeful when he discovered that he now despised



<P>them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>With this conviction came a store of assur-



<P>ance.&nbsp; He felt a quiet manhood, nonassertive but



<P>of sturdy and strong blood.&nbsp; He knew that he



<P>would no more quail before his guides wher-



<P>ever they should point.&nbsp; He had been to touch



<P>the great death, and found that, after all, it was



<P>but the great death.&nbsp; He was a man.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>So it came to pass that as he trudged from



<P>the place of blood and wrath his soul changed.



<P>He came from hot plowshares to prospects of



<P>clover tranquilly, and it was as if hot plowshares



<P>were not.&nbsp; Scars faded as flowers.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It rained.&nbsp; The procession of weary soldiers



<P>became a bedraggled train, despondent and



<P>muttering, marching with churning effort in a



<P>trough of liquid brown mud under a low,



<P>wretched sky.&nbsp; Yet the youth smiled, for he saw



<P>that the world was a world for him, though many



<P>discovered it to be made of oaths and walking



<P>sticks.&nbsp; He had rid himself of the red sickness of



<P>battle.&nbsp; The sultry nightmare was in the past.



<P>He had been an animal blistered and sweating in



<P>the heat and pain of war.&nbsp; He turned now with a



<P>lover's thirst to images of tranquil skies, fresh



<P>meadows, cool brooks--an existence of soft and



<P>eternal peace.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Over the river a golden ray of sun came



<P>through the hosts of leaden rain clouds.

<BR>&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+1>The End</FONT></B>

<BR>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER VIII.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>THE trees began softly to sing a hymn of twi-



<P>light.&nbsp; The sun sank until slanted bronze rays



<P>struck the forest.&nbsp; There was a lull in the noises



<P>of insects as if they had bowed their beaks and



<P>were making a devotional pause.&nbsp; There was



<P>silence save for the chanted chorus of the trees.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Then, upon this stillness, there suddenly broke



<P>a tremendous clangor of sounds.&nbsp; A crimson roar



<P>came from the distance.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth stopped.&nbsp; He was transfixed by



<P>this terrific medley of all noises.&nbsp; It was as if



<P>worlds were being rended.&nbsp; There was the rip-



<P>ping sound of musketry and the breaking crash



<P>of the artillery.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His mind flew in all directions.&nbsp; He conceived



<P>the two armies to be at each other panther



<P>fashion.&nbsp; He listened for a time.&nbsp; Then he began



<P>to run in the direction of the battle.&nbsp; He saw



<P>that it was an ironical thing for him to be run-



<P>ning thus toward that which he had been at such



<P>pains to avoid.&nbsp; But he said, in substance, to him-



<P>self that if the earth and the moon were about to



<P>clash, many persons would doubtless plan to get



<P>upon the roofs to witness the collision.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As he ran, he became aware that the forest



<P>had stopped its music, as if at last becoming



<P>capable of hearing the foreign sounds.&nbsp; The trees



<P>hushed and stood motionless.&nbsp; Everything seemed



<P>to be listening to the crackle and clatter and ear-



<P>shaking thunder.&nbsp; The chorus pealed over the



<P>still earth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It suddenly occurred to the youth that the



<P>fight in which he had been was, after all, but



<P>perfunctory popping.&nbsp; In the hearing of this



<P>present din he was doubtful if he had seen real



<P>battle scenes.&nbsp; This uproar explained a celes-



<P>tial battle; it was tumbling hordes a-struggle in



<P>the air.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Reflecting, he saw a sort of a humor in the



<P>point of view of himself and his fellows during



<P>the late encounter.&nbsp; They had taken themselves



<P>and the enemy very seriously and had imagined



<P>that they were deciding the war.&nbsp; Individuals



<P>must have supposed that they were cutting the



<P>letters of their names deep into everlasting tablets



<P>of brass, or enshrining their reputations forever in



<P>the hearts of their countrymen, while, as to fact,



<P>the affair would appear in printed reports under a



<P>meek and immaterial title.&nbsp; But he saw that it was



<P>good, else, he said, in battle every one would



<P>surely run save forlorn hopes and their ilk.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He went rapidly on.&nbsp; He wished to come to



<P>the edge of the forest that he might peer out.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As he hastened, there passed through his mind



<P>pictures of stupendous conflicts.&nbsp; His accumulated



<P>thought upon such subjects was used to form



<P>scenes.&nbsp; The noise was as the voice of an eloquent



<P>being, describing.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Sometimes the brambles formed chains and



<P>tried to hold him back.&nbsp; Trees, confronting him,



<P>stretched out their arms and forbade him to pass.



<P>After its previous hostility this new resistance of



<P>the forest filled him with a fine bitterness.&nbsp; It



<P>seemed that Nature could not be quite ready to



<P>kill him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But he obstinately took roundabout ways, and



<P>presently he was where he could see long gray



<P>walls of vapor where lay battle lines.&nbsp; The voices



<P>of cannon shook him.&nbsp; The musketry sounded in



<P>long irregular surges that played havoc with his



<P>ears.&nbsp; He stood regardant for a moment.&nbsp; His



<P>eyes had an awestruck expression.&nbsp; He gawked



<P>in the direction of the fight.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Presently he proceeded again on his forward



<P>way.&nbsp; The battle was like the grinding of an



<P>immense and terrible machine to him.&nbsp; Its com-



<P>plexities and powers, its grim processes, fascinated



<P>him.&nbsp; He must go close and see it produce



<P>corpses.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He came to a fence and clambered over it.



<P>On the far side, the ground was littered with



<P>clothes and guns.&nbsp; A newspaper, folded up, lay



<P>in the dirt.&nbsp; A dead soldier was stretched with



<P>his face hidden in his arm.&nbsp; Farther off there



<P>was a group of four or five corpses keeping



<P>mournful company.&nbsp; A hot sun had blazed upon



<P>the spot.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In this place the youth felt that he was an



<P>invader.&nbsp; This forgotten part of the battle ground



<P>was owned by the dead men, and he hurried, in



<P>the vague apprehension that one of the swollen



<P>forms would rise and tell him to begone.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He came finally to a road from which he



<P>could see in the distance dark and agitated



<P>bodies of troops, smoke-fringed.&nbsp; In the lane



<P>was a blood-stained crowd streaming to the rear.



<P>The wounded men were cursing, groaning, and



<P>wailing.&nbsp; In the air, always, was a mighty swell



<P>of sound that it seemed could sway the earth.



<P>With the courageous words of the artillery and



<P>the spiteful sentences of the musketry mingled



<P>red cheers.&nbsp; And from this region of noises came



<P>the steady current of the maimed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>One of the wounded men had a shoeful of



<P>blood.&nbsp; He hopped like a schoolboy in a game.



<P>He was laughing hysterically.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>One was swearing that he had been shot in the



<P>arm through the commanding general's misman-



<P>agement of the army.&nbsp; One was marching with



<P>an air imitative of some sublime drum major.



<P>Upon his features was an unholy mixture of



<P>merriment and agony.&nbsp; As he marched he sang



<P>a bit of doggerel in a high and quavering voice:

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Sing a song 'a vic'try,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp; A pocketful 'a bullets,



<P>Five an' twenty dead men



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp; Baked in a--pie."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Parts of the procession limped and staggered to



<P>this tune.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Another had the gray seal of death already



<P>upon his face.&nbsp; His lips were curled in hard lines



<P>and his teeth were clinched.&nbsp; His hands were



<P>bloody from where he had pressed them upon his



<P>wound.&nbsp; He seemed to be awaiting the moment



<P>when he should pitch headlong.&nbsp; He stalked like



<P>the specter of a soldier, his eyes burning with the



<P>power of a stare into the unknown.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There were some who proceeded sullenly, full



<P>of anger at their wounds, and ready to turn upon



<P>anything as an obscure cause.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>An officer was carried along by two privates.



<P>He was peevish.&nbsp; "Don't joggle so, Johnson, yeh



<P>fool," he cried.&nbsp; "Think m' leg is made of iron?



<P>If yeh can't carry me decent, put me down an'



<P>let some one else do it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He bellowed at the tottering crowd who



<P>blocked the quick march of his bearers.&nbsp; "Say,



<P>make way there, can't yeh?&nbsp; Make way, dickens



<P>take it all."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They sulkily parted and went to the road-



<P>sides.&nbsp; As he was carried past they made pert



<P>remarks to him.&nbsp; When he raged in reply and



<P>threatened them, they told him to be damned.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The shoulder of one of the tramping bearers



<P>knocked heavily against the spectral soldier who



<P>was staring into the unknown.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth joined this crowd and marched



<P>along with it.&nbsp; The torn bodies expressed the



<P>awful machinery in which the men had been



<P>entangled.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Orderlies and couriers occasionally broke



<P>through the throng in the roadway, scattering



<P>wounded men right and left, galloping on fol-



<P>lowed by howls.&nbsp; The melancholy march was



<P>continually disturbed by the messengers, and



<P>sometimes by bustling batteries that came swing-



<P>ing and thumping down upon them, the officers



<P>shouting orders to clear the way.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was a tattered man, fouled with dust,



<P>blood and powder stain from hair to shoes, who



<P>trudged quietly at the youth's side.&nbsp; He was lis-



<P>tening with eagerness and much humility to the



<P>lurid descriptions of a bearded sergeant.&nbsp; His



<P>lean features wore an expression of awe and ad-



<P>miration.&nbsp; He was like a listener in a country



<P>store to wondrous tales told among the sugar



<P>barrels.&nbsp; He eyed the story-teller with unspeak-



<P>able wonder.&nbsp; His mouth was agape in yokel



<P>fashion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The sergeant, taking note of this, gave pause



<P>to his elaborate history while he administered a



<P>sardonic comment.&nbsp; "Be keerful, honey, you 'll



<P>be a-ketchin' flies," he said.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The tattered man shrank back abashed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After a time he began to sidle near to the



<P>youth, and in a different way try to make him a



<P>friend.&nbsp; His voice was gentle as a girl's voice



<P>and his eyes were pleading.&nbsp; The youth saw



<P>with surprise that the soldier had two wounds,



<P>one in the head, bound with a blood-soaked rag,



<P>and the other in the arm, making that member



<P>dangle like a broken bough.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After they had walked together for some time



<P>the tattered man mustered sufficient courage to



<P>speak.&nbsp; "Was pretty good fight, wa'n't it?"



<P>he timidly said.&nbsp; The youth, deep in thought,



<P>glanced up at the bloody and grim figure with



<P>its lamblike eyes.&nbsp; "What?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Was pretty good fight, wa'n't it?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes," said the youth shortly.&nbsp; He quick-



<P>ened his pace.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But the other hobbled industriously after him.



<P>There was an air of apology in his manner, but



<P>he evidently thought that he needed only to talk



<P>for a time, and the youth would perceive that he



<P>was a good fellow.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Was pretty good fight, wa'n't it?" he began



<P>in a small voice, and then he achieved the forti-



<P>tude to continue.&nbsp; "Dern me if I ever see fellers



<P>fight so.&nbsp; Laws, how they did fight!&nbsp; I knowed



<P>th' boys 'd like when they onct got square at it.



<P>Th' boys ain't had no fair chanct up t' now, but



<P>this time they showed what they was.&nbsp; I knowed



<P>it 'd turn out this way.&nbsp; Yeh can't lick them boys.



<P>No, sir!&nbsp; They're fighters, they be."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He breathed a deep breath of humble ad-



<P>miration.&nbsp; He had looked at the youth for en-



<P>couragement several times.&nbsp; He received none,



<P>but gradually he seemed to get absorbed in his



<P>subject.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I was talkin' 'cross pickets with a boy from



<P>Georgie, onct, an' that boy, he ses, 'Your fellers



<P>'ll all run like hell when they onct hearn a gun,'



<P>he ses.&nbsp; 'Mebbe they will,' I ses, 'but I don't



<P>b'lieve none of it,' I ses; 'an' b'jiminey,' I ses back



<P>t' 'um, 'mebbe your fellers 'll all run like hell



<P>when they onct hearn a gun,' I ses.&nbsp; He larfed.



<P>Well, they didn't run t' day, did they, hey?&nbsp; No,



<P>sir!&nbsp; They fit, an' fit, an' fit."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His homely face was suffused with a light of



<P>love for the army which was to him all things



<P>beautiful and powerful.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After a time he turned to the youth.&nbsp; "Where



<P>yeh hit, ol' boy?" he asked in a brotherly tone.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth felt instant panic at this question,



<P>although at first its full import was not borne in



<P>upon him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"What?" he asked.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Where yeh hit?" repeated the tattered man.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Why," began the youth, "I--I--that is--



<P>why--I--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He turned away suddenly and slid through



<P>the crowd.&nbsp; His brow was heavily flushed, and



<P>his fingers were picking nervously at one of his



<P>buttons.&nbsp; He bent his head and fastened his eyes



<P>studiously upon the button as if it were a little



<P>problem.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The tattered man looked after him in astonishment.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XX.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>WHEN the two youths turned with the flag



<P>they saw that much of the regiment had crum-



<P>bled away, and the dejected remnant was coming



<P>slowly back.&nbsp; The men, having hurled themselves



<P>in projectile fashion, had presently expended their



<P>forces.&nbsp; They slowly retreated, with their faces



<P>still toward the spluttering woods, and their hot



<P>rifles still replying to the din.&nbsp; Several officers



<P>were giving orders, their voices keyed to screams.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Where in hell yeh goin'?" the lieutenant was



<P>asking in a sarcastic howl.&nbsp; And a red-bearded



<P>officer, whose voice of triple brass could plainly



<P>be heard, was commanding: "Shoot into 'em!



<P>Shoot into 'em, Gawd damn their souls!"&nbsp; There



<P>was a melee of screeches, in which the men were



<P>ordered to do conflicting and impossible things.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth and his friend had a small scuffle



<P>over the flag.&nbsp; "Give it t' me!"&nbsp; "No, let me



<P>keep it!"&nbsp; Each felt satisfied with the other's pos-



<P>session of it, but each felt bound to declare, by

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>189



<P>an offer to carry the emblem, his willingness to



<P>further risk himself.&nbsp; The youth roughly pushed



<P>his friend away.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The regiment fell back to the stolid trees.



<P>There it halted for a moment to blaze at some



<P>dark forms that had begun to steal upon its track.



<P>Presently it resumed its march again, curving



<P>among the tree trunks.&nbsp; By the time the depleted



<P>regiment had again reached the first open space



<P>they were receiving a fast and merciless fire.



<P>There seemed to be mobs all about them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The greater part of the men, discouraged,



<P>their spirits worn by the turmoil, acted as if



<P>stunned.&nbsp; They accepted the pelting of the bul-



<P>lets with bowed and weary heads.&nbsp; It was of no



<P>purpose to strive against walls.&nbsp; It was of no use



<P>to batter themselves against granite.&nbsp; And from



<P>this consciousness that they had attempted to



<P>conquer an unconquerable thing there seemed



<P>to arise a feeling that they had been betrayed.



<P>They glowered with bent brows, but danger-



<P>ously, upon some of the officers, more particu-



<P>larly upon the red-bearded one with the voice of



<P>triple brass.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>However, the rear of the regiment was fringed



<P>with men, who continued to shoot irritably at the



<P>advancing foes.&nbsp; They seemed resolved to make



<P>every trouble.&nbsp; The youthful lieutenant was per-



<P>haps the last man in the disordered mass.&nbsp; His



<P>forgotten back was toward the enemy.&nbsp; He had



<P>been shot in the arm.&nbsp; It hung straight and rigid.



<P>Occasionally he would cease to remember it, and



<P>be about to emphasize an oath with a sweeping



<P>gesture.&nbsp; The multiplied pain caused him to



<P>swear with incredible power.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth went along with slipping, uncertain



<P>feet.&nbsp; He kept watchful eyes rearward.&nbsp; A scowl



<P>of mortification and rage was upon his face.&nbsp; He



<P>had thought of a fine revenge upon the officer



<P>who had referred to him and his fellows as mule



<P>drivers.&nbsp; But he saw that it could not come to



<P>pass.&nbsp; His dreams had collapsed when the mule



<P>drivers, dwindling rapidly, had wavered and hes-



<P>itated on the little clearing, and then had recoiled.



<P>And now the retreat of the mule drivers was a



<P>march of shame to him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A dagger-pointed gaze from without his black-



<P>ened face was held toward the enemy, but his



<P>greater hatred was riveted upon the man, who,



<P>not knowing him, had called him a mule driver.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When he knew that he and his comrades had



<P>failed to do anything in successful ways that might



<P>bring the little pangs of a kind of remorse upon



<P>the officer, the youth allowed the rage of the baf-



<P>fled to possess him.&nbsp; This cold officer upon a



<P>monument, who dropped epithets unconcernedly



<P>down, would be finer as a dead man, he thought.



<P>So grievous did he think it that he could



<P>never possess the secret right to taunt truly in



<P>answer.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He had pictured red letters of curious revenge.



<P>"We ARE mule drivers, are we?"&nbsp; And now he



<P>was compelled to throw them away.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He presently wrapped his heart in the cloak



<P>of his pride and kept the flag erect.&nbsp; He ha-



<P>rangued his fellows, pushing against their chests



<P>with his free hand.&nbsp; To those he knew well he



<P>made frantic appeals, beseeching them by name.



<P>Between him and the lieutenant, scolding and



<P>near to losing his mind with rage, there was felt a



<P>subtle fellowship and equality.&nbsp; They supported



<P>each other in all manner of hoarse, howling pro-



<P>tests.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But the regiment was a machine run down.



<P>The two men babbled at a forceless thing.&nbsp; The



<P>soldiers who had heart to go slowly were con-



<P>tinually shaken in their resolves by a knowledge



<P>that comrades were slipping with speed back to



<P>the lines.&nbsp; It was difficult to think of reputation



<P>when others were thinking of skins.&nbsp; Wounded



<P>men were left crying on this black journey.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The smoke fringes and flames blustered al-



<P>ways.&nbsp; The youth, peering once through a sud-



<P>den rift in a cloud, saw a brown mass of troops,



<P>interwoven and magnified until they appeared to



<P>be thousands.&nbsp; A fierce-hued flag flashed before



<P>his vision.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Immediately, as if the uplifting of the smoke



<P>had been prearranged, the discovered troops



<P>burst into a rasping yell, and a hundred flames



<P>jetted toward the retreating band.&nbsp; A rolling



<P>gray cloud again interposed as the regiment dog-



<P>gedly replied.&nbsp; The youth had to depend again



<P>upon his misused ears, which were trembling



<P>and buzzing from the melee of musketry and yells.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The way seemed eternal.&nbsp; In the clouded haze



<P>men became panicstricken with the thought that



<P>the regiment had lost its path, and was proceed-



<P>ing in a perilous direction.&nbsp; Once the men who



<P>headed the wild procession turned and came push-



<P>ing back against their comrades, screaming that



<P>they were being fired upon from points which



<P>they had considered to be toward their own lines.



<P>At this cry a hysterical fear and dismay beset the



<P>troops.&nbsp; A soldier, who heretofore had been am-



<P>bitious to make the regiment into a wise little



<P>band that would proceed calmly amid the huge-



<P>appearing difficulties, suddenly sank down and



<P>buried his face in his arms with an air of bowing



<P>to a doom.&nbsp; From another a shrill lamentation



<P>rang out filled with profane allusions to a general.



<P>Men ran hither and thither, seeking with their



<P>eyes roads of escape.&nbsp; With serene regularity, as



<P>if controlled by a schedule, bullets buffed into



<P>men.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth walked stolidly into the midst of



<P>the mob, and with his flag in his hands took a



<P>stand as if he expected an attempt to push him to



<P>the ground.&nbsp; He unconsciously assumed the atti-



<P>tude of the color bearer in the fight of the pre-



<P>ceding day.&nbsp; He passed over his brow a hand



<P>that trembled.&nbsp; His breath did not come freely.



<P>He was choking during this small wait for the



<P>crisis.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His friend came to him.&nbsp; "Well, Henry, I



<P>guess this is good-by--John."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh, shut up, you damned fool!" replied the



<P>youth, and he would not look at the other.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The officers labored like politicians to beat



<P>the mass into a proper circle to face the men-



<P>aces.&nbsp; The ground was uneven and torn.&nbsp; The



<P>men curled into depressions and fitted them-



<P>selves snugly behind whatever would frustrate



<P>a bullet.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth noted with vague surprise that the



<P>lieutenant was standing mutely with his legs far



<P>apart and his sword held in the manner of a cane.



<P>The youth wondered what had happened to his



<P>vocal organs that he no more cursed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was something curious in this little in-



<P>tent pause of the lieutenant.&nbsp; He was like a babe



<P>which, having wept its fill, raises its eyes and



<P>fixes upon a distant toy.&nbsp; He was engrossed in



<P>this contemplation, and the soft under lip quivered



<P>from self-whispered words.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Some lazy and ignorant smoke curled slowly.



<P>The men, hiding from the bullets, waited anx-



<P>iously for it to lift and disclose the plight of the



<P>regiment.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The silent ranks were suddenly thrilled by the



<P>eager voice of the youthful lieutenant bawling



<P>out: "Here they come!&nbsp; Right onto us,



<P>b'Gawd!"&nbsp; His further words were lost in a roar



<P>of wicked thunder from the men's rifles.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth's eyes had instantly turned in the



<P>direction indicated by the awakened and agitated



<P>lieutenant, and he had seen the haze of treachery



<P>disclosing a body of soldiers of the enemy.&nbsp; They



<P>were so near that he could see their features.



<P>There was a recognition as he looked at the types



<P>of faces.&nbsp; Also he perceived with dim amazement



<P>that their uniforms were rather gay in effect,



<P>being light gray, accented with a brilliant-hued



<P>facing.&nbsp; Too, the clothes seemed new.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>These troops had apparently been going for-



<P>ward with caution, their rifles held in readiness,



<P>when the youthful lieutenant had discovered



<P>them and their movement had been interrupted



<P>by the volley from the blue regiment.&nbsp; From the



<P>moment's glimpse, it was derived that they had



<P>been unaware of the proximity of their dark-



<P>suited foes or had mistaken the direction.&nbsp; Al-



<P>most instantly they were shut utterly from the



<P>youth's sight by the smoke from the energetic



<P>rifles of his companions.&nbsp; He strained his vision



<P>to learn the accomplishment of the volley, but the



<P>smoke hung before him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The two bodies of troops exchanged blows in



<P>the manner of a pair of boxers.&nbsp; The fast angry



<P>firings went back and forth.&nbsp; The men in blue



<P>were intent with the despair of their circum-



<P>stances and they seized upon the revenge to be



<P>had at close range.&nbsp; Their thunder swelled loud



<P>and valiant.&nbsp; Their curving front bristled with



<P>flashes and the place resounded with the clangor



<P>of their ramrods.&nbsp; The youth ducked and dodged



<P>for a time and achieved a few unsatisfactory



<P>views of the enemy.&nbsp; There appeared to be many



<P>of them and they were replying swiftly.&nbsp; They



<P>seemed moving toward the blue regiment, step



<P>by step.&nbsp; He seated himself gloomily on the



<P>ground with his flag between his knees.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As he noted the vicious, wolflike temper of



<P>his comrades he had a sweet thought that if the



<P>enemy was about to swallow the regimental



<P>broom as a large prisoner, it could at least have



<P>the consolation of going down with bristles for-



<P>ward.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But the blows of the antagonist began to



<P>grow more weak.&nbsp; Fewer bullets ripped the air,



<P>and finally, when the men slackened to learn of



<P>the fight, they could see only dark, floating



<P>smoke.&nbsp; The regiment lay still and gazed.&nbsp; Pres-



<P>ently some chance whim came to the pestering



<P>blur, and it began to coil heavily away.&nbsp; The men



<P>saw a ground vacant of fighters.&nbsp; It would have



<P>been an empty stage if it were not for a few



<P>corpses that lay thrown and twisted into fantastic



<P>shapes upon the sward.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At sight of this tableau, many of the men in



<P>blue sprang from behind their covers and made



<P>an ungainly dance of joy.&nbsp; Their eyes burned



<P>and a hoarse cheer of elation broke from their



<P>dry lips.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It had begun to seem to them that events were



<P>trying to prove that they were impotent.&nbsp; These



<P>little battles had evidently endeavored to demon-



<P>strate that the men could not fight well.&nbsp; When



<P>on the verge of submission to these opinions, the



<P>small duel had showed them that the propor-



<P>tions were not impossible, and by it they had



<P>revenged themselves upon their misgivings and



<P>upon the foe.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The impetus of enthusiasm was theirs again.



<P>They gazed about them with looks of uplifted



<P>pride, feeling new trust in the grim, always



<P>confident weapons in their hands.&nbsp; And they



<P>were men.



<P>

   <TITLE>chapter21</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XXI.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>PRESENTLY they knew that no firing threat-



<P>ened them.&nbsp; All ways seemed once more opened



<P>to them.&nbsp; The dusty blue lines of their friends



<P>were disclosed a short distance away.&nbsp; In the



<P>distance there were many colossal noises, but in



<P>all this part of the field there was a sudden



<P>stillness.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They perceived that they were free.&nbsp; The



<P>depleted band drew a long breath of relief



<P>and gathered itself into a bunch to complete



<P>its trip.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In this last length of journey the men began



<P>to show strange emotions.&nbsp; They hurried with



<P>nervous fear.&nbsp; Some who had been dark and un-



<P>faltering in the grimmest moments now could not



<P>conceal an anxiety that made them frantic.&nbsp; It



<P>was perhaps that they dreaded to be killed in



<P>insignificant ways after the times for proper



<P>military deaths had passed.&nbsp; Or, perhaps, they



<P>thought it would be too ironical to get killed at

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>199



<P>the portals of safety.&nbsp; With backward looks of



<P>perturbation, they hastened.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As they approached their own lines there was



<P>some sarcasm exhibited on the part of a gaunt



<P>and bronzed regiment that lay resting in the



<P>shade of trees.&nbsp; Questions were wafted to them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Where th' hell yeh been?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"What yeh comin' back fer?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Why didn't yeh stay there?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Was it warm out there, sonny?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Goin' home now, boys?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>One shouted in taunting mimicry: "Oh,



<P>mother, come quick an' look at th' sojers!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was no reply from the bruised and bat-



<P>tered regiment, save that one man made broad-



<P>cast challenges to fist fights and the red-bearded



<P>officer walked rather near and glared in great



<P>swashbuckler style at a tall captain in the other



<P>regiment.&nbsp; But the lieutenant suppressed the



<P>man who wished to fist fight, and the tall cap-



<P>tain, flushing at the little fanfare of the red-



<P>bearded one, was obliged to look intently at some



<P>trees.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth's tender flesh was deeply stung by



<P>these remarks.&nbsp; From under his creased brows



<P>he glowered with hate at the mockers.&nbsp; He



<P>meditated upon a few revenges.&nbsp; Still, many in



<P>the regiment hung their heads in criminal fashion,



<P>so that it came to pass that the men trudged with



<P>sudden heaviness, as if they bore upon their



<P>bended shoulders the coffin of their honor.&nbsp; And



<P>the youthful lieutenant, recollecting himself, be-



<P>gan to mutter softly in black curses.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They turned when they arrived at their old



<P>position to regard the ground over which they



<P>had charged.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth in this contemplation was smitten



<P>with a large astonishment.&nbsp; He discovered that



<P>the distances, as compared with the brilliant



<P>measurings of his mind, were trivial and ridicu-



<P>lous.&nbsp; The stolid trees, where much had taken



<P>place, seemed incredibly near.&nbsp; The time, too,



<P>now that he reflected, he saw to have been short.



<P>He wondered at the number of emotions and



<P>events that had been crowded into such little



<P>spaces.&nbsp; Elfin thoughts must have exaggerated



<P>and enlarged everything, he said.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It seemed, then, that there was bitter justice



<P>in the speeches of the gaunt and bronzed vet-



<P>erans.&nbsp; He veiled a glance of disdain at his fel-



<P>lows who strewed the ground, choking with dust,



<P>red from perspiration, misty-eyed, disheveled.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They were gulping at their canteens, fierce to



<P>wring every mite of water from them, and they



<P>polished at their swollen and watery features



<P>with coat sleeves and bunches of grass.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>However, to the youth there was a consider-



<P>able joy in musing upon his performances during



<P>the charge.&nbsp; He had had very little time pre-



<P>viously in which to appreciate himself, so that



<P>there was now much satisfaction in quietly think-



<P>ing of his actions.&nbsp; He recalled bits of color that



<P>in the flurry had stamped themselves unawares



<P>upon his engaged senses.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As the regiment lay heaving from its hot exer-



<P>tions the officer who had named them as mule



<P>drivers came galloping along the line.&nbsp; He had



<P>lost his cap.&nbsp; His tousled hair streamed wildly,



<P>and his face was dark with vexation and wrath.



<P>His temper was displayed with more clearness



<P>by the way in which he managed his horse.&nbsp; He



<P>jerked and wrenched savagely at his bridle, stop-



<P>ping the hard-breathing animal with a furious



<P>pull near the colonel of the regiment.&nbsp; He im-



<P>mediately exploded in reproaches which came



<P>unbidden to the ears of the men.&nbsp; They were



<P>suddenly alert, being always curious about black



<P>words between officers.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh, thunder, MacChesnay, what an awful



<P>bull you made of this thing!" began the officer.



<P>He attempted low tones, but his indignation



<P>caused certain of the men to learn the sense of



<P>his words.&nbsp; "What an awful mess you made!



<P>Good Lord, man, you stopped about a hun-



<P>dred feet this side of a very pretty success!&nbsp; If



<P>your men had gone a hundred feet farther you



<P>would have made a great charge, but as it is



<P>--what a lot of mud diggers you've got any-



<P>way!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The men, listening with bated breath, now



<P>turned their curious eyes upon the colonel.



<P>They had a ragamuffin interest in this affair.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The colonel was seen to straighten his form



<P>and put one hand forth in oratorical fashion.



<P>He wore an injured air; it was as if a deacon



<P>had been accused of stealing.&nbsp; The men were



<P>wiggling in an ecstasy of excitement.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But of a sudden the colonel's manner changed



<P>from that of a deacon to that of a Frenchman.



<P>He shrugged his shoulders.&nbsp; "Oh, well, general,



<P>we went as far as we could," he said calmly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"As far as you could?&nbsp; Did you, b'Gawd?"



<P>snorted the other.&nbsp; "Well, that wasn't very far,



<P>was it?" he added, with a glance of cold con-



<P>tempt into the other's eyes.&nbsp; "Not very far, I



<P>think.&nbsp; You were intended to make a diversion



<P>in favor of Whiterside.&nbsp; How well you succeeded



<P>your own ears can now tell you."&nbsp; He wheeled



<P>his horse and rode stiffly away.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The colonel, bidden to hear the jarring noises



<P>of an engagement in the woods to the left, broke



<P>out in vague damnations.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The lieutenant, who had listened with an air



<P>of impotent rage to the interview, spoke suddenly



<P>in firm and undaunted tones.&nbsp; "I don't care what



<P>a man is--whether he is a general or what--if



<P>he says th' boys didn't put up a good fight out



<P>there he's a damned fool."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Lieutenant," began the colonel, severely,



<P>"this is my own affair, and I'll trouble you--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The lieutenant made an obedient gesture.



<P>"All right, colonel, all right," he said.&nbsp; He sat



<P>down with an air of being content with him-



<P>self.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The news that the regiment had been re-



<P>proached went along the line.&nbsp; For a time the



<P>men were bewildered by it.&nbsp; "Good thunder!"



<P>they ejaculated, staring at the vanishing form of



<P>the general.&nbsp; They conceived it to be a huge



<P>mistake.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Presently, however, they began to believe that



<P>in truth their efforts had been called light.&nbsp; The



<P>youth could see this conviction weigh upon the



<P>entire regiment until the men were like cuffed



<P>and cursed animals, but withal rebellious.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The friend, with a grievance in his eye,



<P>went to the youth.&nbsp; "I wonder what he does



<P>want," he said.&nbsp; "He must think we went out



<P>there an' played marbles!&nbsp; I never see sech a



<P>man!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth developed a tranquil philosophy



<P>for these moments of irritation.&nbsp; "Oh, well," he



<P>rejoined, "he probably didn't see nothing of it at



<P>all and got mad as blazes, and concluded we were



<P>a lot of sheep, just because we didn't do what he



<P>wanted done.&nbsp; It's a pity old Grandpa Hender-



<P>son got killed yestirday--he'd have known that



<P>we did our best and fought good.&nbsp; It's just our



<P>awful luck, that's what."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I should say so," replied the friend.&nbsp; He



<P>seemed to be deeply wounded at an injustice.



<P>"I should say we did have awful luck!&nbsp; There's



<P>no fun in fightin' fer people when everything



<P>yeh do--no matter what--ain't done right.&nbsp; I



<P>have a notion t' stay behind next time an' let



<P>'em take their ol' charge an' go t' th' devil



<P>with it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth spoke soothingly to his comrade.



<P>"Well, we both did good.&nbsp; I'd like to see the



<P>fool what'd say we both didn't do as good as we



<P>could!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Of course we did," declared the friend



<P>stoutly.&nbsp; "An' I'd break th' feller's neck if he was



<P>as big as a church.&nbsp; But we're all right, anyhow,



<P>for I heard one feller say that we two fit th' best



<P>in th' reg'ment, an' they had a great argument



<P>'bout it.&nbsp; Another feller, 'a course, he had t' up



<P>an' say it was a lie--he seen all what was goin'



<P>on an' he never seen us from th' beginnin' t' th'



<P>end.&nbsp; An' a lot more struck in an' ses it wasn't



<P>a lie--we did fight like thunder, an' they give



<P>us quite a send-off.&nbsp; But this is what I can't



<P>stand--these everlastin' ol' soldiers, titterin' an'



<P>laughin', an' then that general, he's crazy."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth exclaimed with sudden exaspera-



<P>tion: "He's a lunkhead!&nbsp; He makes me mad.



<P>I wish he'd come along next time.&nbsp; We'd show



<P>'im what--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He ceased because several men had come



<P>hurrying up.&nbsp; Their faces expressed a bringing



<P>of great news.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"O Flem, yeh jest oughta heard!" cried one,



<P>eagerly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Heard what?" said the youth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yeh jest oughta heard!" repeated the other,



<P>and he arranged himself to tell his tidings.&nbsp; The



<P>others made an excited circle.&nbsp; "Well, sir, th'



<P>colonel met your lieutenant right by us--it was



<P>damnedest thing I ever heard--an' he ses: 'Ahem!



<P>ahem!' he ses.&nbsp; 'Mr. Hasbrouck!' he ses, 'by



<P>th' way, who was that lad what carried th' flag?'



<P>he ses.&nbsp; There, Flemin', what d' yeh think 'a



<P>that?&nbsp; 'Who was th' lad what carried th' flag?'



<P>he ses, an' th' lieutenant, he speaks up right



<P>away: 'That's Flemin', an' he's a jimhickey,' he



<P>ses, right away.&nbsp; What?&nbsp; I say he did.&nbsp; 'A jim-



<P>hickey,' he ses--those 'r his words.&nbsp; He did, too.



<P>I say he did.&nbsp; If you kin tell this story better



<P>than I kin, go ahead an' tell it.&nbsp; Well, then, keep



<P>yer mouth shet.&nbsp; Th' lieutenant, he ses: 'He's a



<P>jimhickey,' an' th' colonel, he ses: 'Ahem! ahem!



<P>he is, indeed, a very good man t' have, ahem!&nbsp; He



<P>kep' th' flag 'way t' th' front.&nbsp; I saw 'im.&nbsp; He's a



<P>good un,' ses th' colonel.&nbsp; 'You bet,' ses th' lieu-



<P>tenant, 'he an' a feller named Wilson was at th'



<P>head 'a th' charge, an' howlin' like Indians all th'



<P>time,' he ses.&nbsp; 'Head 'a th' charge all th' time,'



<P>he ses.&nbsp; 'A feller named Wilson,' he ses.&nbsp; There,



<P>Wilson, m'boy, put that in a letter an' send it



<P>hum t' yer mother, hay?&nbsp; 'A feller named Wil-



<P>son,' he ses.&nbsp; An' th' colonel, he ses: 'Were they,



<P>indeed?&nbsp; Ahem! ahem!&nbsp; My sakes!' he ses.&nbsp; 'At



<P>th' head 'a th' reg'ment?' he ses.&nbsp; 'They were,'



<P>ses th' lieutenant.&nbsp; 'My sakes!' ses th' colonel.



<P>He ses: 'Well, well, well,' he ses, 'those two



<P>babies?'&nbsp; 'They were,' ses th' lieutenant.



<P>'Well, well,' ses th' colonel, 'they deserve t' be



<P>major generals,' he ses.&nbsp; 'They deserve t' be



<P>major-generals.'

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth and his friend had said: "Huh!"



<P>"Yer lyin', Thompson."&nbsp; "Oh, go t' blazes!"



<P>"He never sed it."&nbsp; "Oh, what a lie!"&nbsp; "Huh!"



<P>But despite these youthful scoffings and embar-



<P>rassments, they knew that their faces were deeply



<P>flushing from thrills of pleasure.&nbsp; They ex-



<P>changed a secret glance of joy and congratula-



<P>tion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They speedily forgot many things.&nbsp; The past



<P>held no pictures of error and disappointment.



<P>They were very happy, and their hearts swelled



<P>with grateful affection for the colonel and the



<P>youthful lieutenant.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">
