<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER IX.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>THE youth fell back in the procession until



<P>the tattered soldier was not in sight.&nbsp; Then he



<P>started to walk on with the others.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But he was amid wounds.&nbsp; The mob of men



<P>was bleeding.&nbsp; Because of the tattered soldier's



<P>question he now felt that his shame could be



<P>viewed.&nbsp; He was continually casting sidelong



<P>glances to see if the men were contemplating the



<P>letters of guilt he felt burned into his brow.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At times he regarded the wounded soldiers



<P>in an envious way.&nbsp; He conceived persons with



<P>torn bodies to be peculiarly happy.&nbsp; He wished



<P>that he, too, had a wound, a red badge of cour-



<P>age.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The spectral soldier was at his side like a



<P>stalking reproach.&nbsp; The man's eyes were still



<P>fixed in a stare into the unknown.&nbsp; His gray,



<P>appalling face had attracted attention in the



<P>crowd, and men, slowing to his dreary pace, were



<P>walking with him.&nbsp; They were discussing his



<P>plight, questioning him and giving him advice.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>91



<P>In a dogged way he repelled them, signing to them



<P>to go on and leave him alone.&nbsp; The shadows of



<P>his face were deepening and his tight lips seemed



<P>holding in check the moan of great despair.



<P>There could be seen a certain stiffness in the



<P>movements of his body, as if he were taking



<P>infinite care not to arouse the passion of his



<P>wounds.&nbsp; As he went on, he seemed always look-



<P>ing for a place, like one who goes to choose a



<P>grave.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Something in the gesture of the man as he



<P>waved the bloody and pitying soldiers away



<P>made the youth start as if bitten.&nbsp; He yelled in



<P>horror.&nbsp; Tottering forward he laid a quivering



<P>hand upon the man's arm.&nbsp; As the latter slowly



<P>turned his waxlike features toward him, the



<P>youth screamed:

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Gawd!&nbsp; Jim Conklin!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The tall soldier made a little commonplace



<P>smile.&nbsp; "Hello, Henry," he said.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth swayed on his legs and glared



<P>strangely.&nbsp; He stuttered and stammered.&nbsp; "Oh,



<P>Jim--oh, Jim--oh, Jim--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The tall soldier held out his gory hand.&nbsp; There



<P>was a curious red and black combination of new



<P>blood and old blood upon it.&nbsp; "Where yeh been,



<P>Henry?" he asked.&nbsp; He continued in a monoto-



<P>nous voice, "I thought mebbe yeh got keeled



<P>over.&nbsp; There 's been thunder t' pay t'-day.&nbsp; I was



<P>worryin' about it a good deal."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth still lamented.&nbsp; "Oh, Jim--oh, Jim



<P>--oh, Jim--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yeh know," said the tall soldier, "I was out



<P>there."&nbsp; He made a careful gesture.&nbsp; "An',



<P>Lord, what a circus!&nbsp; An', b'jiminey, I got shot--



<P>I got shot.&nbsp; Yes, b'jiminey, I got shot."&nbsp; He



<P>reiterated this fact in a bewildered way, as if he



<P>did not know how it came about.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth put forth anxious arms to assist



<P>him, but the tall soldier went firmly on as if pro-



<P>pelled.&nbsp; Since the youth's arrival as a guardian



<P>for his friend, the other wounded men had ceased



<P>to display much interest.&nbsp; They occupied them-



<P>selves again in dragging their own tragedies



<P>toward the rear.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Suddenly, as the two friends marched on, the



<P>tall soldier seemed to be overcome by a terror.



<P>His face turned to a semblance of gray paste.



<P>He clutched the youth's arm and looked all about



<P>him, as if dreading to be overheard.&nbsp; Then he



<P>began to speak in a shaking whisper:

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I tell yeh what I'm 'fraid of, Henry--I 'll tell



<P>yeh what I 'm 'fraid of.&nbsp; I 'm 'fraid I 'll fall down



<P>--an' then yeh know--them damned artillery



<P>wagons--they like as not 'll run over me.&nbsp; That 's



<P>what I 'm 'fraid of--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth cried out to him hysterically: "I 'll



<P>take care of yeh, Jim!&nbsp; I'll take care of yeh!&nbsp; I



<P>swear t' Gawd I will!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Sure--will yeh, Henry?" the tall soldier



<P>beseeched.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes--yes--I tell yeh--I'll take care of yeh,



<P>Jim!" protested the youth.&nbsp; He could not speak



<P>accurately because of the gulpings in his throat.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But the tall soldier continued to beg in a



<P>lowly way.&nbsp; He now hung babelike to the



<P>youth's arm.&nbsp; His eyes rolled in the wildness of



<P>his terror.&nbsp; "I was allus a good friend t' yeh,



<P>wa'n't I, Henry?&nbsp; I 've allus been a pretty good



<P>feller, ain't I?&nbsp; An' it ain't much t' ask, is it?&nbsp; Jest



<P>t' pull me along outer th' road?&nbsp; I 'd do it fer you,



<P>Wouldn't I, Henry?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He paused in piteous anxiety to await his



<P>friend's reply.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth had reached an anguish where the



<P>sobs scorched him.&nbsp; He strove to express his



<P>loyalty, but he could only make fantastic gestures.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>However, the tall soldier seemed suddenly to



<P>forget all those fears.&nbsp; He became again the



<P>grim, stalking specter of a soldier.&nbsp; He went



<P>stonily forward.&nbsp; The youth wished his friend to



<P>lean upon him, but the other always shook his



<P>head and strangely protested.&nbsp; "No--no--no--



<P>leave me be--leave me be--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His look was fixed again upon the unknown.



<P>He moved with mysterious purpose, and all of



<P>the youth's offers he brushed aside.&nbsp; "No--no--



<P>leave me be--leave me be--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth had to follow.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Presently the latter heard a voice talking



<P>softly near his shoulders.&nbsp; Turning he saw that it



<P>belonged to the tattered soldier.&nbsp; "Ye 'd better



<P>take 'im outa th' road, pardner.&nbsp; There 's a batt'ry



<P>comin' helitywhoop down th' road an' he 'll git



<P>runned over.&nbsp; He 's a goner anyhow in about five



<P>minutes--yeh kin see that.&nbsp; Ye 'd better take 'im



<P>outa th' road.&nbsp; Where th' blazes does he git his



<P>stren'th from?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Lord knows!" cried the youth.&nbsp; He was



<P>shaking his hands helplessly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He ran forward presently and grasped the



<P>tall soldier by the arm.&nbsp; "Jim!&nbsp; Jim!" he coaxed,



<P>"come with me."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The tall soldier weakly tried to wrench himself



<P>free.&nbsp; "Huh," he said vacantly.&nbsp; He stared at the



<P>youth for a moment.&nbsp; At last he spoke as if dimly



<P>comprehending.&nbsp; "Oh!&nbsp; Inteh th' fields?&nbsp; Oh!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He started blindly through the grass.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth turned once to look at the lashing



<P>riders and jouncing guns of the battery.&nbsp; He was



<P>startled from this view by a shrill outcry from



<P>the tattered man.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Gawd!&nbsp; He's runnin'!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Turning his head swiftly, the youth saw his



<P>friend running in a staggering and stumbling



<P>way toward a little clump of bushes.&nbsp; His heart



<P>seemed to wrench itself almost free from his



<P>body at this sight.&nbsp; He made a noise of pain.



<P>He and the tattered man began a pursuit.&nbsp; There



<P>was a singular race.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When he overtook the tall soldier he began



<P>to plead with all the words he could find.&nbsp; "Jim



<P>--Jim--what are you doing--what makes you do



<P>this way--you 'll hurt yerself."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The same purpose was in the tall soldier's face.



<P>He protested in a dulled way, keeping his eyes



<P>fastened on the mystic place of his intentions.



<P>"No--no--don't tech me--leave me be--leave



<P>me be--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth, aghast and filled with wonder at the



<P>tall soldier, began quaveringly to question him.



<P>"Where yeh goin', Jim?&nbsp; What you thinking



<P>about?&nbsp; Where you going?&nbsp; Tell me, won't you,



<P>Jim?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The tall soldier faced about as upon relentless



<P>pursuers.&nbsp; In his eyes there was a great appeal.



<P>"Leave me be, can't yeh?&nbsp; Leave me be fer a



<P>minnit."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth recoiled.&nbsp; "Why, Jim," he said, in



<P>a dazed way, "what's the matter with you?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The tall soldier turned and, lurching danger-



<P>ously, went on.&nbsp; The youth and the tattered



<P>soldier followed, sneaking as if whipped, feeling



<P>unable to face the stricken man if he should again



<P>confront them.&nbsp; They began to have thoughts of



<P>a solemn ceremony.&nbsp; There was something rite-



<P>like in these movements of the doomed soldier.



<P>And there was a resemblance in him to a devotee



<P>of a mad religion, blood-sucking, muscle-wrench-



<P>ing, bone-crushing.&nbsp; They were awed and afraid.



<P>They hung back lest he have at command a



<P>dreadful weapon.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At last, they saw him stop and stand motion-



<P>less.&nbsp; Hastening up, they perceived that his face



<P>wore an expression telling that he had at last



<P>found the place for which he had struggled.&nbsp; His



<P>spare figure was erect; his bloody hands were



<P>quietly at his side.&nbsp; He was waiting with patience



<P>for something that he had come to meet.&nbsp; He was



<P>at the rendezvous.&nbsp; They paused and stood, ex-



<P>pectant.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was a silence.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Finally, the chest of the doomed soldier began



<P>to heave with a strained motion.&nbsp; It increased in



<P>violence until it was as if an animal was within



<P>and was kicking and tumbling furiously to be



<P>free.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>This spectacle of gradual strangulation made



<P>the youth writhe, and once as his friend rolled his



<P>eyes, he saw something in them that made him



<P>sink wailing to the ground.&nbsp; He raised his voice



<P>in a last supreme call.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Jim--Jim--Jim--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The tall soldier opened his lips and spoke.



<P>He made a gesture.&nbsp; "Leave me be--don't tech



<P>me--leave me be--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was another silence while he waited.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Suddenly, his form stiffened and straightened.



<P>Then it was shaken by a prolonged ague.&nbsp; He



<P>stared into space.&nbsp; To the two watchers there



<P>was a curious and profound dignity in the firm



<P>lines of his awful face.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He was invaded by a creeping strangeness



<P>that slowly enveloped him.&nbsp; For a moment the



<P>tremor of his legs caused him to dance a sort of



<P>hideous hornpipe.&nbsp; His arms beat wildly about



<P>his head in expression of implike enthusiasm.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His tall figure stretched itself to its full height.



<P>There was a slight rending sound.&nbsp; Then it began



<P>to swing forward, slow and straight, in the man-



<P>ner of a falling tree.&nbsp; A swift muscular contortion



<P>made the left shoulder strike the ground first.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The body seemed to bounce a little way from



<P>the earth.&nbsp; "God!" said the tattered soldier.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth had watched, spellbound, this



<P>ceremony at the place of meeting.&nbsp; His face



<P>had been twisted into an expression of every



<P>agony he had imagined for his friend.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He now sprang to his feet and, going closer,



<P>gazed upon the pastelike face.&nbsp; The mouth was



<P>open and the teeth showed in a laugh.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As the flap of the blue jacket fell away from



<P>the body, he could see that the side looked as if it



<P>had been chewed by wolves.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth turned, with sudden, livid rage,



<P>toward the battlefield.&nbsp; He shook his fist.&nbsp; He



<P>seemed about to deliver a philippic.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Hell--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The red sun was pasted in the sky like a wafer.



<P>

<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XXIII.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>THE colonel came running along back of the



<P>line.&nbsp; There were other officers following him.



<P>"We must charge'm!" they shouted.&nbsp; "We must



<P>charge'm!" they cried with resentful voices, as



<P>if anticipating a rebellion against this plan by the



<P>men.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth, upon hearing the shouts, began to



<P>study the distance between him and the enemy.



<P>He made vague calculations.&nbsp; He saw that to be



<P>firm soldiers they must go forward.&nbsp; It would be



<P>death to stay in the present place, and with all



<P>the circumstances to go backward would exalt



<P>too many others.&nbsp; Their hope was to push the



<P>galling foes away from the fence.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He expected that his companions, weary and



<P>stiffened, would have to be driven to this assault,



<P>but as he turned toward them he perceived with



<P>a certain surprise that they were giving quick



<P>and unqualified expressions of assent.&nbsp; There was



<P>an ominous, clanging overture to the charge

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>217



<P>when the shafts of the bayonets rattled upon the



<P>rifle barrels.&nbsp; At the yelled words of command



<P>the soldiers sprang forward in eager leaps.



<P>There was new and unexpected force in the



<P>movement of the regiment.&nbsp; A knowledge of its



<P>faded and jaded condition made the charge ap-



<P>pear like a paroxysm, a display of the strength



<P>that comes before a final feebleness.&nbsp; The men



<P>scampered in insane fever of haste, racing as if to



<P>achieve a sudden success before an exhilarating



<P>fluid should leave them.&nbsp; It was a blind and de-



<P>spairing rush by the collection of men in dusty



<P>and tattered blue, over a green sward and under



<P>a sapphire sky, toward a fence, dimly outlined in



<P>smoke, from behind which spluttered the fierce



<P>rifles of enemies.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth kept the bright colors to the front.



<P>He was waving his free arm in furious circles,



<P>the while shrieking mad calls and appeals, urging



<P>on those that did not need to be urged, for it



<P>seemed that the mob of blue men hurling them-



<P>selves on the dangerous group of rifles were



<P>again grown suddenly wild with an enthusiasm of



<P>unselfishness.&nbsp; From the many firings starting



<P>toward them, it looked as if they would merely



<P>succeed in making a great sprinkling of corpses



<P>on the grass between their former position and



<P>the fence.&nbsp; But they were in a state of frenzy,



<P>perhaps because of forgotten vanities, and it made



<P>an exhibition of sublime recklessness.&nbsp; There was



<P>no obvious questioning, nor figurings, nor dia-



<P>grams.&nbsp; There was, apparently, no considered



<P>loopholes.&nbsp; It appeared that the swift wings of



<P>their desires would have shattered against the



<P>iron gates of the impossible.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He himself felt the daring spirit of a savage



<P>religion mad.&nbsp; He was capable of profound sacri-



<P>fices, a tremendous death.&nbsp; He had no time for



<P>dissections, but he knew that he thought of the



<P>bullets only as things that could prevent him



<P>from reaching the place of his endeavor.&nbsp; There



<P>were subtle flashings of joy within him that thus



<P>should be his mind.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He strained all his strength.&nbsp; His eyesight



<P>was shaken and dazzled by the tension of thought



<P>and muscle.&nbsp; He did not see anything excepting



<P>the mist of smoke gashed by the little knives of



<P>fire, but he knew that in it lay the aged fence of a



<P>vanished farmer protecting the snuggled bodies



<P>of the gray men.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As he ran a thought of the shock of contact



<P>gleamed in his mind.&nbsp; He expected a great con-



<P>cussion when the two bodies of troops crashed



<P>together.&nbsp; This became a part of his wild battle



<P>madness.&nbsp; He could feel the onward swing of the



<P>regiment about him and he conceived of a thun-



<P>derous, crushing blow that would prostrate the



<P>resistance and spread consternation and amaze-



<P>ment for miles.&nbsp; The flying regiment was going



<P>to have a catapultian effect.&nbsp; This dream made



<P>him run faster among his comrades, who were



<P>giving vent to hoarse and frantic cheers.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But presently he could see that many of the



<P>men in gray did not intend to abide the blow.



<P>The smoke, rolling, disclosed men who ran, their



<P>faces still turned.&nbsp; These grew to a crowd, who



<P>retired stubbornly.&nbsp; Individuals wheeled fre-



<P>quently to send a bullet at the blue wave.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But at one part of the line there was a grim



<P>and obdurate group that made no movement.



<P>They were settled firmly down behind posts and



<P>rails.&nbsp; A flag, ruffled and fierce, waved over them



<P>and their rifles dinned fiercely.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The blue whirl of men got very near, until



<P>it seemed that in truth there would be a close



<P>and frightful scuffle.&nbsp; There was an expressed



<P>disdain in the opposition of the little group,



<P>that changed the meaning of the cheers of the



<P>men in blue.&nbsp; They became yells of wrath,



<P>directed, personal.&nbsp; The cries of the two parties



<P>were now in sound an interchange of scathing



<P>insults.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They in blue showed their teeth; their eyes



<P>shone all white.&nbsp; They launched themselves as at



<P>the throats of those who stood resisting.&nbsp; The



<P>space between dwindled to an insignificant dis-



<P>tance.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth had centered the gaze of his soul



<P>upon that other flag.&nbsp; Its possession would be



<P>high pride.&nbsp; It would express bloody minglings,



<P>near blows.&nbsp; He had a gigantic hatred for those



<P>who made great difficulties and complications.



<P>They caused it to be as a craved treasure of my-



<P>thology, hung amid tasks and contrivances of



<P>danger.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He plunged like a mad horse at it.&nbsp; He was



<P>resolved it should not escape if wild blows and



<P>darings of blows could seize it.&nbsp; His own em-



<P>blem, quivering and aflare, was winging toward



<P>the other.&nbsp; It seemed there would shortly be



<P>an encounter of strange beaks and claws, as of



<P>eagles.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The swirling body of blue men came to a



<P>sudden halt at close and disastrous range and



<P>roared a swift volley.&nbsp; The group in gray was



<P>split and broken by this fire, but its riddled body



<P>still fought.&nbsp; The men in blue yelled again and



<P>rushed in upon it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth, in his leapings, saw, as through a



<P>mist, a picture of four or five men stretched upon



<P>the ground or writhing upon their knees with



<P>bowed heads as if they had been stricken by bolts



<P>from the sky.&nbsp; Tottering among them was the



<P>rival color bearer, whom the youth saw had been



<P>bitten vitally by the bullets of the last formidable



<P>volley.&nbsp; He perceived this man fighting a last



<P>struggle, the struggle of one whose legs are



<P>grasped by demons.&nbsp; It was a ghastly battle.



<P>Over his face was the bleach of death, but set



<P>upon it was the dark and hard lines of desperate



<P>purpose.&nbsp; With this terrible grin of resolution he



<P>hugged his precious flag to him and was stum-



<P>bling and staggering in his design to go the way



<P>that led to safety for it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But his wounds always made it seem that his



<P>feet were retarded, held, and he fought a grim



<P>fight, as with invisible ghouls fastened greedily



<P>upon his limbs.&nbsp; Those in advance of the scam-



<P>pering blue men, howling cheers, leaped at the



<P>fence.&nbsp; The despair of the lost was in his eyes as



<P>he glanced back at them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth's friend went over the obstruction



<P>in a tumbling heap and sprang at the flag as a



<P>panther at prey.&nbsp; He pulled at it and, wrench-



<P>ing it free, swung up its red brilliancy with a



<P>mad cry of exultation even as the color bearer,



<P>gasping, lurched over in a final throe and, stiff-



<P>ening convulsively, turned his dead face to the



<P>ground.&nbsp; There was much blood upon the grass



<P>blades.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At the place of success there began more wild



<P>clamorings of cheers.&nbsp; The men gesticulated and



<P>bellowed in an ecstasy.&nbsp; When they spoke it was



<P>as if they considered their listener to be a mile



<P>away.&nbsp; What hats and caps were left to them



<P>they often slung high in the air.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At one part of the line four men had been



<P>swooped upon, and they now sat as prisoners.



<P>Some blue men were about them in an eager and



<P>curious circle.&nbsp; The soldiers had trapped strange



<P>birds, and there was an examination.&nbsp; A flurry of



<P>fast questions was in the air.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>One of the prisoners was nursing a superficial



<P>wound in the foot.&nbsp; He cuddled it, baby-wise,



<P>but he looked up from it often to curse with an



<P>astonishing utter abandon straight at the noses of



<P>his captors.&nbsp; He consigned them to red regions;



<P>he called upon the pestilential wrath of strange



<P>gods.&nbsp; And with it all he was singularly free



<P>from recognition of the finer points of the con-



<P>duct of prisoners of war.&nbsp; It was as if a clumsy



<P>clod had trod upon his toe and he conceived it to



<P>be his privilege, his duty, to use deep, resentful



<P>oaths.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Another, who was a boy in years, took his



<P>plight with great calmness and apparent good



<P>nature.&nbsp; He conversed with the men in blue,



<P>studying their faces with his bright and keen



<P>eyes.&nbsp; They spoke of battles and conditions.



<P>There was an acute interest in all their faces dur-



<P>ing this exchange of view points.&nbsp; It seemed a



<P>great satisfaction to hear voices from where all



<P>had been darkness and speculation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The third captive sat with a morose counte-



<P>nance.&nbsp; He preserved a stoical and cold attitude.



<P>To all advances he made one reply without varia-



<P>tion, "Ah, go t' hell!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The last of the four was always silent and,



<P>for the most part, kept his face turned in un-



<P>molested directions.&nbsp; From the views the youth



<P>received he seemed to be in a state of absolute



<P>dejection.&nbsp; Shame was upon him, and with it



<P>profound regret that he was, perhaps, no more



<P>to be counted in the ranks of his fellows.&nbsp; The



<P>youth could detect no expression that would



<P>allow him to believe that the other was giving



<P>a thought to his narrowed future, the pictured



<P>dungeons, perhaps, and starvations and brutali-



<P>ties, liable to the imagination.&nbsp; All to be seen



<P>was shame for captivity and regret for the right



<P>to antagonize.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After the men had celebrated sufficiently they



<P>settled down behind the old rail fence, on the



<P>opposite side to the one from which their foes



<P>had been driven.&nbsp; A few shot perfunctorily at



<P>distant marks.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was some long grass.&nbsp; The youth



<P>nestled in it and rested, making a convenient rail



<P>support the flag.&nbsp; His friend, jubilant and glori-



<P>fied, holding his treasure with vanity, came to



<P>him there.&nbsp; They sat side by side and congratu-



<P>lated each other.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter22</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XXII.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>WHEN the woods again began to pour forth



<P>the dark-hued masses of the enemy the youth felt



<P>serene self-confidence.&nbsp; He smiled briefly when



<P>he saw men dodge and duck at the long screech-



<P>ings of shells that were thrown in giant handfuls



<P>over them.&nbsp; He stood, erect and tranquil, watch-



<P>ing the attack begin against a part of the line



<P>that made a blue curve along the side of an adja-



<P>cent hill.&nbsp; His vision being unmolested by smoke



<P>from the rifles of his companions, he had oppor-



<P>tunities to see parts of the hard fight.&nbsp; It was a



<P>relief to perceive at last from whence came some



<P>of these noises which had been roared into his



<P>ears.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Off a short way he saw two regiments fight-



<P>ing a little separate battle with two other regi-



<P>ments.&nbsp; It was in a cleared space, wearing a set-



<P>apart look.&nbsp; They were blazing as if upon a



<P>wager, giving and taking tremendous blows.



<P>The firings were incredibly fierce and rapid.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>209



<P>These intent regiments apparently were oblivious



<P>of all larger purposes of war, and were slugging



<P>each other as if at a matched game.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In another direction he saw a magnificent



<P>brigade going with the evident intention of driv-



<P>ing the enemy from a wood.&nbsp; They passed in out



<P>of sight and presently there was a most awe-in-



<P>spiring racket in the wood.&nbsp; The noise was un-



<P>speakable.&nbsp; Having stirred this prodigious up-



<P>roar, and, apparently, finding it too prodigious,



<P>the brigade, after a little time, came marching



<P>airily out again with its fine formation in nowise



<P>disturbed.&nbsp; There were no traces of speed in its



<P>movements.&nbsp; The brigade was jaunty and seemed



<P>to point a proud thumb at the yelling wood.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>On a slope to the left there was a long row of



<P>guns, gruff and maddened, denouncing the



<P>enemy, who, down through the woods, were



<P>forming for another attack in the pitiless mo-



<P>notony of conflicts.&nbsp; The round red discharges



<P>from the guns made a crimson flare and a high,



<P>thick smoke.&nbsp; Occasional glimpses could be



<P>caught of groups of the toiling artillerymen.&nbsp; In



<P>the rear of this row of guns stood a house, calm



<P>and white, amid bursting shells.&nbsp; A congregation



<P>of horses, tied to a long railing, were tugging



<P>frenziedly at their bridles.&nbsp; Men were running



<P>hither and thither.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The detached battle between the four regi-



<P>ments lasted for some time.&nbsp; There chanced to



<P>be no interference, and they settled their dispute



<P>by themselves.&nbsp; They struck savagely and pow-



<P>erfully at each other for a period of minutes, and



<P>then the lighter-hued regiments faltered and



<P>drew back, leaving the dark-blue lines shouting.



<P>The youth could see the two flags shaking with



<P>laughter amid the smoke remnants.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Presently there was a stillness, pregnant with



<P>meaning.&nbsp; The blue lines shifted and changed a



<P>trifle and stared expectantly at the silent woods



<P>and fields before them.&nbsp; The hush was solemn



<P>and churchlike, save for a distant battery that,



<P>evidently unable to remain quiet, sent a faint



<P>rolling thunder over the ground.&nbsp; It irritated,



<P>like the noises of unimpressed boys.&nbsp; The men



<P>imagined that it would prevent their perched



<P>ears from hearing the first words of the new



<P>battle.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Of a sudden the guns on the slope roared out



<P>a message of warning.&nbsp; A spluttering sound had



<P>begun in the woods.&nbsp; It swelled with amazing



<P>speed to a profound clamor that involved the



<P>earth in noises.&nbsp; The splitting crashes swept



<P>along the lines until an interminable roar was



<P>developed.&nbsp; To those in the midst of it it became



<P>a din fitted to the universe.&nbsp; It was the whirring



<P>and thumping of gigantic machinery, complica-



<P>tions among the smaller stars.&nbsp; The youth's ears



<P>were filled up.&nbsp; They were incapable of hearing



<P>more.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>On an incline over which a road wound he



<P>saw wild and desperate rushes of men perpet-



<P>ually backward and forward in riotous surges.



<P>These parts of the opposing armies were two



<P>long waves that pitched upon each other madly



<P>at dictated points.&nbsp; To and fro they swelled.



<P>Sometimes, one side by its yells and cheers would



<P>proclaim decisive blows, but a moment later



<P>the other side would be all yells and cheers.



<P>Once the youth saw a spray of light forms go in



<P>houndlike leaps toward the waving blue lines.



<P>There was much howling, and presently it went



<P>away with a vast mouthful of prisoners.&nbsp; Again,



<P>he saw a blue wave dash with such thunderous



<P>force against a gray obstruction that it seemed to



<P>clear the earth of it and leave nothing but



<P>trampled sod.&nbsp; And always in their swift and



<P>deadly rushes to and fro the men screamed



<P>and yelled like maniacs.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Particular pieces of fence or secure positions



<P>behind collections of trees were wrangled over,



<P>as gold thrones or pearl bedsteads.&nbsp; There were



<P>desperate lunges at these chosen spots seemingly



<P>every instant, and most of them were bandied like



<P>light toys between the contending forces.&nbsp; The



<P>youth could not tell from the battle flags flying



<P>like crimson foam in many directions which color



<P>of cloth was winning.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His emaciated regiment bustled forth with



<P>undiminished fierceness when its time came.



<P>When assaulted again by bullets, the men burst



<P>out in a barbaric cry of rage and pain.&nbsp; They



<P>bent their heads in aims of intent hatred



<P>behind the projected hammers of their guns.



<P>Their ramrods clanged loud with fury as their



<P>eager arms pounded the cartridges into the rifle



<P>barrels.&nbsp; The front of the regiment was a smoke-



<P>wall penetrated by the flashing points of yellow



<P>and red.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Wallowing in the fight, they were in an



<P>astonishingly short time resmudged.&nbsp; They



<P>surpassed in stain and dirt all their previous ap-



<P>pearances.&nbsp; Moving to and fro with strained



<P>exertion, jabbering the while, they were, with



<P>their swaying bodies, black faces, and glowing



<P>eyes, like strange and ugly friends jigging heavily



<P>in the smoke.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The lieutenant, returning from a tour after a



<P>bandage, produced from a hidden receptacle of



<P>his mind new and portentous oaths suited to the



<P>emergency.&nbsp; Strings of expletives he swung



<P>lashlike over the backs of his men, and it was



<P>evident that his previous efforts had in nowise



<P>impaired his resources.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth, still the bearer of the colors, did



<P>not feel his idleness.&nbsp; He was deeply absorbed as



<P>a spectator.&nbsp; The crash and swing of the great



<P>drama made him lean forward, intent-eyed, his



<P>face working in small contortions.&nbsp; Sometimes he



<P>prattled, words coming unconsciously from him



<P>in grotesque exclamations.&nbsp; He did not know



<P>that he breathed; that the flag hung silently over



<P>him, so absorbed was he.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A formidable line of the enemy came within



<P>dangerous range.&nbsp; They could be seen plainly--



<P>tall, gaunt men with excited faces running with



<P>long strides toward a wandering fence.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At sight of this danger the men suddenly



<P>ceased their cursing monotone.&nbsp; There was an



<P>instant of strained silence before they threw up



<P>their rifles and fired a plumping volley at the



<P>foes.&nbsp; There had been no order given; the men,



<P>upon recognizing the menace, had immedi-



<P>ately let drive their flock of bullets without wait-



<P>ing for word of command.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But the enemy were quick to gain the protec-



<P>tion of the wandering line of fence.&nbsp; They slid down



<P>behind it with remarkable celerity, and from this



<P>position they began briskly to slice up the blue men.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>These latter braced their energies for a great



<P>struggle.&nbsp; Often, white clinched teeth shone



<P>from the dusky faces.&nbsp; Many heads surged to



<P>and fro, floating upon a pale sea of smoke.



<P>Those behind the fence frequently shouted and



<P>yelped in taunts and gibelike cries, but the regi-



<P>ment maintained a stressed silence.&nbsp; Perhaps, at



<P>this new assault the men recalled the fact that



<P>they had been named mud diggers, and it made



<P>their situation thrice bitter.&nbsp; They were breath-



<P>lessly intent upon keeping the ground and thrust-



<P>ing away the rejoicing body of the enemy.&nbsp; They



<P>fought swiftly and with a despairing savageness



<P>denoted in their expressions.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth had resolved not to budge what-



<P>ever should happen.&nbsp; Some arrows of scorn that



<P>had buried themselves in his heart had generated



<P>strange and unspeakable hatred.&nbsp; It was clear



<P>to him that his final and absolute revenge was to



<P>be achieved by his dead body lying, torn and



<P>gluttering, upon the field.&nbsp; This was to be a



<P>poignant retaliation upon the officer who had



<P>said "mule drivers," and later "mud diggers,"



<P>for in all the wild graspings of his mind for a



<P>unit responsible for his sufferings and commo-



<P>tions he always seized upon the man who had



<P>dubbed him wrongly.&nbsp; And it was his idea,



<P>vaguely formulated, that his corpse would be for



<P>those eyes a great and salt reproach.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The regiment bled extravagantly.&nbsp; Grunting



<P>bundles of blue began to drop.&nbsp; The orderly



<P>sergeant of the youth's company was shot through



<P>the cheeks.&nbsp; Its supports being injured, his jaw



<P>hung afar down, disclosing in the wide cavern of



<P>his mouth a pulsing mass of blood and teeth.



<P>And with it all he made attempts to cry out.



<P>In his endeavor there was a dreadful earnestness,



<P>as if he conceived that one great shriek would



<P>make him well.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth saw him presently go rearward.



<P>His strength seemed in nowise impaired.&nbsp; He



<P>ran swiftly, casting wild glances for succor.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Others fell down about the feet of their com-



<P>panions.&nbsp; Some of the wounded crawled out and



<P>away, but many lay still, their bodies twisted into



<P>impossible shapes.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth looked once for his friend.&nbsp; He



<P>saw a vehement young man, powder-smeared and



<P>frowzled, whom he knew to be him.&nbsp; The lieu-



<P>tenant, also, was unscathed in his position at the



<P>rear.&nbsp; He had continued to curse, but it was now



<P>with the air of a man who was using his last box



<P>of oaths.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>For the fire of the regiment had begun to



<P>wane and drip.&nbsp; The robust voice, that had come



<P>strangely from the thin ranks, was growing



<P>rapidly weak.



<P>

<BR><FONT SIZE=+1><A HREF="chapter08.htm">Chapter&nbsp;&nbsp; 8</A>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;

<A HREF="chapter20.htm">Chapter&nbsp;&nbsp; 20</A></FONT>

<BR><FONT SIZE=+1><A HREF="chapter09.htm">Chapter&nbsp;&nbsp; 9</A>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;

<A HREF="chapter21.htm">Chapter&nbsp;&nbsp; 21</A></FONT>

<BR><FONT SIZE=+1><A HREF="chapter10.htm">Chapter&nbsp; 10</A>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;

<BR><FONT SIZE=+1><A HREF="chapter11.htm">Chapter&nbsp; 11</A>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;

<BR><FONT SIZE=+1><A HREF="chapter12.htm">Chapter&nbsp; 12</A>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;

   <TITLE>chapter13</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XIII.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>THE youth went slowly toward the fire in-



<P>dicated by his departed friend.&nbsp; As he reeled, he



<P>bethought him of the welcome his comrades



<P>would give him.&nbsp; He had a conviction that he



<P>would soon feel in his sore heart the barbed



<P>missiles of ridicule.&nbsp; He had no strength to in-



<P>vent a tale; he would be a soft target.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He made vague plans to go off into the deeper



<P>darkness and hide, but they were all destroyed



<P>by the voices of exhaustion and pain from his



<P>body.&nbsp; His ailments, clamoring, forced him to



<P>seek the place of food and rest, at whatever cost.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He swung unsteadily toward the fire.&nbsp; He



<P>could see the forms of men throwing black



<P>shadows in the red light, and as he went nearer



<P>it became known to him in some way that the



<P>ground was strewn with sleeping men.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Of a sudden he confronted a black and



<P>monstrous figure.&nbsp; A rifle barrel caught some



<P>glinting beams.&nbsp; "Halt! halt!"&nbsp; He was dis-

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>129



<P>mayed for a moment, but he presently thought



<P>that he recognized the nervous voice.&nbsp; As he



<P>stood tottering before the rifle barrel, he called



<P>out: "Why, hello, Wilson, you--you here?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The rifle was lowered to a position of caution



<P>and the loud soldier came slowly forward.&nbsp; He



<P>peered into the youth's face.&nbsp; "That you,



<P>Henry?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes, it's--it's me."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, well, ol' boy," said the other, "by



<P>ginger, I'm glad t' see yeh!&nbsp; I give yeh up



<P>fer a goner.&nbsp; I thought yeh was dead sure



<P>enough."&nbsp; There was husky emotion in his



<P>voice.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth found that now he could barely



<P>stand upon his feet.&nbsp; There was a sudden sinking



<P>of his forces.&nbsp; He thought he must hasten to pro-



<P>duce his tale to protect him from the missiles



<P>already at the lips of his redoubtable comrades.



<P>So, staggering before the loud soldier, he began:



<P>"Yes, yes.&nbsp; I've--I've had an awful time.&nbsp; I've



<P>been all over.&nbsp; Way over on th' right.&nbsp; Ter'ble



<P>fightin' over there.&nbsp; I had an awful time.&nbsp; I got



<P>separated from th' reg'ment.&nbsp; Over on th' right,



<P>I got shot.&nbsp; In th' head.&nbsp; I never see sech



<P>fightin'.&nbsp; Awful time.&nbsp; I don't see how I could 'a



<P>got separated from th' reg'ment.&nbsp; I got shot,



<P>too."



<P>His friend had stepped forward quickly.



<P>"What?&nbsp; Got shot?&nbsp; Why didn't yeh say so



<P>first?&nbsp; Poor ol' boy, we must--hol' on a minnit;



<P>what am I doin'.&nbsp; I'll call Simpson."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Another figure at that moment loomed in the



<P>gloom.&nbsp; They could see that it was the corporal.



<P>"Who yeh talkin' to, Wilson?" he demanded.



<P>His voice was anger-toned.&nbsp; "Who yeh talkin'



<P>to?&nbsp; Yeh th' derndest sentinel--why--hello,



<P>Henry, you here?&nbsp; Why, I thought you was



<P>dead four hours ago!&nbsp; Great Jerusalem, they



<P>keep turnin' up every ten minutes or so!&nbsp; We



<P>thought we'd lost forty-two men by straight



<P>count, but if they keep on a-comin' this way, we'll



<P>git th' comp'ny all back by mornin' yit.&nbsp; Where



<P>was yeh?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Over on th' right.&nbsp; I got separated"--began



<P>the youth with considerable glibness.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But his friend had interrupted hastily.&nbsp; "Yes,



<P>an' he got shot in th' head an' he's in a fix, an' we



<P>must see t' him right away."&nbsp; He rested his rifle



<P>in the hollow of his left arm and his right around



<P>the youth's shoulder.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Gee, it must hurt like thunder!" he said.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth leaned heavily upon his friend.



<P>"Yes, it hurts--hurts a good deal," he replied.



<P>There was a faltering in his voice.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh," said the corporal.&nbsp; He linked his arm



<P>in the youth's and drew him forward.&nbsp; "Come



<P>on, Henry.&nbsp; I'll take keer 'a yeh."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As they went on together the loud private



<P>called out after them: "Put 'im t' sleep in my



<P>blanket, Simpson.&nbsp; An'--hol' on a minnit--here's



<P>my canteen.&nbsp; It's full 'a coffee.&nbsp; Look at his head



<P>by th' fire an' see how it looks.&nbsp; Maybe it's a



<P>pretty bad un.&nbsp; When I git relieved in a couple



<P>'a minnits, I'll be over an' see t' him."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth's senses were so deadened that his



<P>friend's voice sounded from afar and he could



<P>scarcely feel the pressure of the corporal's arm.



<P>He submitted passively to the latter's directing



<P>strength.&nbsp; His head was in the old manner hang-



<P>ing forward upon his breast.&nbsp; His knees wobbled.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The corporal led him into the glare of the



<P>fire.&nbsp; "Now, Henry," he said, "let's have look at



<P>yer ol' head."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth sat down obediently and the cor-



<P>poral, laying aside his rifle, began to fumble in the



<P>bushy hair of his comrade.&nbsp; He was obliged to



<P>turn the other's head so that the full flush of the



<P>fire light would beam upon it.&nbsp; He puckered his



<P>mouth with a critical air.&nbsp; He drew back his lips



<P>and whistled through his teeth when his fingers



<P>came in contact with the splashed blood and the



<P>rare wound.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Ah, here we are!" he said.&nbsp; He awkwardly



<P>made further investigations.&nbsp; "Jest as I thought,"



<P>he added, presently.&nbsp; "Yeh've been grazed by a



<P>ball.&nbsp; It's raised a queer lump jest as if some



<P>feller had lammed yeh on th' head with a club.



<P>It stopped a-bleedin' long time ago.&nbsp; Th' most



<P>about it is that in th' mornin' yeh'll feel that a



<P>number ten hat wouldn't fit yeh.&nbsp; An' your



<P>head'll be all het up an' feel as dry as burnt pork.



<P>An' yeh may git a lot 'a other sicknesses, too, by



<P>mornin'.&nbsp; Yeh can't never tell.&nbsp; Still, I don't



<P>much think so.&nbsp; It's jest a damn' good belt on th'



<P>head, an' nothin' more.&nbsp; Now, you jest sit here



<P>an' don't move, while I go rout out th' relief.



<P>Then I'll send Wilson t' take keer 'a yeh."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The corporal went away.&nbsp; The youth re-



<P>mained on the ground like a parcel.&nbsp; He stared



<P>with a vacant look into the fire.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After a time he aroused, for some part, and



<P>the things about him began to take form.&nbsp; He



<P>saw that the ground in the deep shadows was



<P>cluttered with men, sprawling in every con-



<P>ceivable posture.&nbsp; Glancing narrowly into the



<P>more distant darkness, he caught occasional



<P>glimpses of visages that loomed pallid and



<P>ghostly, lit with a phosphorescent glow.&nbsp; These



<P>faces expressed in their lines the deep stupor of



<P>the tired soldiers.&nbsp; They made them appear like



<P>men drunk with wine.&nbsp; This bit of forest might



<P>have appeared to an ethereal wanderer as a scene



<P>of the result of some frightful debauch.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>On the other side of the fire the youth



<P>observed an officer asleep, seated bolt upright,



<P>with his back against a tree.&nbsp; There was some-



<P>thing perilous in his position.&nbsp; Badgered by



<P>dreams, perhaps, he swayed with little bounces



<P>and starts, like an old toddy-stricken grandfather



<P>in a chimney corner.&nbsp; Dust and stains were upon



<P>his face.&nbsp; His lower jaw hung down as if lacking



<P>strength to assume its normal position.&nbsp; He was



<P>the picture of an exhausted soldier after a feast of



<P>war.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He had evidently gone to sleep with his



<P>sword in his arms.&nbsp; These two had slumbered in



<P>an embrace, but the weapon had been allowed



<P>in time to fall unheeded to the ground.&nbsp; The



<P>brass-mounted hilt lay in contact with some parts



<P>of the fire.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Within the gleam of rose and orange light



<P>from the burning sticks were other soldiers,



<P>snoring and heaving, or lying deathlike in



<P>slumber.&nbsp; A few pairs of legs were stuck forth,



<P>rigid and straight.&nbsp; The shoes displayed the mud



<P>or dust of marches and bits of rounded trousers,



<P>protruding from the blankets, showed rents and



<P>tears from hurried pitchings through the dense



<P>brambles.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The fire crackled musically.&nbsp; From it swelled



<P>light smoke.&nbsp; Overhead the foliage moved



<P>softly.&nbsp; The leaves, with their faces turned



<P>toward the blaze, were colored shifting hues of



<P>silver, often edged with red.&nbsp; Far off to the right,



<P>through a window in the forest could be seen a



<P>handful of stars lying, like glittering pebbles, on



<P>the black level of the night.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Occasionally, in this low-arched hall, a soldier



<P>would arouse and turn his body to a new posi-



<P>tion, the experience of his sleep having taught



<P>him of uneven and objectionable places upon the



<P>ground under him.&nbsp; Or, perhaps, he would lift



<P>himself to a sitting posture, blink at the fire for



<P>an unintelligent moment, throw a swift glance at



<P>his prostrate companion, and then cuddle down



<P>again with a grunt of sleepy content.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth sat in a forlorn heap until his



<P>friend the loud young soldier came, swinging two



<P>canteens by their light strings.&nbsp; "Well, now,



<P>Henry, ol' boy," said the latter, "we'll have yeh



<P>fixed up in jest about a minnit."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He had the bustling ways of an amateur



<P>nurse.&nbsp; He fussed around the fire and stirred the



<P>sticks to brilliant exertions.&nbsp; He made his patient



<P>drink largely from the canteen that contained the



<P>coffee.&nbsp; It was to the youth a delicious draught.



<P>He tilted his head afar back and held the canteen



<P>long to his lips.&nbsp; The cool mixture went caress-



<P>ingly down his blistered throat.&nbsp; Having finished,



<P>he sighed with comfortable delight.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The loud young soldier watched his comrade



<P>with an air of satisfaction.&nbsp; He later produced



<P>an extensive handkerchief from his pocket.&nbsp; He



<P>folded it into a manner of bandage and soused



<P>water from the other canteen upon the middle of



<P>it.&nbsp; This crude arrangement he bound over the



<P>youth's head, tying the ends in a queer knot at



<P>the back of the neck.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"There," he said, moving off and surveying



<P>his deed, "yeh look like th' devil, but I bet yeh



<P>feel better."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth contemplated his friend with grate-



<P>ful eyes.&nbsp; Upon his aching and swelling head the



<P>cold cloth was like a tender woman's hand.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yeh don't holler ner say nothin'," remarked



<P>his friend approvingly.&nbsp; "I know I'm a black-



<P>smith at takin' keer 'a sick folks, an' yeh never



<P>squeaked.&nbsp; Yer a good un, Henry.&nbsp; Most 'a men



<P>would a' been in th' hospital long ago.&nbsp; A shot in



<P>th' head ain't foolin' business."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth made no reply, but began to fumble



<P>with the buttons of his jacket.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, come, now," continued his friend,



<P>"come on.&nbsp; I must put yeh t' bed an' see that yeh



<P>git a good night's rest."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The other got carefully erect, and the loud



<P>young soldier led him among the sleeping forms



<P>lying in groups and rows.&nbsp; Presently he stooped



<P>and picked up his blankets.&nbsp; He spread the rubber



<P>one upon the ground and placed the woolen one



<P>about the youth's shoulders.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"There now," he said, "lie down an' git some



<P>sleep."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth, with his manner of doglike obe-



<P>dience, got carefully down like a crone stoop-



<P>ing.&nbsp; He stretched out with a murmur of relief



<P>and comfort.&nbsp; The ground felt like the softest



<P>couch.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But of a sudden he ejaculated: "Hol' on a



<P>minnit!&nbsp; Where you goin' t' sleep?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His friend waved his hand impatiently.



<P>"Right down there by yeh."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, but hol' on a minnit," continued the



<P>youth.&nbsp; "What yeh goin' t' sleep in?&nbsp; I've got



<P>your--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The loud young soldier snarled: "Shet up



<P>an' go on t' sleep.&nbsp; Don't be makin' a damn' fool



<P>'a yerself," he said severely.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After the reproof the youth said no more.



<P>An exquisite drowsiness had spread through him.



<P>The warm comfort of the blanket enveloped him



<P>and made a gentle languor.&nbsp; His head fell for-



<P>ward on his crooked arm and his weighted lids



<P>went softly down over his eyes.&nbsp; Hearing a



<P>splatter of musketry from the distance, he



<P>wondered indifferently if those men sometimes



<P>slept.&nbsp; He gave a long sigh, snuggled down into



<P>his blanket, and in a moment was like his com-



<P>rades.



<P>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER VII.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>THE youth cringed as if discovered in a crime.



<P>By heavens, they had won after all!&nbsp; The im-



<P>becile line had remained and become victors.



<P>He could hear cheering.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He lifted himself upon his toes and looked in



<P>the direction of the fight.&nbsp; A yellow fog lay wal-



<P>lowing on the treetops.&nbsp; From beneath it came



<P>the clatter of musketry.&nbsp; Hoarse cries told of an



<P>advance.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He turned away amazed and angry.&nbsp; He felt



<P>that he had been wronged.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He had fled, he told himself, because annihila-



<P>tion approached.&nbsp; He had done a good part in



<P>saving himself, who was a little piece of the army.



<P>He had considered the time, he said, to be one in



<P>which it was the duty of every little piece to res-



<P>cue itself if possible.&nbsp; Later the officers could fit



<P>the little pieces together again, and make a battle



<P>front.&nbsp; If none of the little pieces were wise enough



<P>to save themselves from the flurry of death at such



<P>a time, why, then, where would be the army?&nbsp; It



<P>was all plain that he had proceeded according to



<P>very correct and commendable rules.&nbsp; His ac-



<P>tions had been sagacious things.&nbsp; They had been



<P>full of strategy.&nbsp; They were the work of a mas-



<P>ter's legs.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Thoughts of his comrades came to him.&nbsp; The



<P>brittle blue line had withstood the blows and won.



<P>He grew bitter over it.&nbsp; It seemed that the blind



<P>ignorance and stupidity of those little pieces had



<P>betrayed him.&nbsp; He had been overturned and



<P>crushed by their lack of sense in holding the po-



<P>sition, when intelligent deliberation would have



<P>convinced them that it was impossible.&nbsp; He, the



<P>enlightened man who looks afar in the dark, had



<P>fled because of his superior perceptions and



<P>knowledge.&nbsp; He felt a great anger against his



<P>comrades.&nbsp; He knew it could be proved that



<P>they had been fools.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He wondered what they would remark when



<P>later he appeared in camp.&nbsp; His mind heard



<P>howls of derision.&nbsp; Their density would not en-



<P>able them to understand his sharper point of



<P>view.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He began to pity himself acutely.&nbsp; He was



<P>ill used.&nbsp; He was trodden beneath the feet of an



<P>iron injustice.&nbsp; He had proceeded with wisdom



<P>and from the most righteous motives under



<P>heaven's blue only to be frustrated by hateful



<P>circumstances.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A dull, animal-like rebellion against his fel-



<P>lows, war in the abstract, and fate grew within



<P>him.&nbsp; He shambled along with bowed head, his



<P>brain in a tumult of agony and despair.&nbsp; When



<P>he looked loweringly up, quivering at each



<P>sound, his eyes had the expression of those of



<P>a criminal who thinks his guilt and his pun-



<P>ishment great, and knows that he can find no



<P>words.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He went from the fields into a thick woods, as



<P>if resolved to bury himself.&nbsp; He wished to get



<P>out of hearing of the crackling shots which were



<P>to him like voices.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The ground was cluttered with vines and



<P>bushes, and the trees grew close and spread out



<P>like bouquets.&nbsp; He was obliged to force his way



<P>with much noise.&nbsp; The creepers, catching against



<P>his legs, cried out harshly as their sprays were



<P>torn from the barks of trees.&nbsp; The swishing sap-



<P>lings tried to make known his presence to the



<P>world.&nbsp; He could not conciliate the forest.&nbsp; As



<P>he made his way, it was always calling out prot-



<P>estations.&nbsp; When he separated embraces of trees



<P>and vines the disturbed foliages waved their arms



<P>and turned their face leaves toward him.&nbsp; He



<P>dreaded lest these noisy motions and cries should



<P>bring men to look at him.&nbsp; So he went far, seek-



<P>ing dark and intricate places.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After a time the sound of musketry grew faint



<P>and the cannon boomed in the distance.&nbsp; The sun,



<P>suddenly apparent, blazed among the trees.&nbsp; The



<P>insects were making rhythmical noises.&nbsp; They



<P>seemed to be grinding their teeth in unison.&nbsp; A



<P>woodpecker stuck his impudent head around the



<P>side of a tree.&nbsp; A bird flew on lighthearted wing.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Off was the rumble of death.&nbsp; It seemed now



<P>that Nature had no ears.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>This landscape gave him assurance.&nbsp; A fair



<P>field holding life.&nbsp; It was the religion of peace.



<P>It would die if its timid eyes were compelled to



<P>see blood.&nbsp; He conceived Nature to be a woman



<P>with a deep aversion to tragedy.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He threw a pine cone at a jovial squirrel, and



<P>he ran with chattering fear.&nbsp; High in a treetop



<P>he stopped, and, poking his head cautiously from



<P>behind a branch, looked down with an air of trepi-



<P>dation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth felt triumphant at this exhibition.



<P>There was the law, he said.&nbsp; Nature had given



<P>him a sign.&nbsp; The squirrel, immediately upon rec-



<P>ognizing danger, had taken to his legs without



<P>ado.&nbsp; He did not stand stolidly baring his furry



<P>belly to the missile, and die with an upward



<P>glance at the sympathetic heavens.&nbsp; On the con-



<P>trary, he had fled as fast as his legs could carry



<P>him; and he was but an ordinary squirrel, too--



<P>doubtless no philosopher of his race.&nbsp; The youth



<P>wended, feeling that Nature was of his mind.



<P>She re-enforced his argument with proofs that



<P>lived where the sun shone.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Once he found himself almost into a swamp.



<P>He was obliged to walk upon bog tufts and



<P>watch his feet to keep from the oily mire.&nbsp; Paus-



<P>ing at one time to look about him he saw, out at



<P>some black water, a small animal pounce in and



<P>emerge directly with a gleaming fish.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth went again into the deep thickets.



<P>The brushed branches made a noise that drowned



<P>the sounds of cannon.&nbsp; He walked on, going from



<P>obscurity into promises of a greater obscurity.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At length he reached a place where the high,



<P>arching boughs made a chapel.&nbsp; He softly pushed



<P>the green doors aside and entered.&nbsp; Pine needles



<P>were a gentle brown carpet.&nbsp; There was a reli-



<P>gious half light.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Near the threshold he stopped, horror-stricken



<P>at the sight of a thing.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He was being looked at by a dead man who



<P>was seated with his back against a columnlike



<P>tree.&nbsp; The corpse was dressed in a uniform that



<P>once had been blue, but was now faded to a mel-



<P>ancholy shade of green.&nbsp; The eyes, staring at the



<P>youth, had changed to the dull hue to be seen on



<P>the side of a dead fish.&nbsp; The mouth was open.



<P>Its red had changed to an appalling yellow.



<P>Over the gray skin of the face ran little ants.



<P>One was trundling some sort of a bundle along



<P>the upper lip.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth gave a shriek as he confronted the



<P>thing.&nbsp; He was for moments turned to stone be-



<P>fore it.&nbsp; He remained staring into the liquid-look-



<P>ing eyes.&nbsp; The dead man and the living man ex-



<P>changed a long look.&nbsp; Then the youth cautiously



<P>put one hand behind him and brought it against



<P>a tree.&nbsp; Leaning upon this he retreated, step by



<P>step, with his face still toward the thing.&nbsp; He



<P>feared that if he turned his back the body might



<P>spring up and stealthily pursue him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The branches, pushing against him, threat-



<P>ened to throw him over upon it.&nbsp; His unguided



<P>feet, too, caught aggravatingly in brambles; and



<P>with it all he received a subtle suggestion to



<P>touch the corpse.&nbsp; As he thought of his hand



<P>upon it he shuddered profoundly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At last he burst the bonds which had fastened



<P>him to the spot and fled, unheeding the under-



<P>brush.&nbsp; He was pursued by a sight of the black



<P>ants swarming greedily upon the gray face and



<P>venturing horribly near to the eyes.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After a time he paused, and, breathless and



<P>panting, listened.&nbsp; He imagined some strange



<P>voice would come from the dead throat and



<P>squawk after him in horrible menaces.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The trees about the portal of the chapel



<P>moved soughingly in a soft wind.&nbsp; A sad silence



<P>was upon the little guarding edifice.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter06</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER VI.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>THE youth awakened slowly.&nbsp; He came grad-



<P>ually back to a position from which he could re-



<P>gard himself.&nbsp; For moments he had been scruti-



<P>nizing his person in a dazed way as if he had



<P>never before seen himself.&nbsp; Then he picked up



<P>his cap from the ground.&nbsp; He wriggled in his



<P>jacket to make a more comfortable fit, and kneel-



<P>ing relaced his shoe.&nbsp; He thoughtfully mopped



<P>his reeking features.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>So it was all over at last!&nbsp; The supreme trial



<P>had been passed.&nbsp; The red, formidable difficulties



<P>of war had been vanquished.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He went into an ecstasy of self-satisfaction.



<P>He had the most delightful sensations of his life.



<P>Standing as if apart from himself, he viewed that



<P>last scene.&nbsp; He perceived that the man who had



<P>fought thus was magnificent.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He felt that he was a fine fellow.&nbsp; He saw



<P>himself even with those ideals which he had con-



<P>sidered as far beyond him.&nbsp; He smiled in deep



<P>gratification.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Upon his fellows he beamed tenderness and



<P>good will.&nbsp; "Gee! ain't it hot, hey?" he said



<P>affably to a man who was polishing his stream-



<P>ing face with his coat sleeves.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"You bet!" said the other, grinning sociably.



<P>"I never seen sech dumb hotness."&nbsp; He sprawled



<P>out luxuriously on the ground.&nbsp; "Gee, yes!&nbsp; An'



<P>I hope we don't have no more fightin' till a week



<P>from Monday."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There were some handshakings and deep



<P>speeches with men whose features were familiar,



<P>but with whom the youth now felt the bonds of



<P>tied hearts.&nbsp; He helped a cursing comrade to



<P>bind up a wound of the shin.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But, of a sudden, cries of amazement broke



<P>out along the ranks of the new regiment.&nbsp; "Here



<P>they come ag'in!&nbsp; Here they come ag'in!"&nbsp; The



<P>man who had sprawled upon the ground started



<P>up and said, "Gosh!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth turned quick eyes upon the field.



<P>He discerned forms begin to swell in masses out



<P>of a distant wood.&nbsp; He again saw the tilted flag



<P>speeding forward.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The shells, which had ceased to trouble the



<P>regiment for a time, came swirling again, and ex-



<P>ploded in the grass or among the leaves of the



<P>trees.&nbsp; They looked to be strange war flowers



<P>bursting into fierce bloom.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The men groaned.&nbsp; The luster faded from



<P>their eyes.&nbsp; Their smudged countenances now



<P>expressed a profound dejection.&nbsp; They moved



<P>their stiffened bodies slowly, and watched in sul-



<P>len mood the frantic approach of the enemy.&nbsp; The



<P>slaves toiling in the temple of this god began to



<P>feel rebellion at his harsh tasks.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They fretted and complained each to each.



<P>"Oh, say, this is too much of a good thing!&nbsp; Why



<P>can't somebody send us supports?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"We ain't never goin' to stand this second



<P>banging.&nbsp; I didn't come here to fight the hull



<P>damn' rebel army."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was one who raised a doleful cry.&nbsp; "I



<P>wish Bill Smithers had trod on my hand, in-



<P>steader me treddin' on his'n."&nbsp; The sore joints of



<P>the regiment creaked as it painfully floundered



<P>into position to repulse.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth stared.&nbsp; Surely, he thought, this



<P>impossible thing was not about to happen.&nbsp; He



<P>waited as if he expected the enemy to suddenly



<P>stop, apologize, and retire bowing.&nbsp; It was all a



<P>mistake.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But the firing began somewhere on the regi-



<P>mental line and ripped along in both directions.



<P>The level sheets of flame developed great clouds



<P>of smoke that tumbled and tossed in the mild



<P>wind near the ground for a moment, and then



<P>rolled through the ranks as through a gate.&nbsp; The



<P>clouds were tinged an earthlike yellow in the



<P>sunrays and in the shadow were a sorry blue.



<P>The flag was sometimes eaten and lost in this



<P>mass of vapor, but more often it projected, sun-



<P>touched, resplendent.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Into the youth's eyes there came a look that



<P>one can see in the orbs of a jaded horse.&nbsp; His



<P>neck was quivering with nervous weakness and



<P>the muscles of his arms felt numb and bloodless.



<P>His hands, too, seemed large and awkward as if



<P>he was wearing invisible mittens.&nbsp; And there was



<P>a great uncertainty about his knee joints.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The words that comrades had uttered previous



<P>to the firing began to recur to him.&nbsp; "Oh, say,



<P>this is too much of a good thing!&nbsp; What do they



<P>take us for--why don't they send supports?&nbsp; I



<P>didn't come here to fight the hull damned rebel



<P>army."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He began to exaggerate the endurance, the



<P>skill, and the valor of those who were coming.



<P>Himself reeling from exhaustion, he was aston-



<P>ished beyond measure at such persistency.&nbsp; They



<P>must be machines of steel.&nbsp; It was very gloomy



<P>struggling against such affairs, wound up perhaps



<P>to fight until sundown.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He slowly lifted his rifle and catching a



<P>glimpse of the thickspread field he blazed at a



<P>cantering cluster.&nbsp; He stopped then and began



<P>to peer as best he could through the smoke.&nbsp; He



<P>caught changing views of the ground covered



<P>with men who were all running like pursued



<P>imps, and yelling.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>To the youth it was an onslaught of redoubt-



<P>able dragons.&nbsp; He became like the man who lost



<P>his legs at the approach of the red and green



<P>monster.&nbsp; He waited in a sort of a horrified,



<P>listening attitude.&nbsp; He seemed to shut his eyes



<P>and wait to be gobbled.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A man near him who up to this time had been



<P>working feverishly at his rifle suddenly stopped



<P>and ran with howls.&nbsp; A lad whose face had borne



<P>an expression of exalted courage, the majesty of



<P>he who dares give his life, was, at an instant,



<P>smitten abject.&nbsp; He blanched like one who has



<P>come to the edge of a cliff at midnight and is sud-



<P>denly made aware.&nbsp; There was a revelation.&nbsp; He,



<P>too, threw down his gun and fled.&nbsp; There was no



<P>shame in his face.&nbsp; He ran like a rabbit.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Others began to scamper away through the



<P>smoke.&nbsp; The youth turned his head, shaken from



<P>his trance by this movement as if the regiment



<P>was leaving him behind.&nbsp; He saw the few fleeting



<P>forms.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He yelled then with fright and swung about.



<P>For a moment, in the great clamor, he was like a



<P>proverbial chicken.&nbsp; He lost the direction of



<P>safety.&nbsp; Destruction threatened him from all



<P>points.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Directly he began to speed toward the rear in



<P>great leaps.&nbsp; His rifle and cap were gone.&nbsp; His



<P>unbuttoned coat bulged in the wind.&nbsp; The flap of



<P>his cartridge box bobbed wildly, and his canteen,



<P>by its slender cord, swung out behind.&nbsp; On his



<P>face was all the horror of those things which he



<P>imagined.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The lieutenant sprang forward bawling.&nbsp; The



<P>youth saw his features wrathfully red, and saw



<P>him make a dab with his sword.&nbsp; His one thought



<P>of the incident was that the lieutenant was a pecul-



<P>iar creature to feel interested in such matters



<P>upon this occasion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He ran like a blind man.&nbsp; Two or three times



<P>he fell down.&nbsp; Once he knocked his shoulder so



<P>heavily against a tree that he went headlong.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Since he had turned his back upon the fight



<P>his fears had been wondrously magnified.&nbsp; Death



<P>about to thrust him between the shoulder blades



<P>was far more dreadful than death about to smite



<P>him between the eyes.&nbsp; When he thought of it



<P>later, he conceived the impression that it is better



<P>to view the appalling than to be merely within



<P>hearing.&nbsp; The noises of the battle were like



<P>stones; he believed himself liable to be crushed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As he ran he mingled with others.&nbsp; He



<P>dimly saw men on his right and on his left, and



<P>he heard footsteps behind him.&nbsp; He thought that



<P>all the regiment was fleeing, pursued by these



<P>ominous crashes.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In his flight the sound of these following foot-



<P>steps gave him his one meager relief.&nbsp; He felt



<P>vaguely that death must make a first choice of



<P>the men who were nearest; the initial morsels for



<P>the dragons would be then those who were fol-



<P>lowing him.&nbsp; So he displayed the zeal of an insane



<P>sprinter in his purpose to keep them in the rear.



<P>There was a race.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As he, leading, went across a little field, he



<P>found himself in a region of shells.&nbsp; They hurtled



<P>over his head with long wild screams.&nbsp; As he



<P>listened he imagined them to have rows of cruel



<P>teeth that grinned at him.&nbsp; Once one lit before



<P>him and the livid lightning of the explosion



<P>effectually barred the way in his chosen direc-



<P>tion.&nbsp; He groveled on the ground and then



<P>springing up went careering off through some



<P>bushes.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He experienced a thrill of amazement when



<P>he came within view of a battery in action.&nbsp; The



<P>men there seemed to be in conventional moods,



<P>altogether unaware of the impending annihila-



<P>tion.&nbsp; The battery was disputing with a distant



<P>antagonist and the gunners were wrapped in



<P>admiration of their shooting.&nbsp; They were con-



<P>tinually bending in coaxing postures over the



<P>guns.&nbsp; They seemed to be patting them on the



<P>back and encouraging them with words.&nbsp; The



<P>guns, stolid and undaunted, spoke with dogged



<P>valor.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The precise gunners were coolly enthusiastic.



<P>They lifted their eyes every chance to the smoke-



<P>wreathed hillock from whence the hostile battery



<P>addressed them.&nbsp; The youth pitied them as he



<P>ran.&nbsp; Methodical idiots!&nbsp; Machine-like fools!&nbsp; The



<P>refined joy of planting shells in the midst of the



<P>other battery's formation would appear a little



<P>thing when the infantry came swooping out of



<P>the woods.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The face of a youthful rider, who was jerking



<P>his frantic horse with an abandon of temper



<P>he might display in a placid barnyard, was im-



<P>pressed deeply upon his mind.&nbsp; He knew that



<P>he looked upon a man who would presently be



<P>dead.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Too, he felt a pity for the guns, standing, six



<P>good comrades, in a bold row.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He saw a brigade going to the relief of its pes-



<P>tered fellows.&nbsp; He scrambled upon a wee hill and



<P>watched it sweeping finely, keeping formation in



<P>difficult places.&nbsp; The blue of the line was crusted



<P>with steel color, and the brilliant flags projected.



<P>Officers were shouting.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>This sight also filled him with wonder.&nbsp; The



<P>brigade was hurrying briskly to be gulped into



<P>the infernal mouths of the war god.&nbsp; What man-



<P>ner of men were they, anyhow?&nbsp; Ah, it was some



<P>wondrous breed!&nbsp; Or else they didn't compre-



<P>hend--the fools.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A furious order caused commotion in the artil-



<P>lery.&nbsp; An officer on a bounding horse made mani-



<P>acal motions with his arms.&nbsp; The teams went



<P>swinging up from the rear, the guns were whirled



<P>about, and the battery scampered away.&nbsp; The



<P>cannon with their noses poked slantingly at the



<P>ground grunted and grumbled like stout men,



<P>brave but with objections to hurry.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth went on, moderating his pace since



<P>he had left the place of noises.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Later he came upon a general of division



<P>seated upon a horse that pricked its ears in



<P>an interested way at the battle.&nbsp; There was a



<P>great gleaming of yellow and patent leather



<P>about the saddle and bridle.&nbsp; The quiet man



<P>astride looked mouse-colored upon such a splen-



<P>did charger.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A jingling staff was galloping hither and



<P>thither.&nbsp; Sometimes the general was surrounded



<P>by horsemen and at other times he was quite



<P>alone.&nbsp; He looked to be much harassed.&nbsp; He had



<P>the appearance of a business man whose market



<P>is swinging up and down.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth went slinking around this spot.



<P>He went as near as he dared trying to overhear



<P>words.&nbsp; Perhaps the general, unable to compre-



<P>hend chaos, might call upon him for information.



<P>And he could tell him.&nbsp; He knew all concerning



<P>it.&nbsp; Of a surety the force was in a fix, and any



<P>fool could see that if they did not retreat while



<P>they had opportunity--why--

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He felt that he would like to thrash the gen-



<P>eral, or at least approach and tell him in plain



<P>words exactly what he thought him to be.&nbsp; It



<P>was criminal to stay calmly in one spot and make



<P>no effort to stay destruction.&nbsp; He loitered in a



<P>fever of eagerness for the division commander to



<P>apply to him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As he warily moved about, he heard the gen-



<P>eral call out irritably: "Tompkins, go over an'



<P>see Taylor, an' tell him not t' be in such an all-



<P>fired hurry; tell him t' halt his brigade in th'



<P>edge of th' woods; tell him t' detach a reg'ment



<P>--say I think th' center 'll break if we don't help



<P>it out some; tell him t' hurry up."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A slim youth on a fine chestnut horse caught



<P>these swift words from the mouth of his superior.



<P>He made his horse bound into a gallop almost



<P>from a walk in his haste to go upon his mission.



<P>There was a cloud of dust.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A moment later the youth saw the general



<P>bounce excitedly in his saddle.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes, by heavens, they have!"&nbsp; The officer



<P>leaned forward.&nbsp; His face was aflame with excite-



<P>ment.&nbsp; "Yes, by heavens, they 've held 'im!



<P>They 've held 'im!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He began to blithely roar at his staff: "We 'll



<P>wallop 'im now.&nbsp; We 'll wallop 'im now.&nbsp; We 've



<P>got 'em sure."&nbsp; He turned suddenly upon an aid:



<P>"Here--you--Jones--quick--ride after Tompkins



<P>--see Taylor--tell him t' go in--everlastingly--



<P>like blazes--anything."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As another officer sped his horse after the first



<P>messenger, the general beamed upon the earth



<P>like a sun.&nbsp; In his eyes was a desire to chant a



<P>paean.&nbsp; He kept repeating, "They 've held 'em,



<P>by heavens!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His excitement made his horse plunge, and he



<P>merrily kicked and swore at it.&nbsp; He held a little



<P>carnival of joy on horseback.



<P>

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">
