   <TITLE>chapter12</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XII.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>THE column that had butted stoutly at the



<P>obstacles in the roadway was barely out of the



<P>youth's sight before he saw dark waves of men



<P>come sweeping out of the woods and down



<P>through the fields.&nbsp; He knew at once that the



<P>steel fibers had been washed from their hearts.



<P>They were bursting from their coats and



<P>their equipments as from entanglements.&nbsp; They



<P>charged down upon him like terrified buffaloes.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Behind them blue smoke curled and clouded



<P>above the treetops, and through the thickets he



<P>could sometimes see a distant pink glare.&nbsp; The



<P>voices of the cannon were clamoring in intermi-



<P>nable chorus.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth was horrorstricken.&nbsp; He stared



<P>in agony and amazement.&nbsp; He forgot that he



<P>was engaged in combating the universe.&nbsp; He



<P>threw aside his mental pamphlets on the philoso-



<P>phy of the retreated and rules for the guidance



<P>of the damned.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>118

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The fight was lost.&nbsp; The dragons were com-



<P>ing with invincible strides.&nbsp; The army, helpless



<P>in the matted thickets and blinded by the over-



<P>hanging night, was going to be swallowed.&nbsp; War,



<P>the red animal, war, the blood-swollen god, would



<P>have bloated fill.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Within him something bade to cry out.&nbsp; He



<P>had the impulse to make a rallying speech, to sing



<P>a battle hymn, but he could only get his tongue to



<P>call into the air: "Why--why--what--what 's



<P>th' matter?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Soon he was in the midst of them.&nbsp; They



<P>were leaping and scampering all about him.



<P>Their blanched faces shone in the dusk.&nbsp; They



<P>seemed, for the most part, to be very burly men.



<P>The youth turned from one to another of them as



<P>they galloped along.&nbsp; His incoherent questions



<P>were lost.&nbsp; They were heedless of his appeals.



<P>They did not seem to see him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They sometimes gabbled insanely.&nbsp; One huge



<P>man was asking of the sky: "Say, where de



<P>plank road?&nbsp; Where de plank road!"&nbsp; It was as if



<P>he had lost a child.&nbsp; He wept in his pain and



<P>dismay.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Presently, men were running hither and



<P>thither in all ways.&nbsp; The artillery booming,



<P>forward, rearward, and on the flanks made



<P>jumble of ideas of direction.&nbsp; Landmarks had



<P>vanished into the gathered gloom.&nbsp; The youth



<P>began to imagine that he had got into the



<P>center of the tremendous quarrel, and he could



<P>perceive no way out of it.&nbsp; From the mouths of



<P>the fleeing men came a thousand wild questions,



<P>but no one made answers.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth, after rushing about and throwing



<P>interrogations at the heedless bands of retreating



<P>infantry, finally clutched a man by the arm.&nbsp; They



<P>swung around face to face.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Why--why--" stammered the youth strug-



<P>gling with his balking tongue.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The man screamed: "Let go me!&nbsp; Let go



<P>me!"&nbsp; His face was livid and his eyes were roll-



<P>ing uncontrolled.&nbsp; He was heaving and panting.



<P>He still grasped his rifle, perhaps having for-



<P>gotten to release his hold upon it.&nbsp; He tugged



<P>frantically, and the youth being compelled to lean



<P>forward was dragged several paces.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Let go me!&nbsp; Let go me!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Why--why--" stuttered the youth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, then!" bawled the man in a lurid



<P>rage.&nbsp; He adroitly and fiercely swung his rifle.



<P>It crushed upon the youth's head.&nbsp; The man



<P>ran on.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth's fingers had turned to paste upon



<P>the other's arm.&nbsp; The energy was smitten from



<P>his muscles.&nbsp; He saw the flaming wings of light-



<P>ning flash before his vision.&nbsp; There was a deaf-



<P>ening rumble of thunder within his head.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Suddenly his legs seemed to die.&nbsp; He sank



<P>writhing to the ground.&nbsp; He tried to arise.&nbsp; In



<P>his efforts against the numbing pain he was like a



<P>man wrestling with a creature of the air.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was a sinister struggle.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Sometimes he would achieve a position half



<P>erect, battle with the air for a moment, and



<P>then fall again, grabbing at the grass.&nbsp; His face



<P>was of a clammy pallor.&nbsp; Deep groans were



<P>wrenched from him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At last, with a twisting movement, he got



<P>upon his hands and knees, and from thence, like a



<P>babe trying to walk, to his feet.&nbsp; Pressing his



<P>hands to his temples he went lurching over the



<P>grass.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He fought an intense battle with his body.



<P>His dulled senses wished him to swoon and he



<P>opposed them stubbornly, his mind portraying



<P>unknown dangers and mutilations if he should



<P>fall upon the field.&nbsp; He went tall soldier fashion.



<P>He imagined secluded spots where he could fall



<P>and be unmolested.&nbsp; To search for one he strove



<P>against the tide of his pain.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Once he put his hand to the top of his head



<P>and timidly touched the wound.&nbsp; The scratching



<P>pain of the contact made him draw a long breath



<P>through his clinched teeth.&nbsp; His fingers were



<P>dabbled with blood.&nbsp; He regarded them with a



<P>fixed stare.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Around him he could hear the grumble of



<P>jolted cannon as the scurrying horses were lashed



<P>toward the front.&nbsp; Once, a young officer on a



<P>besplashed charger nearly ran him down.&nbsp; He



<P>turned and watched the mass of guns, men, and



<P>horses sweeping in a wide curve toward a gap in



<P>a fence.&nbsp; The officer was making excited motions



<P>with a gauntleted hand.&nbsp; The guns followed the



<P>teams with an air of unwillingness, of being



<P>dragged by the heels.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Some officers of the scattered infantry were



<P>cursing and railing like fishwives.&nbsp; Their scold-



<P>ing voices could be heard above the din.&nbsp; Into



<P>the unspeakable jumble in the roadway rode a



<P>squadron of cavalry.&nbsp; The faded yellow of their



<P>facings shone bravely.&nbsp; There was a mighty



<P>altercation.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The artillery were assembling as if for a con-



<P>ference.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The blue haze of evening was upon the field.



<P>The lines of forest were long purple shadows.



<P>One cloud lay along the western sky partly



<P>smothering the red.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As the youth left the scene behind him, he



<P>heard the guns suddenly roar out.&nbsp; He imagined



<P>them shaking in black rage.&nbsp; They belched and



<P>howled like brass devils guarding a gate.&nbsp; The



<P>soft air was filled with the tremendous remon-



<P>strance.&nbsp; With it came the shattering peal of



<P>opposing infantry.&nbsp; Turning to look behind him,



<P>he could see sheets of orange light illumine the



<P>shadowy distance.&nbsp; There were subtle and sudden



<P>lightnings in the far air.&nbsp; At times he thought he



<P>could see heaving masses of men.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He hurried on in the dusk.&nbsp; The day had



<P>faded until he could barely distinguish place for



<P>his feet.&nbsp; The purple darkness was filled with



<P>men who lectured and jabbered.&nbsp; Sometimes he



<P>could see them gesticulating against the blue and



<P>somber sky.&nbsp; There seemed to be a great ruck of



<P>men and munitions spread about in the forest and



<P>in the fields.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The little narrow roadway now lay lifeless.



<P>There were overturned wagons like sun-dried



<P>bowlders.&nbsp; The bed of the former torrent was



<P>choked with the bodies of horses and splintered



<P>parts of war machines.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It had come to pass that his wound pained him



<P>but little.&nbsp; He was afraid to move rapidly, how-



<P>ever, for a dread of disturbing it.&nbsp; He held his



<P>head very still and took many precautions against



<P>stumbling.&nbsp; He was filled with anxiety, and his



<P>face was pinched and drawn in anticipation of the



<P>pain of any sudden mistake of his feet in the



<P>gloom.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His thoughts, as he walked, fixed intently



<P>upon his hurt.&nbsp; There was a cool, liquid feeling



<P>about it and he imagined blood moving slowly



<P>down under his hair.&nbsp; His head seemed swollen



<P>to a size that made him think his neck to be



<P>inadequate.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The new silence of his wound made much



<P>worriment.&nbsp; The little blistering voices of pain



<P>that had called out from his scalp were, he



<P>thought, definite in their expression of danger.



<P>By them he believed that he could measure his



<P>plight.&nbsp; But when they remained ominously



<P>silent he became frightened and imagined ter-



<P>rible fingers that clutched into his brain.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Amid it he began to reflect upon various



<P>incidents and conditions of the past.&nbsp; He be-



<P>thought him of certain meals his mother had



<P>cooked at home, in which those dishes of which



<P>he was particularly fond had occupied prominent



<P>positions.&nbsp; He saw the spread table.&nbsp; The pine



<P>walls of the kitchen were glowing in the warm



<P>light from the stove.&nbsp; Too, he remembered how



<P>he and his companions used to go from the school-



<P>house to the bank of a shaded pool.&nbsp; He saw his



<P>clothes in disorderly array upon the grass of the



<P>bank.&nbsp; He felt the swash of the fragrant water



<P>upon his body.&nbsp; The leaves of the overhanging



<P>maple rustled with melody in the wind of youth-



<P>ful summer.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He was overcome presently by a dragging



<P>weariness.&nbsp; His head hung forward and his



<P>shoulders were stooped as if he were bearing a



<P>great bundle.&nbsp; His feet shuffled along the



<P>ground.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He held continuous arguments as to whether



<P>he should lie down and sleep at some near spot,



<P>or force himself on until he reached a certain



<P>haven.&nbsp; He often tried to dismiss the question,



<P>but his body persisted in rebellion and his senses



<P>nagged at him like pampered babies.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At last he heard a cheery voice near his



<P>shoulder: "Yeh seem t' be in a pretty bad way,



<P>boy?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth did not look up, but he assented



<P>with thick tongue.&nbsp; "Uh!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The owner of the cheery voice took him firmly



<P>by the arm.&nbsp; "Well," he said, with a round



<P>laugh, "I'm goin' your way.&nbsp; Th' hull gang is



<P>goin' your way.&nbsp; An' I guess I kin give yeh a



<P>lift."&nbsp; They began to walk like a drunken man



<P>and his friend.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As they went along, the man questioned the



<P>youth and assisted him with the replies like one



<P>manipulating the mind of a child.&nbsp; Sometimes he



<P>interjected anecdotes.&nbsp; "What reg'ment do yeh



<P>b'long teh?&nbsp; Eh?&nbsp; What's that?&nbsp; Th' 304th N'



<P>York?&nbsp; Why, what corps is that in?&nbsp; Oh, it is?



<P>Why, I thought they wasn't engaged t'-day--



<P>they 're 'way over in th' center.&nbsp; Oh, they was,



<P>eh?&nbsp; Well, pretty nearly everybody got their



<P>share 'a fightin' t'-day.&nbsp; By dad, I give myself up



<P>fer dead any number 'a times.&nbsp; There was shootin'



<P>here an' shootin' there, an' hollerin' here an'



<P>hollerin' there, in th' damn' darkness, until I



<P>couldn't tell t' save m' soul which side I was on.



<P>Sometimes I thought I was sure 'nough from



<P>Ohier, an' other times I could 'a swore I was



<P>from th' bitter end of Florida.&nbsp; It was th' most



<P>mixed up dern thing I ever see.&nbsp; An' these here



<P>hull woods is a reg'lar mess.&nbsp; It'll be a miracle



<P>if we find our reg'ments t'-night.&nbsp; Pretty soon,



<P>though, we 'll meet a-plenty of guards an' provost-



<P>guards, an' one thing an' another.&nbsp; Ho! there they



<P>go with an off'cer, I guess.&nbsp; Look at his hand



<P>a-draggin'.&nbsp; He 's got all th' war he wants, I bet.



<P>He won't be talkin' so big about his reputation



<P>an' all when they go t' sawin' off his leg.&nbsp; Poor



<P>feller!&nbsp; My brother 's got whiskers jest like that.



<P>How did yeh git 'way over here, anyhow?&nbsp; Your



<P>reg'ment is a long way from here, ain't it?&nbsp; Well,



<P>I guess we can find it.&nbsp; Yeh know there was a



<P>boy killed in my comp'ny t'-day that I thought



<P>th' world an' all of.&nbsp; Jack was a nice feller.&nbsp; By



<P>ginger, it hurt like thunder t' see ol' Jack jest git



<P>knocked flat.&nbsp; We was a-standin' purty peaceable



<P>fer a spell, 'though there was men runnin' ev'ry



<P>way all 'round us, an' while we was a-standin'



<P>like that, 'long come a big fat feller.&nbsp; He began



<P>t' peck at Jack's elbow, an' he ses: 'Say, where 's



<P>th' road t' th' river?'&nbsp; An' Jack, he never paid no



<P>attention, an' th' feller kept on a-peckin' at his



<P>elbow an' sayin': 'Say, where 's th' road t' th'



<P>river?'&nbsp; Jack was a-lookin' ahead all th' time



<P>tryin' t' see th' Johnnies comin' through th'



<P>woods, an' he never paid no attention t' this big



<P>fat feller fer a long time, but at last he turned



<P>'round an' he ses: 'Ah, go t' hell an' find th'



<P>road t' th' river!'&nbsp; An' jest then a shot slapped



<P>him bang on th' side th' head.&nbsp; He was a sergeant,



<P>too.&nbsp; Them was his last words.&nbsp; Thunder, I wish



<P>we was sure 'a findin' our reg'ments t'-night.&nbsp; It 's



<P>goin' t' be long huntin'.&nbsp; But I guess we kin



<P>do it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In the search which followed, the man of the



<P>cheery voice seemed to the youth to possess a



<P>wand of a magic kind.&nbsp; He threaded the mazes



<P>of the tangled forest with a strange fortune.&nbsp; In



<P>encounters with guards and patrols he displayed



<P>the keenness of a detective and the valor of a



<P>gamin.&nbsp; Obstacles fell before him and became of



<P>assistance.&nbsp; The youth, with his chin still on his



<P>breast, stood woodenly by while his companion



<P>beat ways and means out of sullen things.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The forest seemed a vast hive of men buzzing



<P>about in frantic circles, but the cheery man con-



<P>ducted the youth without mistakes, until at last



<P>he began to chuckle with glee and self-satisfaction.



<P>"Ah, there yeh are!&nbsp; See that fire?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth nodded stupidly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, there 's where your reg'ment is.&nbsp; An'



<P>now, good-by, ol' boy, good luck t' yeh."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A warm and strong hand clasped the youth's



<P>languid fingers for an instant, and then he heard



<P>a cheerful and audacious whistling as the man



<P>strode away.&nbsp; As he who had so befriended him



<P>was thus passing out of his life, it suddenly oc-



<P>curred to the youth that he had not once seen his



<P>face.



<P>

<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER IV.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>THE brigade was halted in the fringe of a



<P>grove.&nbsp; The men crouched among the trees and



<P>pointed their restless guns out at the fields.



<P>They tried to look beyond the smoke.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Out of this haze they could see running men.



<P>Some shouted information and gestured as they



<P>hurried.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The men of the new regiment watched and



<P>listened eagerly, while their tongues ran on in



<P>gossip of the battle.&nbsp; They mouthed rumors that



<P>had flown like birds out of the unknown.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"They say Perry has been driven in with big



<P>loss."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes, Carrott went t' th' hospital.&nbsp; He said he



<P>was sick.&nbsp; That smart lieutenant is commanding



<P>'G' Company.&nbsp; Th' boys say they won't be



<P>under Carrott no more if they all have t' desert.



<P>They allus knew he was a--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Hannises' batt'ry is took."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"It ain't either.&nbsp; I saw Hannises' batt'ry off on



<P>th' left not more'n fifteen minutes ago."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>47

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Th' general, he ses he is goin' t' take th' hull



<P>cammand of th' 304th when we go inteh action,



<P>an' then he ses we'll do sech fightin' as never



<P>another one reg'ment done."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"They say we're catchin' it over on th' left.



<P>They say th' enemy driv' our line inteh a devil of



<P>a swamp an' took Hannises' batt'ry."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No sech thing.&nbsp; Hannises' batt'ry was 'long



<P>here 'bout a minute ago."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"That young Hasbrouck, he makes a good



<P>off'cer.&nbsp; He ain't afraid 'a nothin'."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I met one of th' 148th Maine boys an' he ses



<P>his brigade fit th' hull rebel army fer four hours



<P>over on th' turnpike road an' killed about five



<P>thousand of 'em.&nbsp; He ses one more sech fight as



<P>that an' th' war 'll be over."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Bill wasn't scared either.&nbsp; No, sir!&nbsp; It wasn't



<P>that.&nbsp; Bill ain't a-gittin' scared easy.&nbsp; He was



<P>jest mad, that's what he was.&nbsp; When that feller



<P>trod on his hand, he up an' sed that he was willin'



<P>t' give his hand t' his country, but he be dumbed



<P>if he was goin' t' have every dumb bushwhacker



<P>in th' kentry walkin' 'round on it.&nbsp; Se he went t'



<P>th' hospital disregardless of th' fight.&nbsp; Three



<P>fingers was crunched.&nbsp; Th' dern doctor wanted



<P>t' amputate 'm, an' Bill, he raised a heluva row, I



<P>hear.&nbsp; He's a funny feller."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The din in front swelled to a tremendous



<P>chorus.&nbsp; The youth and his fellows were frozen



<P>to silence.&nbsp; They could see a flag that tossed in



<P>the smoke angrily.&nbsp; Near it were the blurred and



<P>agitated forms of troops.&nbsp; There came a turbulent



<P>stream of men across the fields.&nbsp; A battery chang-



<P>ing position at a frantic gallop scattered the



<P>stragglers right and left.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A shell screaming like a storm banshee went



<P>over the huddled heads of the reserves.&nbsp; It landed



<P>in the grove, and exploding redly flung the brown



<P>earth.&nbsp; There was a little shower of pine needles.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Bullets began to whistle among the branches



<P>and nip at the trees.&nbsp; Twigs and leaves came



<P>sailing down.&nbsp; It was as if a thousand axes, wee



<P>and invisible, were being wielded.&nbsp; Many of the



<P>men were constantly dodging and ducking their



<P>heads.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The lieutenant of the youth's company was



<P>shot in the hand.&nbsp; He began to swear so won-



<P>drously that a nervous laugh went along the regi-



<P>mental line.&nbsp; The officer's profanity sounded



<P>conventional.&nbsp; It relieved the tightened senses of



<P>the new men.&nbsp; It was as if he had hit his fingers



<P>with a tack hammer at home.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He held the wounded member carefully away



<P>from his side so that the blood would not drip



<P>upon his trousers.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The captain of the company, tucking his sword



<P>under his arm, produced a handkerchief and



<P>began to bind with it the lieutenant's wound.



<P>And they disputed as to how the binding should



<P>be done.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The battle flag in the distance jerked about



<P>madly.&nbsp; It seemed to be struggling to free itself



<P>from an agony.&nbsp; The billowing smoke was filled



<P>with horizontal flashes.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Men running swiftly emerged from it.&nbsp; They



<P>grew in numbers until it was seen that the whole



<P>command was fleeing.&nbsp; The flag suddenly sank



<P>down as if dying.&nbsp; Its motion as it fell was a



<P>gesture of despair.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Wild yells came from behind the walls of



<P>smoke.&nbsp; A sketch in gray and red dissolved into



<P>a moblike body of men who galloped like wild



<P>horses.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The veteran regiments on the right and left of



<P>the 304th immediately began to jeer.&nbsp; With the



<P>passionate song of the bullets and the banshee



<P>shrieks of shells were mingled loud catcalls and



<P>bits of facetious advice concerning places of safety.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But the new regiment was breathless with hor-



<P>ror.&nbsp; "Gawd!&nbsp; Saunders's got crushed!" whis-



<P>pered the man at the youth's elbow.&nbsp; They



<P>shrank back and crouched as if compelled to



<P>await a flood.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth shot a swift glance along the blue



<P>ranks of the regiment.&nbsp; The profiles were motion-



<P>less, carven; and afterward he remembered that



<P>the color sergeant was standing with his legs



<P>apart, as if he expected to be pushed to the



<P>ground.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The following throng went whirling around



<P>the flank.&nbsp; Here and there were officers carried



<P>along on the stream like exasperated chips.&nbsp; They



<P>were striking about them with their swords



<P>and with their left fists, punching every head



<P>they could reach.&nbsp; They cursed like highway-



<P>men.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A mounted officer displayed the furious anger



<P>of a spoiled child.&nbsp; He raged with his head, his



<P>arms, and his legs.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Another, the commander of the brigade, was



<P>galloping about bawling.&nbsp; His hat was gone and



<P>his clothes were awry.&nbsp; He resembled a man



<P>who has come from bed to go to a fire.&nbsp; The



<P>hoofs of his horse often threatened the heads of



<P>the running men, but they scampered with sin-



<P>gular fortune.&nbsp; In this rush they were apparently



<P>all deaf and blind.&nbsp; They heeded not the largest



<P>and longest of the oaths that were thrown at



<P>them from all directions.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Frequently over this tumult could be heard



<P>the grim jokes of the critical veterans; but the



<P>retreating men apparently were not even con-



<P>scious of the presence of an audience.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The battle reflection that shone for an instant



<P>in the faces on the mad current made the youth



<P>feel that forceful hands from heaven would not



<P>have been able to have held him in place if he



<P>could have got intelligent control of his legs.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was an appalling imprint upon these



<P>faces.&nbsp; The struggle in the smoke had pictured



<P>an exaggeration of itself on the bleached cheeks



<P>and in the eyes wild with one desire.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The sight of this stampede exerted a floodlike



<P>force that seemed able to drag sticks and stones



<P>and men from the ground.&nbsp; They of the reserves



<P>had to hold on.&nbsp; They grew pale and firm, and



<P>red and quaking.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth achieved one little thought in the



<P>midst of this chaos.&nbsp; The composite monster



<P>which had caused the other troops to flee had



<P>not then appeared.&nbsp; He resolved to get a view



<P>of it, and then, he thought he might very likely



<P>run better than the best of them.



<P>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER X.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>THE tattered man stood musing.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, he was reg'lar jim-dandy fer nerve,



<P>wa'n't he," said he finally in a little awestruck



<P>voice.&nbsp; "A reg'lar jim-dandy."&nbsp; He thoughtfully



<P>poked one of the docile hands with his foot.&nbsp; "I



<P>wonner where he got 'is stren'th from?&nbsp; I never



<P>seen a man do like that before.&nbsp; It was a funny



<P>thing.&nbsp; Well, he was a reg'lar jim-dandy."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth desired to screech out his grief.



<P>He was stabbed, but his tongue lay dead in the



<P>tomb of his mouth.&nbsp; He threw himself again



<P>upon the ground and began to brood.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The tattered man stood musing.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Look-a-here, pardner," he said, after a time.



<P>He regarded the corpse as he spoke.&nbsp; "He 's up



<P>an' gone, ain't 'e, an' we might as well begin t'



<P>look out fer ol' number one.&nbsp; This here thing is



<P>all over.&nbsp; He 's up an' gone, ain't 'e?&nbsp; An' he 's all



<P>right here.&nbsp; Nobody won't bother 'im.&nbsp; An' I



<P>must say I ain't enjoying any great health m'self



<P>these days."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth, awakened by the tattered soldier's



<P>tone, looked quickly up.&nbsp; He saw that he was



<P>swinging uncertainly on his legs and that his face



<P>had turned to a shade of blue.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Good Lord!" he cried, "you ain't goin' t'--



<P>not you, too."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The tattered man waved his hand.&nbsp; "Nary



<P>die," he said.&nbsp; "All I want is some pea soup an'



<P>a good bed.&nbsp; Some pea soup," he repeated



<P>dreamfully.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth arose from the ground.&nbsp; "I wonder



<P>where he came from.&nbsp; I left him over there."



<P>He pointed.&nbsp; "And now I find 'im here.&nbsp; And



<P>he was coming from over there, too."&nbsp; He in-



<P>dicated a new direction.&nbsp; They both turned



<P>toward the body as if to ask of it a question.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well," at length spoke the tattered man,



<P>"there ain't no use in our stayin' here an' tryin' t'



<P>ask him anything."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth nodded an assent wearily.&nbsp; They



<P>both turned to gaze for a moment at the corpse.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth murmured something.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, he was a jim-dandy, wa'n't 'e?" said



<P>the tattered man as if in response.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They turned their backs upon it and started



<P>away.&nbsp; For a time they stole softly, treading



<P>with their toes.&nbsp; It remained laughing there in



<P>the grass.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I'm commencin' t' feel pretty bad," said the



<P>tattered man, suddenly breaking one of his little



<P>silences.&nbsp; "I'm commencin' t' feel pretty damn'



<P>bad."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth groaned.&nbsp; "O Lord!"&nbsp; He won-



<P>dered if he was to be the tortured witness of



<P>another grim encounter.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But his companion waved his hand reassur-



<P>ingly.&nbsp; "Oh, I'm not goin' t' die yit!&nbsp; There too



<P>much dependin' on me fer me t' die yit.&nbsp; No, sir!



<P>Nary die!&nbsp; I CAN'T!&nbsp; Ye'd oughta see th' swad



<P>a' chil'ren I've got, an' all like that."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth glancing at his companion could



<P>see by the shadow of a smile that he was making



<P>some kind of fun.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As they plodded on the tattered soldier con-



<P>tinued to talk.&nbsp; "Besides, if I died, I wouldn't



<P>die th' way that feller did.&nbsp; That was th' funniest



<P>thing.&nbsp; I'd jest flop down, I would.&nbsp; I never seen



<P>a feller die th' way that feller did.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yeh know Tom Jamison, he lives next door



<P>t' me up home.&nbsp; He's a nice feller, he is, an' we



<P>was allus good friends.&nbsp; Smart, too.&nbsp; Smart as a



<P>steel trap.&nbsp; Well, when we was a-fightin' this



<P>atternoon, all-of-a-sudden he begin t' rip up an'



<P>cuss an' beller at me.&nbsp; 'Yer shot, yeh blamed



<P>infernal!'--he swear horrible--he ses t' me.&nbsp; I



<P>put up m' hand t' m' head an' when I looked at



<P>m' fingers, I seen, sure 'nough, I was shot.&nbsp; I



<P>give a holler an' begin t' run, but b'fore I could



<P>git away another one hit me in th' arm an' whirl'



<P>me clean 'round.&nbsp; I got skeared when they was



<P>all a-shootin' b'hind me an' I run t' beat all,



<P>but I cotch it pretty bad.&nbsp; I've an idee I'd



<P>a' been fightin' yit, if t'was n't fer Tom Jami-



<P>son."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Then he made a calm announcement: "There's



<P>two of 'em--little ones--but they 're beginnin' t'



<P>have fun with me now.&nbsp; I don't b'lieve I kin walk



<P>much furder."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They went slowly on in silence.&nbsp; "Yeh look



<P>pretty peek-ed yerself," said the tattered man at



<P>last.&nbsp; "I bet yeh 've got a worser one than yeh



<P>think.&nbsp; Ye'd better take keer of yer hurt.&nbsp; It



<P>don't do t' let sech things go.&nbsp; It might be inside



<P>mostly, an' them plays thunder.&nbsp; Where is it



<P>located?"&nbsp; But he continued his harangue with-



<P>out waiting for a reply.&nbsp; "I see 'a feller git hit



<P>plum in th' head when my reg'ment was a-standin'



<P>at ease onct.&nbsp; An' everybody yelled out to 'im:



<P>Hurt, John?&nbsp; Are yeh hurt much?&nbsp; 'No," ses he.



<P>He looked kinder surprised, an' he went on tellin'



<P>'em how he felt.&nbsp; He sed he didn't feel nothin'.



<P>But, by dad, th' first thing that feller knowed he



<P>was dead.&nbsp; Yes, he was dead--stone dead.&nbsp; So,



<P>yeh wanta watch out.&nbsp; Yeh might have some



<P>queer kind 'a hurt yerself.&nbsp; Yeh can't never tell.



<P>Where is your'n located?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth had been wriggling since the intro-



<P>duction of this topic.&nbsp; He now gave a cry of ex-



<P>asperation and made a furious motion with his



<P>hand.&nbsp; "Oh, don't bother me!" he said.&nbsp; He was



<P>enraged against the tattered man, and could have



<P>strangled him.&nbsp; His companions seemed ever to



<P>play intolerable parts.&nbsp; They were ever uprais-



<P>ing the ghost of shame on the stick of their



<P>curiosity.&nbsp; He turned toward the tattered man as



<P>one at bay.&nbsp; "Now, don't bother me," he re-



<P>peated with desperate menace.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, Lord knows I don't wanta bother any-



<P>body," said the other.&nbsp; There was a little accent



<P>of despair in his voice as he replied, "Lord



<P>knows I 've gota 'nough m' own t' tend to."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth, who had been holding a bitter de-



<P>bate with himself and casting glances of hatred



<P>and contempt at the tattered man, here spoke in



<P>a hard voice.&nbsp; "Good-by," he said.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The tattered man looked at him in gaping



<P>amazement.&nbsp; "Why--why, pardner, where yeh



<P>goin'?" he asked unsteadily.&nbsp; The youth looking



<P>at him, could see that he, too, like that other one,



<P>was beginning to act dumb and animal-like.&nbsp; His



<P>thoughts seemed to be floundering about in his



<P>head.&nbsp; "Now--now--look--a--here, you Tom



<P>Jamison--now--I won't have this--this here



<P>won't do.&nbsp; Where--where yeh goin'?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth pointed vaguely.&nbsp; "Over there,"



<P>he replied.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, now look--a--here--now," said the



<P>tattered man, rambling on in idiot fashion.&nbsp; His



<P>head was hanging forward and his words were



<P>slurred.&nbsp; "This thing won't do, now, Tom Jami-



<P>son.&nbsp; It won't do.&nbsp; I know yeh, yeh pig-headed



<P>devil.&nbsp; Yeh wanta go trompin' off with a bad



<P>hurt.&nbsp; It ain't right--now--Tom Jamison--it ain't.



<P>Yeh wanta leave me take keer of yeh, Tom Jami-



<P>son.&nbsp; It ain't--right--it ain't--fer yeh t' go--



<P>trompin' off--with a bad hurt--it ain't--ain't--



<P>ain't right--it ain't."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In reply the youth climbed a fence and



<P>started away.&nbsp; He could hear the tattered man



<P>bleating plaintively.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Once he faced about angrily.&nbsp; "What?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Look--a--here, now, Tom Jamison--now--



<P>it ain't--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth went on.&nbsp; Turning at a distance he



<P>saw the tattered man wandering about helplessly



<P>in the field.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He now thought that he wished he was dead.



<P>He believed that he envied those men whose



<P>bodies lay strewn over the grass of the fields and



<P>on the fallen leaves of the forest.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The simple questions of the tattered man had



<P>been knife thrusts to him.&nbsp; They asserted a



<P>society that probes pitilessly at secrets until all is



<P>apparent.&nbsp; His late companion's chance persist-



<P>ency made him feel that he could not keep his



<P>crime concealed in his bosom.&nbsp; It was sure to be



<P>brought plain by one of those arrows which



<P>cloud the air and are constantly pricking, dis-



<P>covering, proclaiming those things which are



<P>willed to be forever hidden.&nbsp; He admitted that



<P>he could not defend himself against this agency.



<P>It was not within the power of vigilance.



<P>

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter11</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XI.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>HE became aware that the furnace roar of the



<P>battle was growing louder.&nbsp; Great brown clouds



<P>had floated to the still heights of air before him.



<P>The noise, too, was approaching.&nbsp; The woods



<P>filtered men and the fields became dotted.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As he rounded a hillock, he perceived that the



<P>roadway was now a crying mass of wagons,



<P>teams, and men.&nbsp; From the heaving tangle issued



<P>exhortations, commands, imprecations.&nbsp; Fear was



<P>sweeping it all along.&nbsp; The cracking whips bit



<P>and horses plunged and tugged.&nbsp; The white-



<P>topped wagons strained and stumbled in their



<P>exertions like fat sheep.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth felt comforted in a measure by this



<P>sight.&nbsp; They were all retreating.&nbsp; Perhaps, then,



<P>he was not so bad after all.&nbsp; He seated himself



<P>and watched the terror-stricken wagons.&nbsp; They



<P>fled like soft, ungainly animals.&nbsp; All the roarers



<P>and lashers served to help him to magnify the



<P>dangers and horrors of the engagement that he



<P>might try to prove to himself that the thing with



<P>which men could charge him was in truth a



<P>symmetrical act.&nbsp; There was an amount of pleas-



<P>ure to him in watching the wild march of this



<P>vindication.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Presently the calm head of a forward-going



<P>column of infantry appeared in the road.&nbsp; It



<P>came swiftly on.&nbsp; Avoiding the obstructions gave



<P>it the sinuous movement of a serpent.&nbsp; The men



<P>at the head butted mules with their musket



<P>stocks.&nbsp; They prodded teamsters indifferent to



<P>all howls.&nbsp; The men forced their way through



<P>parts of the dense mass by strength.&nbsp; The blunt



<P>head of the column pushed.&nbsp; The raving team-



<P>sters swore many strange oaths.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The commands to make way had the ring of a



<P>great importance in them.&nbsp; The men were going



<P>forward to the heart of the din.&nbsp; They were to



<P>confront the eager rush of the enemy.&nbsp; They felt



<P>the pride of their onward movement when the



<P>remainder of the army seemed trying to dribble



<P>down this road.&nbsp; They tumbled teams about



<P>with a fine feeling that it was no matter so long



<P>as their column got to the front in time.&nbsp; This



<P>importance made their faces grave and stern.



<P>And the backs of the officers were very rigid.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As the youth looked at them the black weight



<P>of his woe returned to him.&nbsp; He felt that he was



<P>regarding a procession of chosen beings.&nbsp; The



<P>separation was as great to him as if they had



<P>marched with weapons of flame and banners of



<P>sunlight.&nbsp; He could never be like them.&nbsp; He



<P>could have wept in his longings.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He searched about in his mind for an ade-



<P>quate malediction for the indefinite cause, the



<P>thing upon which men turn the words of final



<P>blame.&nbsp; It--whatever it was--was responsible for



<P>him, he said.&nbsp; There lay the fault.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The haste of the column to reach the battle



<P>seemed to the forlorn young man to be some-



<P>thing much finer than stout fighting.&nbsp; Heroes, he



<P>thought, could find excuses in that long seething



<P>lane.&nbsp; They could retire with perfect self-respect



<P>and make excuses to the stars.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He wondered what those men had eaten that



<P>they could be in such haste to force their way to



<P>grim chances of death.&nbsp; As he watched his envy



<P>grew until he thought that he wished to change



<P>lives with one of them.&nbsp; He would have liked to



<P>have used a tremendous force, he said, throw off



<P>himself and become a better.&nbsp; Swift pictures of



<P>himself, apart, yet in himself, came to him--a



<P>blue desperate figure leading lurid charges with



<P>one knee forward and a broken blade high--a



<P>blue, determined figure standing before a crimson



<P>and steel assault, getting calmly killed on a high



<P>place before the eyes of all.&nbsp; He thought of the



<P>magnificent pathos of his dead body.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>These thoughts uplifted him.&nbsp; He felt the



<P>quiver of war desire.&nbsp; In his ears, he heard the



<P>ring of victory.&nbsp; He knew the frenzy of a rapid



<P>successful charge.&nbsp; The music of the trampling



<P>feet, the sharp voices, the clanking arms of the



<P>column near him made him soar on the red wings



<P>of war.&nbsp; For a few moments he was sublime.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He thought that he was about to start for the



<P>front.&nbsp; Indeed, he saw a picture of himself, dust-



<P>stained, haggard, panting, flying to the front at



<P>the proper moment to seize and throttle the dark,



<P>leering witch of calamity.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Then the difficulties of the thing began to



<P>drag at him.&nbsp; He hesitated, balancing awkwardly



<P>on one foot.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He had no rifle; he could not fight with his



<P>hands, said he resentfully to his plan.&nbsp; Well,



<P>rifles could be had for the picking.&nbsp; They were



<P>extraordinarily profuse.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Also, he continued, it would be a miracle if he



<P>found his regiment.&nbsp; Well, he could fight with



<P>any regiment.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He started forward slowly.&nbsp; He stepped as if



<P>he expected to tread upon some explosive thing.



<P>Doubts and he were struggling.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He would truly be a worm if any of his com-



<P>rades should see him returning thus, the marks of



<P>his flight upon him.&nbsp; There was a reply that the



<P>intent fighters did not care for what happened



<P>rearward saving that no hostile bayonets ap-



<P>peared there.&nbsp; In the battle-blur his face would,



<P>in a way be hidden, like the face of a cowled



<P>man.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But then he said that his tireless fate would



<P>bring forth, when the strife lulled for a moment,



<P>a man to ask of him an explanation.&nbsp; In imagina-



<P>tion he felt the scrutiny of his companions as he



<P>painfully labored through some lies.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Eventually, his courage expended itself upon



<P>these objections.&nbsp; The debates drained him of his



<P>fire.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He was not cast down by this defeat of his



<P>plan, for, upon studying the affair carefully, he



<P>could not but admit that the objections were very



<P>formidable.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Furthermore, various ailments had begun to



<P>cry out.&nbsp; In their presence he could not persist



<P>in flying high with the wings of war; they



<P>rendered it almost impossible for him to see him-



<P>self in a heroic light.&nbsp; He tumbled headlong.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He discovered that he had a scorching thirst.



<P>His face was so dry and grimy that he thought



<P>he could feel his skin crackle.&nbsp; Each bone of his



<P>body had an ache in it, and seemingly threatened



<P>to break with each movement.&nbsp; His feet were



<P>like two sores.&nbsp; Also, his body was calling for



<P>food.&nbsp; It was more powerful than a direct hunger.



<P>There was a dull, weight like feeling in his stom-



<P>ach, and, when he tried to walk, his head swayed



<P>and he tottered.&nbsp; He could not see with distinct-



<P>ness.&nbsp; Small patches of green mist floated before



<P>his vision.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>While he had been tossed by many emotions,



<P>he had not been aware of ailments.&nbsp; Now they



<P>beset him and made clamor.&nbsp; As he was at last



<P>compelled to pay attention to them, his capacity



<P>for self-hate was multiplied.&nbsp; In despair, he



<P>declared that he was not like those others.&nbsp; He



<P>now conceded it to be impossible that he should



<P>ever become a hero.&nbsp; He was a craven loon.



<P>Those pictures of glory were piteous things.&nbsp; He



<P>groaned from his heart and went staggering off.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A certain mothlike quality within him kept



<P>him in the vicinity of the battle.&nbsp; He had a great



<P>desire to see, and to get news.&nbsp; He wished to



<P>know who was winning.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He told himself that, despite his unprecedented



<P>suffering, he had never lost his greed for a victory,



<P>yet, he said, in a half-apologetic manner to his



<P>conscience, he could not but know that a defeat



<P>for the army this time might mean many favor-



<P>able things for him.&nbsp; The blows of the enemy



<P>would splinter regiments into fragments.&nbsp; Thus,



<P>many men of courage, he considered, would be



<P>obliged to desert the colors and scurry like



<P>chickens.&nbsp; He would appear as one of them.



<P>They would be sullen brothers in distress, and he



<P>could then easily believe he had not run any



<P>farther or faster than they.&nbsp; And if he himself



<P>could believe in his virtuous perfection, he con-



<P>ceived that there would be small trouble in con-



<P>vincing all others.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He said, as if in excuse for this hope, that



<P>previously the army had encountered great



<P>defeats and in a few months had shaken off all



<P>blood and tradition of them, emerging as bright



<P>and valiant as a new one; thrusting out of sight



<P>the memory of disaster, and appearing with the



<P>valor and confidence of unconquered legions.



<P>The shrilling voices of the people at home would



<P>pipe dismally for a time, but various generals



<P>were usually compelled to listen to these ditties.



<P>He of course felt no compunctions for proposing



<P>a general as a sacrifice.&nbsp; He could not tell who



<P>the chosen for the barbs might be, so he could



<P>center no direct sympathy upon him.&nbsp; The



<P>people were afar and he did not conceive public



<P>opinion to be accurate at long range.&nbsp; It was



<P>quite probable they would hit the wrong man



<P>who, after he had recovered from his amazement



<P>would perhaps spend the rest of his days in writ-



<P>ing replies to the songs of his alleged failure.&nbsp; It



<P>would be very unfortunate, no doubt, but in this



<P>case a general was of no consequence to the



<P>youth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In a defeat there would be a roundabout



<P>vindication of himself.&nbsp; He thought it would



<P>prove, in a manner, that he had fled early because



<P>of his superior powers of perception.&nbsp; A serious



<P>prophet upon predicting a flood should be the



<P>first man to climb a tree.&nbsp; This would demon-



<P>strate that he was indeed a seer.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A moral vindication was regarded by the



<P>youth as a very important thing.&nbsp; Without salve,



<P>he could not, he thought, wear the sore badge of



<P>his dishonor through life.&nbsp; With his heart con-



<P>tinually assuring him that he was despicable, he



<P>could not exist without making it, through his



<P>actions, apparent to all men.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>If the army had gone gloriously on he would



<P>be lost.&nbsp; If the din meant that now his army's



<P>flags were tilted forward he was a condemned



<P>wretch.&nbsp; He would be compelled to doom



<P>himself to isolation.&nbsp; If the men were advancing,



<P>their indifferent feet were trampling upon his



<P>chances for a successful life.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As these thoughts went rapidly through his



<P>mind, he turned upon them and tried to thrust



<P>them away.&nbsp; He denounced himself as a villain.



<P>He said that he was the most unutterably selfish



<P>man in existence.&nbsp; His mind pictured the soldiers



<P>who would place their defiant bodies before the



<P>spear of the yelling battle fiend, and as he saw



<P>their dripping corpses on an imagined field, he



<P>said that he was their murderer.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Again he thought that he wished he was dead.



<P>He believed that he envied a corpse.&nbsp; Thinking



<P>of the slain, he achieved a great contempt for



<P>some of them, as if they were guilty for thus



<P>becoming lifeless.&nbsp; They might have been killed



<P>by lucky chances, he said, before they had had



<P>opportunities to flee or before they had been



<P>really tested.&nbsp; Yet they would receive laurels



<P>from tradition.&nbsp; He cried out bitterly that their



<P>crowns were stolen and their robes of glori-



<P>ous memories were shams.&nbsp; However, he still



<P>said that it was a great pity he was not as



<P>they.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A defeat of the army had suggested itself to



<P>him as a means of escape from the consequences



<P>of his fall.&nbsp; He considered, now, however, that it



<P>was useless to think of such a possibility.&nbsp; His



<P>education had been that success for that mighty



<P>blue machine was certain; that it would make



<P>victories as a contrivance turns out buttons.&nbsp; He



<P>presently discarded all his speculations in the



<P>other direction.&nbsp; He returned to the creed of



<P>soldiers.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When he perceived again that it was not



<P>possible for the army to be defeated, he tried



<P>to bethink him of a fine tale which he could take



<P>back to his regiment, and with it turn the expected



<P>shafts of derision.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But, as he mortally feared these shafts, it



<P>became impossible for him to invent a tale he felt



<P>he could trust.&nbsp; He experimented with many



<P>schemes, but threw them aside one by one as



<P>flimsy.&nbsp; He was quick to see vulnerable places in



<P>them all.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Furthermore, he was much afraid that some



<P>arrow of scorn might lay him mentally low before



<P>he could raise his protecting tale.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He imagined the whole regiment saying:



<P>"Where's Henry Fleming?&nbsp; He run, didn't 'e?



<P>Oh, my!"&nbsp; He recalled various persons who



<P>would be quite sure to leave him no peace



<P>about it.&nbsp; They would doubtless question him



<P>with sneers, and laugh at his stammering hesi-



<P>tation.&nbsp; In the next engagement they would



<P>try to keep watch of him to discover when he



<P>would run.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Wherever he went in camp, he would en-



<P>counter insolent and lingeringly cruel stares.&nbsp; As



<P>he imagined himself passing near a crowd of



<P>comrades, he could hear some one say, "There



<P>he goes!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Then, as if the heads were moved by one



<P>muscle, all the faces were turned toward him



<P>with wide, derisive grins.&nbsp; He seemed to hear



<P>some one make a humorous remark in a low tone.



<P>At it the others all crowed and cackled.&nbsp; He was



<P>a slang phrase.



<P>

</BODY>

<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <TITLE>chapter05</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER V.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>THERE were moments of waiting.&nbsp; The youth



<P>thought of the village street at home before the



<P>arrival of the circus parade on a day in the



<P>spring.&nbsp; He remembered how he had stood, a



<P>small, thrillful boy, prepared to follow the dingy



<P>lady upon the white horse, or the band in its



<P>faded chariot.&nbsp; He saw the yellow road, the



<P>lines of expectant people, and the sober houses.



<P>He particularly remembered an old fellow who



<P>used to sit upon a cracker box in front of the



<P>store and feign to despise such exhibitions.&nbsp; A



<P>thousand details of color and form surged in his



<P>mind.&nbsp; The old fellow upon the cracker box ap-



<P>peared in middle prominence.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Some one cried, "Here they come!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was rustling and muttering among the



<P>men.&nbsp; They displayed a feverish desire to have



<P>every possible cartridge ready to their hands.



<P>The boxes were pulled around into various posi-



<P>tions, and adjusted with great care.&nbsp; It was as if



<P>seven hundred new bonnets were being tried on.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The tall soldier, having prepared his rifle, pro-



<P>duced a red handkerchief of some kind.&nbsp; He was



<P>engaged in knitting it about his throat with ex-



<P>quisite attention to its position, when the cry was



<P>repeated up and down the line in a muffled roar



<P>of sound.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Here they come!&nbsp; Here they come!"&nbsp; Gun



<P>locks clicked.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Across the smoke-infested fields came a brown



<P>swarm of running men who were giving shrill



<P>yells.&nbsp; They came on, stooping and swinging



<P>their rifles at all angles.&nbsp; A flag, tilted forward,



<P>sped near the front.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As he caught sight of them the youth was



<P>momentarily startled by a thought that perhaps



<P>his gun was not loaded.&nbsp; He stood trying to



<P>rally his faltering intellect so that he might rec-



<P>ollect the moment when he had loaded, but he



<P>could not.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A hatless general pulled his dripping horse to



<P>a stand near the colonel of the 304th.&nbsp; He shook



<P>his fist in the other's face.&nbsp; "You 've got to hold



<P>'em back!" he shouted, savagely; "you 've got



<P>to hold 'em back!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In his agitation the colonel began to stammer.



<P>"A-all r-right, General, all right, by Gawd!&nbsp; We-



<P>we'll do our--we-we'll d-d-do--do our best, Gen-



<P>eral."&nbsp; The general made a passionate gesture



<P>and galloped away.&nbsp; The colonel, perchance to



<P>relieve his feelings, began to scold like a wet



<P>parrot.&nbsp; The youth, turning swiftly to make



<P>sure that the rear was unmolested, saw the com-



<P>mander regarding his men in a highly regretful



<P>manner, as if he regretted above everything his



<P>association with them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The man at the youth's elbow was mumbling,



<P>as if to himself: "Oh, we 're in for it now! oh,



<P>we 're in for it now!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The captain of the company had been pacing



<P>excitedly to and fro in the rear.&nbsp; He coaxed in



<P>schoolmistress fashion, as to a congregation of



<P>boys with primers.&nbsp; His talk was an endless



<P>repetition.&nbsp; "Reserve your fire, boys--don't



<P>shoot till I tell you--save your fire--wait till



<P>they get close up--don't be damned fools--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Perspiration streamed down the youth's face,



<P>which was soiled like that of a weeping urchin.



<P>He frequently, with a nervous movement, wiped



<P>his eyes with his coat sleeve.&nbsp; His mouth was



<P>still a little ways open.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He got the one glance at the foe-swarming



<P>field in front of him, and instantly ceased to de-



<P>bate the question of his piece being loaded.&nbsp; Be-



<P>fore he was ready to begin--before he had an-



<P>nounced to himself that he was about to fight--



<P>he threw the obedient, well-balanced rifle into



<P>position and fired a first wild shot.&nbsp; Directly he



<P>was working at his weapon like an automatic



<P>affair.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He suddenly lost concern for himself, and for-



<P>got to look at a menacing fate.&nbsp; He became not a



<P>man but a member.&nbsp; He felt that something of



<P>which he was a part--a regiment, an army, a



<P>cause, or a country--was in a crisis.&nbsp; He was



<P>welded into a common personality which was



<P>dominated by a single desire.&nbsp; For some mo-



<P>ments he could not flee no more than a little



<P>finger can commit a revolution from a hand.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>If he had thought the regiment was about to



<P>be annihilated perhaps he could have amputated



<P>himself from it.&nbsp; But its noise gave him assur-



<P>ance.&nbsp; The regiment was like a firework that,



<P>once ignited, proceeds superior to circumstances



<P>until its blazing vitality fades.&nbsp; It wheezed and



<P>banged with a mighty power.&nbsp; He pictured the



<P>ground before it as strewn with the discom-



<P>fited.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was a consciousness always of the pres-



<P>ence of his comrades about him.&nbsp; He felt the



<P>subtle battle brotherhood more potent even than



<P>the cause for which they were fighting.&nbsp; It was a



<P>mysterious fraternity born of the smoke and dan-



<P>ger of death.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He was at a task.&nbsp; He was like a carpenter



<P>who has made many boxes, making still another



<P>box, only there was furious haste in his move-



<P>ments.&nbsp; He, in his thought, was careering off in



<P>other places, even as the carpenter who as he



<P>works whistles and thinks of his friend or his



<P>enemy, his home or a saloon.&nbsp; And these jolted



<P>dreams were never perfect to him afterward, but



<P>remained a mass of blurred shapes.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Presently he began to feel the effects of the



<P>war atmosphere--a blistering sweat, a sensation



<P>that his eyeballs were about to crack like hot



<P>stones.&nbsp; A burning roar filled his ears.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Following this came a red rage.&nbsp; He devel-



<P>oped the acute exasperation of a pestered animal,



<P>a well-meaning cow worried by dogs.&nbsp; He had a



<P>mad feeling against his rifle, which could only be



<P>used against one life at a time.&nbsp; He wished to



<P>rush forward and strangle with his fingers.&nbsp; He



<P>craved a power that would enable him to make a



<P>world-sweeping gesture and brush all back.&nbsp; His



<P>impotency appeared to him, and made his rage



<P>into that of a driven beast.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Buried in the smoke of many rifles his anger



<P>was directed not so much against the men whom



<P>he knew were rushing toward him as against the



<P>swirling battle phantoms which were choking



<P>him, stuffing their smoke robes down his parched



<P>throat.&nbsp; He fought frantically for respite for his



<P>senses, for air, as a babe being smothered attacks



<P>the deadly blankets.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was a blare of heated rage mingled with



<P>a certain expression of intentness on all faces.



<P>Many of the men were making low-toned noises



<P>with their mouths, and these subdued cheers,



<P>snarls, imprecations, prayers, made a wild, bar-



<P>baric song that went as an undercurrent of sound,



<P>strange and chantlike with the resounding chords



<P>of the war march.&nbsp; The man at the youth's elbow



<P>was babbling.&nbsp; In it there was something soft and



<P>tender like the monologue of a babe.&nbsp; The tall



<P>soldier was swearing in a loud voice.&nbsp; From his



<P>lips came a black procession of curious oaths.&nbsp; Of



<P>a sudden another broke out in a querulous way



<P>like a man who has mislaid his hat.&nbsp; "Well, why



<P>don't they support us?&nbsp; Why don't they send



<P>supports?&nbsp; Do they think--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth in his battle sleep heard this as one



<P>who dozes hears.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was a singular absence of heroic poses.



<P>The men bending and surging in their haste and



<P>rage were in every impossible attitude.&nbsp; The steel



<P>ramrods clanked and clanged with incessant din



<P>as the men pounded them furiously into the hot



<P>rifle barrels.&nbsp; The flaps of the cartridge boxes were



<P>all unfastened, and bobbed idiotically with each



<P>movement.&nbsp; The rifles, once loaded, were jerked



<P>to the shoulder and fired without apparent aim



<P>into the smoke or at one of the blurred and shift-



<P>ing forms which upon the field before the regi-



<P>ment had been growing larger and larger like



<P>puppets under a magician's hand.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The officers, at their intervals, rearward, neg-



<P>lected to stand in picturesque attitudes.&nbsp; They



<P>were bobbing to and fro roaring directions and



<P>encouragements.&nbsp; The dimensions of their howls



<P>were extraordinary.&nbsp; They expended their lungs



<P>with prodigal wills.&nbsp; And often they nearly stood



<P>upon their heads in their anxiety to observe the



<P>enemy on the other side of the tumbling smoke.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The lieutenant of the youth's company had en-



<P>countered a soldier who had fled screaming at



<P>the first volley of his comrades.&nbsp; Behind the lines



<P>these two were acting a little isolated scene.&nbsp; The



<P>man was blubbering and staring with sheeplike



<P>eyes at the lieutenant, who had seized him by the



<P>collar and was pommeling him.&nbsp; He drove him



<P>back into the ranks with many blows.&nbsp; The sol-



<P>dier went mechanically, dully, with his animal-



<P>like eyes upon the officer.&nbsp; Perhaps there was to



<P>him a divinity expressed in the voice of the other



<P>--stern, hard, with no reflection of fear in it.&nbsp; He



<P>tried to reload his gun, but his shaking hands pre-



<P>vented.&nbsp; The lieutenant was obliged to assist him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The men dropped here and there like bundles.



<P>The captain of the youth's company had been



<P>killed in an early part of the action.&nbsp; His body



<P>lay stretched out in the position of a tired man



<P>resting, but upon his face there was an astonished



<P>and sorrowful look, as if he thought some friend



<P>had done him an ill turn.&nbsp; The babbling man was



<P>grazed by a shot that made the blood stream



<P>widely down his face.&nbsp; He clapped both hands



<P>to his head.&nbsp; "Oh!" he said, and ran.&nbsp; Another



<P>grunted suddenly as if he had been struck by a



<P>club in the stomach.&nbsp; He sat down and gazed



<P>ruefully.&nbsp; In his eyes there was mute, indefinite



<P>reproach.&nbsp; Farther up the line a man, standing



<P>behind a tree, had had his knee joint splintered



<P>by a ball.&nbsp; Immediately he had dropped his rifle



<P>and gripped the tree with both arms.&nbsp; And there



<P>he remained, clinging desperately and crying for



<P>assistance that he might withdraw his hold upon



<P>the tree.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At last an exultant yell went along the quiver-



<P>ing line.&nbsp; The firing dwindled from an uproar to



<P>a last vindictive popping.&nbsp; As the smoke slowly



<P>eddied away, the youth saw that the charge had



<P>been repulsed.&nbsp; The enemy were scattered into



<P>reluctant groups.&nbsp; He saw a man climb to the



<P>top of the fence, straddle the rail, and fire a part-



<P>ing shot.&nbsp; The waves had receded, leaving bits of



<P>dark debris upon the ground.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Some in the regiment began to whoop fren-



<P>ziedly.&nbsp; Many were silent.&nbsp; Apparently they were



<P>trying to contemplate themselves.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After the fever had left his veins, the youth



<P>thought that at last he was going to suffocate.



<P>He became aware of the foul atmosphere in



<P>which he had been struggling.&nbsp; He was grimy



<P>and dripping like a laborer in a foundry.&nbsp; He



<P>grasped his canteen and took a long swallow of



<P>the warmed water.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A sentence with variations went up and down



<P>the line.&nbsp; "Well, we 've helt 'em back.&nbsp; We 've



<P>helt 'em back; derned if we haven't."&nbsp; The men



<P>said it blissfully, leering at each other with dirty



<P>smiles.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth turned to look behind him and off



<P>to the right and off to the left.&nbsp; He experienced



<P>the joy of a man who at last finds leisure in which



<P>to look about him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Under foot there were a few ghastly forms



<P>motionless.&nbsp; They lay twisted in fantastic contor-



<P>tions.&nbsp; Arms were bent and heads were turned



<P>in incredible ways.&nbsp; It seemed that the dead men



<P>must have fallen from some great height to get



<P>into such positions.&nbsp; They looked to be dumped



<P>out upon the ground from the sky.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>From a position in the rear of the grove a bat-



<P>tery was throwing shells over it.&nbsp; The flash of



<P>the guns startled the youth at first.&nbsp; He thought



<P>they were aimed directly at him.&nbsp; Through the



<P>trees he watched the black figures of the gunners



<P>as they worked swiftly and intently.&nbsp; Their labor



<P>seemed a complicated thing.&nbsp; He wondered how



<P>they could remember its formula in the midst of



<P>confusion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The guns squatted in a row like savage chiefs.



<P>They argued with abrupt violence.&nbsp; It was a



<P>grim pow-wow.&nbsp; Their busy servants ran hither



<P>and thither.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A small procession of wounded men were go-



<P>ing drearily toward the rear.&nbsp; It was a flow of



<P>blood from the torn body of the brigade.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>To the right and to the left were the dark



<P>lines of other troops.&nbsp; Far in front he thought he



<P>could see lighter masses protruding in points



<P>from the forest.&nbsp; They were suggestive of un-



<P>numbered thousands.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Once he saw a tiny battery go dashing along



<P>the line of the horizon.&nbsp; The tiny riders were



<P>beating the tiny horses.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>From a sloping hill came the sound of cheer-



<P>ings and clashes.&nbsp; Smoke welled slowly through



<P>the leaves.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Batteries were speaking with thunderous ora-



<P>torical effort.&nbsp; Here and there were flags, the



<P>red in the stripes dominating.&nbsp; They splashed



<P>bits of warm color upon the dark lines of



<P>troops.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth felt the old thrill at the sight of



<P>the emblem.&nbsp; They were like beautiful birds



<P>strangely undaunted in a storm.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As he listened to the din from the hillside, to



<P>a deep pulsating thunder that came from afar to



<P>the left, and to the lesser clamors which came



<P>from many directions, it occurred to him that



<P>they were fighting, too, over there, and over



<P>there, and over there.&nbsp; Heretofore he had sup-



<P>posed that all the battle was directly under his



<P>nose.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As he gazed around him the youth felt a flash



<P>of astonishment at the blue, pure sky and the



<P>sun gleamings on the trees and fields.&nbsp; It was



<P>surprising that Nature had gone tranquilly on



<P>with her golden process in the midst of so much



<P>devilment.



<P>
