<UL><B><I><FONT SIZE=+3>The Red Badge of Courage</FONT></I></B></UL>

</UL>

</UL>



<UL>

<UL>

<UL>

<UL>

<UL><B><FONT SIZE=+2>by Stephen Crane</FONT></B></UL>

</UL>

</UL>

</UL>

</UL>



<HR WIDTH="100%">

<UL>

<UL>

<UL><B><FONT SIZE=+1>An Episode of the American Civil War</FONT></B></UL>

</UL>

</UL>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER I.</FONT></B>

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>THE cold passed reluctantly from the earth,



<P>and the retiring fogs revealed an army stretched



<P>out on the hills, resting.&nbsp; As the landscape



<P>changed from brown to green, the army awak-



<P>ened, and began to tremble with eagerness at the



<P>noise of rumors.&nbsp; It cast its eyes upon the roads,



<P>which were growing from long troughs of liquid



<P>mud to proper thoroughfares.&nbsp; A river, amber-



<P>tinted in the shadow of its banks, purled at the



<P>army's feet; and at night, when the stream had



<P>become of a sorrowful blackness, one could see



<P>across it the red, eyelike gleam of hostile camp-



<P>fires set in the low brows of distant hills.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Once a certain tall soldier developed virtues



<P>and went resolutely to wash a shirt.&nbsp; He came



<P>flying back from a brook waving his garment



<P>bannerlike.&nbsp; He was swelled with a tale he had



<P>heard from a reliable friend, who had heard it



<P>from a truthful cavalryman, who had heard it



<P>from his trustworthy brother, one of the order-



<P>lies at division headquarters.&nbsp; He adopted the



<P>important air of a herald in red and gold.



<P>"We're goin' t' move t' morrah--sure," he



<P>said pompously to a group in the company



<P>street.&nbsp; "We're goin' 'way up the river, cut



<P>across, an' come around in behint 'em."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>To his attentive audience he drew a loud



<P>and elaborate plan of a very brilliant campaign.



<P>When he had finished, the blue-clothed men



<P>scattered into small arguing groups between the



<P>rows of squat brown huts.&nbsp; A negro teamster who



<P>had been dancing upon a cracker box with the



<P>hilarious encouragement of twoscore soldiers



<P>was deserted.&nbsp; He sat mournfully down.&nbsp; Smoke



<P>drifted lazily from a multitude of quaint chim-



<P>neys.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"It's a lie! that's all it is--a thunderin' lie!"



<P>said another private loudly.&nbsp; His smooth face was



<P>flushed, and his hands were thrust sulkily into his



<P>trousers' pockets.&nbsp; He took the matter as an



<P>affront to him.&nbsp; "I don't believe the derned old



<P>army's ever going to move.&nbsp; We're set.&nbsp; I've



<P>got ready to move eight times in the last two



<P>weeks, and we ain't moved yet."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The tall soldier felt called upon to defend



<P>the truth of a rumor he himself had intro-



<P>duced.&nbsp; He and the loud one came near to fight-



<P>ing over it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A corporal began to swear before the assem-



<P>blage.&nbsp; He had just put a costly board floor in



<P>his house, he said.&nbsp; During the early spring he



<P>had refrained from adding extensively to the



<P>comfort of his environment because he had felt



<P>that the army might start on the march at any



<P>moment.&nbsp; Of late, however, he had been im-



<P>pressed that they were in a sort of eternal camp.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Many of the men engaged in a spirited debate.



<P>One outlined in a peculiarly lucid manner all the



<P>plans of the commanding general.&nbsp; He was op-



<P>posed by men who advocated that there were



<P>other plans of campaign.&nbsp; They clamored at each



<P>other, numbers making futile bids for the pop-



<P>ular attention.&nbsp; Meanwhile, the soldier who had



<P>fetched the rumor bustled about with much



<P>importance.&nbsp; He was continually assailed by



<P>questions.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"What's up, Jim?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Th' army's goin' t' move."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Ah, what yeh talkin' about?&nbsp; How yeh know



<P>it is?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, yeh kin b'lieve me er not, jest as yeh



<P>like.&nbsp; I don't care a hang."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was much food for thought in the man-



<P>ner in which he replied.&nbsp; He came near to con-



<P>vincing them by disdaining to produce proofs.



<P>They grew excited over it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was a youthful private who listened



<P>with eager ears to the words of the tall soldier



<P>and to the varied comments of his comrades.



<P>After receiving a fill of discussions concerning



<P>marches and attacks, he went to his hut and



<P>crawled through an intricate hole that served it



<P>as a door.&nbsp; He wished to be alone with some



<P>new thoughts that had lately come to him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He lay down on a wide bank that stretched



<P>across the end of the room.&nbsp; In the other end,



<P>cracker boxes were made to serve as furniture.



<P>They were grouped about the fireplace.&nbsp; A pic-



<P>ture from an illustrated weekly was upon the log



<P>walls, and three rifles were paralleled on pegs.



<P>Equipments hunt on handy projections, and some



<P>tin dishes lay upon a small pile of firewood.&nbsp; A



<P>folded tent was serving as a roof.&nbsp; The sunlight,



<P>without, beating upon it, made it glow a light



<P>yellow shade.&nbsp; A small window shot an oblique



<P>square of whiter light upon the cluttered floor.



<P>The smoke from the fire at times neglected the



<P>clay chimney and wreathed into the room, and



<P>this flimsy chimney of clay and sticks made end-



<P>less threats to set ablaze the whole establishment.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth was in a little trance of astonish-



<P>ment.&nbsp; So they were at last going to fight.&nbsp; On



<P>the morrow, perhaps, there would be a battle, and



<P>he would be in it.&nbsp; For a time he was obliged



<P>to labor to make himself believe.&nbsp; He could not



<P>accept with assurance an omen that he was about



<P>to mingle in one of those great affairs of the earth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He had, of course, dreamed of battles all



<P>his life--of vague and bloody conflicts that had



<P>thrilled him with their sweep and fire.&nbsp; In visions



<P>he had seen himself in many struggles.&nbsp; He had



<P>imagined peoples secure in the shadow of his



<P>eagle-eyed prowess.&nbsp; But awake he had regarded



<P>battles as crimson blotches on the pages of the



<P>past.&nbsp; He had put them as things of the bygone



<P>with his thought-images of heavy crowns and



<P>high castles.&nbsp; There was a portion of the world's



<P>history which he had regarded as the time of



<P>wars, but it, he thought, had been long gone over



<P>the horizon and had disappeared forever.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>From his home his youthful eyes had looked



<P>upon the war in his own country with distrust.



<P>It must be some sort of a play affair.&nbsp; He had



<P>long despaired of witnessing a Greeklike struggle.



<P>Such would be no more, he had said.&nbsp; Men were



<P>better, or more timid.&nbsp; Secular and religious



<P>education had effaced the throat-grappling in-



<P>stinct, or else firm finance held in check the pas-



<P>sions.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He had burned several times to enlist.&nbsp; Tales



<P>of great movements shook the land.&nbsp; They might



<P>not be distinctly Homeric, but there seemed to



<P>be much glory in them.&nbsp; He had read of marches,



<P>sieges, conflicts, and he had longed to see it all.



<P>His busy mind had drawn for him large pictures



<P>extravagant in color, lurid with breathless deeds.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But his mother had discouraged him.&nbsp; She



<P>had affected to look with some contempt upon



<P>the quality of his war ardor and patriotism.&nbsp; She



<P>could calmly seat herself and with no apparent



<P>difficulty give him many hundreds of reasons



<P>why he was of vastly more importance on the



<P>farm than on the field of battle.&nbsp; She had had



<P>certain ways of expression that told him that her



<P>statements on the subject came from a deep con-



<P>viction.&nbsp; Moreover, on her side, was his belief



<P>that her ethical motive in the argument was



<P>impregnable.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At last, however, he had made firm rebellion



<P>against this yellow light thrown upon the color of



<P>his ambitions.&nbsp; The newspapers, the gossip of the



<P>village, his own picturings had aroused him to



<P>an uncheckable degree.&nbsp; They were in truth



<P>fighting finely down there.&nbsp; Almost every day



<P>the newspapers printed accounts of a decisive



<P>victory.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>One night, as he lay in bed, the winds had



<P>carried to him the clangoring of the church bell



<P>as some enthusiast jerked the rope frantically to



<P>tell the twisted news of a great battle.&nbsp; This



<P>voice of the people rejoicing in the night had



<P>made him shiver in a prolonged ecstasy of ex-



<P>citement.&nbsp; Later, he had gone down to his



<P>mother's room and had spoken thus: "Ma, I'm



<P>going to enlist."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Henry, don't you be a fool," his mother had



<P>replied.&nbsp; She had then covered her face with the



<P>quilt.&nbsp; There was an end to the matter for that



<P>night.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Nevertheless, the next morning he had gone



<P>to a town that was near his mother's farm and



<P>had enlisted in a company that was forming there.



<P>When he had returned home his mother was



<P>milking the brindle cow.&nbsp; Four others stood



<P>waiting.&nbsp; "Ma, I've enlisted," he had said to her



<P>diffidently.&nbsp; There was a short silence.&nbsp; "The



<P>Lord's will be done, Henry," she had finally



<P>replied, and had then continued to milk the



<P>brindle cow.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When he had stood in the doorway with his



<P>soldier's clothes on his back, and with the light of



<P>excitement and expectancy in his eyes almost



<P>defeating the glow of regret for the home bonds,



<P>he had seen two tears leaving their trails on his



<P>mother's scarred cheeks.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Still, she had disappointed him by saying



<P>nothing whatever about returning with his shield



<P>or on it.&nbsp; He had privately primed himself for a



<P>beautiful scene.&nbsp; He had prepared certain sen-



<P>tences which he thought could be used with



<P>touching effect.&nbsp; But her words destroyed his



<P>plans.&nbsp; She had doggedly peeled potatoes and



<P>addressed him as follows: "You watch out,



<P>Henry, an' take good care of yerself in this here



<P>fighting business--you watch out, an' take good



<P>care of yerself.&nbsp; Don't go a-thinkin' you can



<P>lick the hull rebel army at the start, because yeh



<P>can't.&nbsp; Yer jest one little feller amongst a hull lot



<P>of others, and yeh've got to keep quiet an' do what



<P>they tell yeh.&nbsp; I know how you are, Henry.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I've knet yeh eight pair of socks, Henry, and



<P>I've put in all yer best shirts, because I want my



<P>boy to be jest as warm and comf'able as anybody



<P>in the army.&nbsp; Whenever they get holes in 'em, I



<P>want yeh to send 'em right-away back to me, so's



<P>I kin dern 'em.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"An' allus be careful an' choose yer comp'ny.



<P>There's lots of bad men in the army, Henry.



<P>The army makes 'em wild, and they like nothing



<P>better than the job of leading off a young feller



<P>like you, as ain't never been away from home



<P>much and has allus had a mother, an' a-learning



<P>'em to drink and swear.&nbsp; Keep clear of them



<P>folks, Henry.&nbsp; I don't want yeh to ever do any-



<P>thing, Henry, that yeh would be 'shamed to let



<P>me know about.&nbsp; Jest think as if I was a-watchin'



<P>yeh.&nbsp; If yeh keep that in yer mind allus, I guess



<P>yeh'll come out about right.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yeh must allus remember yer father, too,



<P>child, an' remember he never drunk a drop of



<P>licker in his life, and seldom swore a cross oath.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I don't know what else to tell yeh, Henry,



<P>excepting that yeh must never do no shirking,



<P>child, on my account.&nbsp; If so be a time comes when



<P>yeh have to be kilt or do a mean thing, why,



<P>Henry, don't think of anything 'cept what's right,



<P>because there's many a woman has to bear up



<P>'ginst sech things these times, and the Lord 'll



<P>take keer of us all.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Don't forgit about the socks and the shirts,



<P>child; and I've put a cup of blackberry jam with



<P>yer bundle, because I know yeh like it above all



<P>things.&nbsp; Good-by, Henry.&nbsp; Watch out, and be a



<P>good boy."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He had, of course, been impatient under the



<P>ordeal of this speech.&nbsp; It had not been quite what



<P>he expected, and he had borne it with an air of



<P>irritation.&nbsp; He departed feeling vague relief.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Still, when he had looked back from the gate,



<P>he had seen his mother kneeling among the po-



<P>tato parings.&nbsp; Her brown face, upraised, was



<P>stained with tears, and her spare form was quiver-

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>10 RED BADGE OF COURAGE.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>ing.&nbsp; He bowed his head and went on, feeling



<P>suddenly ashamed of his purposes.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>From his home he had gone to the seminary



<P>to bid adieu to many schoolmates.&nbsp; They had



<P>thronged about him with wonder and admiration.



<P>He had felt the gulf now between them and had



<P>swelled with calm pride.&nbsp; He and some of his



<P>fellows who had donned blue were quite over-



<P>whelmed with privileges for all of one afternoon,



<P>and it had been a very delicious thing.&nbsp; They had



<P>strutted.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A certain light-haired girl had made vivacious



<P>fun at his martial spirit, but there was another and



<P>darker girl whom he had gazed at steadfastly, and



<P>he thought she grew demure and sad at sight of



<P>his blue and brass.&nbsp; As he had walked down the



<P>path between the rows of oaks, he had turned his



<P>head and detected her at a window watching his



<P>departure.&nbsp; As he perceived her, she had im-



<P>mediately begun to stare up through the high



<P>tree branches at the sky.&nbsp; He had seen a good



<P>deal of flurry and haste in her movement as she



<P>changed her attitude.&nbsp; He often thought of it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>On the way to Washington his spirit had



<P>soared.&nbsp; The regiment was fed and caressed at



<P>station after station until the youth had believed



<P>that he must be a hero.&nbsp; There was a lavish ex-



<P>penditure of bread and cold meats, coffee, and



<P>pickles and cheese.&nbsp; As he basked in the smiles



<P>of the girls and was patted and complimented by



<P>the old men, he had felt growing within him the



<P>strength to do mighty deeds of arms.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After complicated journeyings with many



<P>pauses, there had come months of monotonous



<P>life in a camp.&nbsp; He had had the belief that real



<P>war was a series of death struggles with small



<P>time in between for sleep and meals; but since his



<P>regiment had come to the field the army had done



<P>little but sit still and try to keep warm.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He was brought then gradually back to his old



<P>ideas.&nbsp; Greeklike struggles would be no more.



<P>Men were better, or more timid.&nbsp; Secular and



<P>religious education had effaced the throat-grap-



<P>pling instinct, or else firm finance held in check



<P>the passions.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He had grown to regard himself merely as a



<P>part of a vast blue demonstration.&nbsp; His province



<P>was to look out, as far as he could, for his per-



<P>sonal comfort.&nbsp; For recreation he could twiddle



<P>his thumbs and speculate on the thoughts which



<P>must agitate the minds of the generals.&nbsp; Also, he



<P>was drilled and drilled and reviewed, and drilled



<P>and drilled and reviewed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The only foes he had seen were some pickets



<P>along the river bank.&nbsp; They were a sun-tanned,



<P>philosophical lot, who sometimes shot reflectively



<P>at the blue pickets.&nbsp; When reproached for this



<P>afterward, they usually expressed sorrow, and



<P>swore by their gods that the guns had exploded



<P>without their permission.&nbsp; The youth, on guard



<P>duty one night, conversed across the stream with



<P>one of them.&nbsp; He was a slightly ragged man, who



<P>spat skillfully between his shoes and possessed a



<P>great fund of bland and infantile assurance.&nbsp; The



<P>youth liked him personally.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yank," the other had informed him, "yer a



<P>right dum good feller."&nbsp; This sentiment, floating



<P>to him upon the still air, had made him tempo-



<P>rarily regret war.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Various veterans had told him tales.&nbsp; Some



<P>talked of gray, bewhiskered hordes who were



<P>advancing with relentless curses and chewing



<P>tobacco with unspeakable valor; tremendous



<P>bodies of fierce soldiery who were sweeping



<P>along like the Huns.&nbsp; Others spoke of tattered



<P>and eternally hungry men who fired despondent



<P>powders.&nbsp; "They'll charge through hell's fire an'



<P>brimstone t' git a holt on a haversack, an' sech



<P>stomachs ain't a-lastin' long," he was told.&nbsp; From



<P>the stories, the youth imagined the red, live bones



<P>sticking out through slits in the faded uniforms.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Still, he could not put a whole faith in veter-



<P>ans' tales, for recruits were their prey.&nbsp; They



<P>talked much of smoke, fire, and blood, but he



<P>could not tell how much might be lies.&nbsp; They



<P>persistently yelled "Fresh fish!" at him, and were



<P>in no wise to be trusted.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>However, he perceived now that it did not



<P>greatly matter what kind of soldiers he was going



<P>to fight, so long as they fought, which fact no one



<P>disputed.&nbsp; There was a more serious problem.&nbsp; He



<P>lay in his bunk pondering upon it.&nbsp; He tried to



<P>mathematically prove to himself that he would



<P>not run from a battle.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Previously he had never felt obliged to wrestle



<P>too seriously with this question.&nbsp; In his life he had



<P>taken certain things for granted, never challeng-



<P>ing his belief in ultimate success, and bothering



<P>little about means and roads.&nbsp; But here he was



<P>confronted with a thing of moment.&nbsp; It had sud-



<P>denly appeared to him that perhaps in a battle he



<P>might run.&nbsp; He was forced to admit that as far as



<P>war was concerned he knew nothing of himself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A sufficient time before he would have allowed



<P>the problem to kick its heels at the outer portals



<P>of his mind, but now he felt compelled to give



<P>serious attention to it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A little panic-fear grew in his mind.&nbsp; As his



<P>imagination went forward to a fight, he saw hide-



<P>ous possibilities.&nbsp; He contemplated the lurking



<P>menaces of the future, and failed in an effort to



<P>see himself standing stoutly in the midst of them.



<P>He recalled his visions of broken-bladed glory,



<P>but in the shadow of the impending tumult he



<P>suspected them to be impossible pictures.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He sprang from the bunk and began to pace



<P>nervously to and fro.&nbsp; "Good Lord, what's th'



<P>matter with me?" he said aloud.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He felt that in this crisis his laws of life were



<P>useless.&nbsp; Whatever he had learned of himself was



<P>here of no avail.&nbsp; He was an unknown quantity.



<P>He saw that he would again be obliged to experi-



<P>ment as he had in early youth.&nbsp; He must accumu-



<P>late information of himself, and meanwhile he re-



<P>solved to remain close upon his guard lest those



<P>qualities of which he knew nothing should ever-



<P>lastingly disgrace him.&nbsp; "Good Lord!" he re-



<P>peated in dismay.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After a time the tall soldier slid dexterously



<P>through the hole.&nbsp; The loud private followed.



<P>They were wrangling.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"That's all right," said the tall soldier as he



<P>entered.&nbsp; He waved his hand expressively.&nbsp; "You



<P>can believe me or not, jest as you like.&nbsp; All you



<P>got to do is to sit down and wait as quiet as you



<P>can.&nbsp; Then pretty soon you'll find out I was right."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His comrade grunted stubbornly.&nbsp; For a mo-



<P>ment he seemed to be searching for a formidable



<P>reply.&nbsp; Finally he said: "Well, you don't know



<P>everything in the world, do you?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Didn't say I knew everything in the world,"



<P>retorted the other sharply.&nbsp; He began to stow



<P>various articles snugly into his knapsack.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth, pausing in his nervous walk, looked



<P>down at the busy figure.&nbsp; "Going to be a battle,



<P>sure, is there, Jim?" he asked.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Of course there is," replied the tall soldier.



<P>"Of course there is.&nbsp; You jest wait 'til to-morrow,



<P>and you'll see one of the biggest battles ever was.



<P>You jest wait."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Thunder!der!" said the youth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh, you'll see fighting this time, my boy,



<P>what'll be regular out-and-out fighting," added



<P>the tall soldier, with the air of a man who is



<P>about to exhibit a battle for the benefit of his



<P>friends.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Huh!" said the loud one from a corner.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well," remarked the youth, "like as not this



<P>story'll turn out jest like them others did."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Not much it won't," replied the tall soldier,



<P>exasperated.&nbsp; "Not much it won't.&nbsp; Didn't the



<P>cavalry all start this morning?"&nbsp; He glared about



<P>him.&nbsp; No one denied his statement.&nbsp; "The cav-



<P>alry started this morning," he continued.&nbsp; "They



<P>say there ain't hardly any cavalry left in camp.



<P>They're going to Richmond, or some place, while



<P>we fight all the Johnnies.&nbsp; It's some dodge like



<P>that.&nbsp; The regiment's got orders, too.&nbsp; A feller



<P>what seen 'em go to headquarters told me a little



<P>while ago.&nbsp; And they're raising blazes all over



<P>camp--anybody can see that."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Shucks!" said the loud one.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth remained silent for a time.&nbsp; At last



<P>he spoke to the tall soldier.&nbsp; "Jim!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"What?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"How do you think the reg'ment 'll do?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh, they'll fight all right, I guess, after they



<P>once get into it," said the other with cold judg-



<P>ment.&nbsp; He made a fine use of the third person.



<P>"There's been heaps of fun poked at 'em because



<P>they're new, of course, and all that; but they'll



<P>fight all right, I guess."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Think any of the boys 'll run?" persisted the



<P>youth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh, there may be a few of 'em run, but



<P>there's them kind in every regiment, 'specially



<P>when they first goes under fire," said the other



<P>in a tolerant way.&nbsp; "Of course it might happen



<P>that the hull kit-and-boodle might start and run,



<P>if some big fighting came first-off, and then again



<P>they might stay and fight like fun.&nbsp; But you can't



<P>bet on nothing.&nbsp; Of course they ain't never been



<P>under fire yet, and it ain't likely they'll lick the



<P>hull rebel army all-to-oncet the first time; but I



<P>think they'll fight better than some, if worse than



<P>others.&nbsp; That's the way I figger.&nbsp; They call the



<P>reg'ment 'Fresh fish' and everything; but the



<P>boys come of good stock, and most of 'em 'll fight



<P>like sin after they oncet git shootin'," he added,



<P>with a mighty emphasis on the last four words.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh, you think you know--" began the loud



<P>soldier with scorn.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The other turned savagely upon him.&nbsp; They



<P>had a rapid altercation, in which they fastened



<P>upon each other various strange epithets.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth at last interrupted them.&nbsp; "Did



<P>you ever think you might run yourself, Jim?" he



<P>asked.&nbsp; On concluding the sentence he laughed



<P>as if he had meant to aim a joke.&nbsp; The loud sol-



<P>dier also giggled.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The tall private waved his hand.&nbsp; "Well," said



<P>he profoundly, "I've thought it might get too hot



<P>for Jim Conklin in some of them scrimmages, and



<P>if a whole lot of boys started and run, why, I



<P>s'pose I'd start and run.&nbsp; And if I once started to



<P>run, I'd run like the devil, and no mistake.&nbsp; But



<P>if everybody was a-standing and a-fighting, why,



<P>I'd stand and fight.&nbsp; Be jiminey, I would.&nbsp; I'll



<P>bet on it."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Huh!" said the loud one.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth of this tale felt gratitude for these



<P>words of his comrade.&nbsp; He had feared that all of



<P>the untried men possessed a great and correct



<P>confidence.&nbsp; He now was in a measure reassured.



<P>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XV.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>THE regiment was standing at order arms at



<P>the side of a lane, waiting for the command to



<P>march, when suddenly the youth remembered



<P>the little packet enwrapped in a faded yellow



<P>envelope which the loud young soldier with lugu-



<P>brious words had intrusted to him.&nbsp; It made him



<P>start.&nbsp; He uttered an exclamation and turned



<P>toward his comrade.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Wilson!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"What?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His friend, at his side in the ranks, was thought-



<P>fully staring down the road.&nbsp; From some cause



<P>his expression was at that moment very meek.



<P>The youth, regarding him with sidelong glances,



<P>felt impelled to change his purpose.&nbsp; "Oh, noth-



<P>ing," he said.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His friend turned his head in some surprise,



<P>"Why, what was yeh goin' t' say?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh, nothing," repeated the youth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He resolved not to deal the little blow.&nbsp; It

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>148



<P>was sufficient that the fact made him glad.&nbsp; It



<P>was not necessary to knock his friend on the head



<P>with the misguided packet.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He had been possessed of much fear of his



<P>friend, for he saw how easily questionings could



<P>make holes in his feelings.&nbsp; Lately, he had as-



<P>sured himself that the altered comrade would not



<P>tantalize him with a persistent curiosity, but he



<P>felt certain that during the first period of leisure



<P>his friend would ask him to relate his adventures



<P>of the previous day.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He now rejoiced in the possession of a small



<P>weapon with which he could prostrate his com-



<P>rade at the first signs of a cross-examination.&nbsp; He



<P>was master.&nbsp; It would now be he who could



<P>laugh and shoot the shafts of derision.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The friend had, in a weak hour, spoken with



<P>sobs of his own death.&nbsp; He had delivered a mel-



<P>ancholy oration previous to his funeral, and had



<P>doubtless in the packet of letters, presented vari-



<P>ous keepsakes to relatives.&nbsp; But he had not died,



<P>and thus he had delivered himself into the hands



<P>of the youth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The latter felt immensely superior to his



<P>friend, but he inclined to condescension.&nbsp; He



<P>adopted toward him an air of patronizing good



<P>humor.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His self-pride was now entirely restored.&nbsp; In



<P>the shade of its flourishing growth he stood with



<P>braced and self-confident legs, and since nothing



<P>could now be discovered he did not shrink from



<P>an encounter with the eyes of judges, and allowed



<P>no thoughts of his own to keep him from an



<P>attitude of manfulness.&nbsp; He had performed his



<P>mistakes in the dark, so he was still a man.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Indeed, when he remembered his fortunes of



<P>yesterday, and looked at them from a distance he



<P>began to see something fine there.&nbsp; He had



<P>license to be pompous and veteranlike.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His panting agonies of the past he put out of



<P>his sight.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In the present, he declared to himself that it



<P>was only the doomed and the damned who roared



<P>with sincerity at circumstance.&nbsp; Few but they



<P>ever did it.&nbsp; A man with a full stomach and the



<P>respect of his fellows had no business to scold



<P>about anything that he might think to be wrong



<P>in the ways of the universe, or even with the



<P>ways of society.&nbsp; Let the unfortunates rail; the



<P>others may play marbles.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He did not give a great deal of thought to



<P>these battles that lay directly before him.&nbsp; It was



<P>not essential that he should plan his ways in



<P>regard to them.&nbsp; He had been taught that many



<P>obligations of a life were easily avoided.&nbsp; The



<P>lessons of yesterday had been that retribution



<P>was a laggard and blind.&nbsp; With these facts before



<P>him he did not deem it necessary that he should



<P>become feverish over the possibilities of the



<P>ensuing twenty-four hours.&nbsp; He could leave



<P>much to chance.&nbsp; Besides, a faith in himself had



<P>secretly blossomed.&nbsp; There was a little flower of



<P>confidence growing within him.&nbsp; He was now a



<P>man of experience.&nbsp; He had been out among the



<P>dragons, he said, and he assured himself that they



<P>were not so hideous as he had imagined them.



<P>Also, they were inaccurate; they did not sting



<P>with precision.&nbsp; A stout heart often defied, and



<P>defying, escaped.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>And, furthermore, how could they kill him



<P>who was the chosen of gods and doomed to



<P>greatness?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He remembered how some of the men had



<P>run from the battle.&nbsp; As he recalled their terror-



<P>struck faces he felt a scorn for them.&nbsp; They had



<P>surely been more fleet and more wild than was



<P>absolutely necessary.&nbsp; They were weak mortals.



<P>As for himself, he had fled with discretion and



<P>dignity.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He was aroused from this reverie by his



<P>friend, who, having hitched about nervously and



<P>blinked at the trees for a time, suddenly coughed



<P>in an introductory way, and spoke.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Fleming!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"What?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The friend put his hand up to his mouth and



<P>coughed again.&nbsp; He fidgeted in his jacket.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well," he gulped, at last, "I guess yeh might



<P>as well give me back them letters."&nbsp; Dark, prick-



<P>ling blood had flushed into his cheeks and brow.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"All right, Wilson," said the youth.&nbsp; He



<P>loosened two buttons of his coat, thrust in his



<P>hand, and brought forth the packet.&nbsp; As he ex-



<P>tended it to his friend the latter's face was turned



<P>from him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He had been slow in the act of producing the



<P>packet because during it he had been trying to



<P>invent a remarkable comment upon the affair.



<P>He could conjure nothing of sufficient point.&nbsp; He



<P>was compelled to allow his friend to escape



<P>unmolested with his packet.&nbsp; And for this he



<P>took unto himself considerable credit.&nbsp; It was a



<P>generous thing.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His friend at his side seemed suffering great



<P>shame.&nbsp; As he contemplated him, the youth felt



<P>his heart grow more strong and stout.&nbsp; He had



<P>never been compelled to blush in such manner



<P>for his acts; he was an individual of extraordi-



<P>nary virtues.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He reflected, with condescending pity: "Too



<P>bad!&nbsp; Too bad!&nbsp; The poor devil, it makes him



<P>feel tough!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After this incident, and as he reviewed the



<P>battle pictures he had seen, he felt quite com-



<P>petent to return home and make the hearts of



<P>the people glow with stories of war.&nbsp; He could



<P>see himself in a room of warm tints telling tales



<P>to listeners.&nbsp; He could exhibit laurels.&nbsp; They



<P>were insignificant; still, in a district where



<P>laurels were infrequent, they might shine.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He saw his gaping audience picturing him as



<P>the central figure in blazing scenes.&nbsp; And he



<P>imagined the consternation and the ejaculations



<P>of his mother and the young lady at the seminary



<P>as they drank his recitals.&nbsp; Their vague feminine



<P>formula for beloved ones doing brave deeds on



<P>the field of battle without risk of life would be



<P>destroyed.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>chapter14</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XIV.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>WHEN the youth awoke it seemed to him that



<P>he had been asleep for a thousand years, and he



<P>felt sure that he opened his eyes upon an unex-



<P>pected world.&nbsp; Gray mists were slowly shifting



<P>before the first efforts of the sun rays.&nbsp; An im-



<P>pending splendor could be seen in the eastern



<P>sky.&nbsp; An icy dew had chilled his face, and im-



<P>mediately upon arousing he curled farther down



<P>into his blanket.&nbsp; He stared for a while at the



<P>leaves overhead, moving in a heraldic wind of



<P>the day.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The distance was splintering and blaring with



<P>the noise of fighting.&nbsp; There was in the sound



<P>an expression of a deadly persistency, as if it had



<P>not begun and was not to cease.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>About him were the rows and groups of men



<P>that he had dimly seen the previous night.&nbsp; They



<P>were getting a last draught of sleep before the



<P>awakening.&nbsp; The gaunt, careworn features and



<P>dusty figures were made plain by this quaint

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>139



<P>light at the dawning, but it dressed the skin of



<P>the men in corpselike hues and made the tangled



<P>limbs appear pulseless and dead.&nbsp; The youth



<P>started up with a little cry when his eyes first



<P>swept over this motionless mass of men, thick-



<P>spread upon the ground, pallid, and in strange



<P>postures.&nbsp; His disordered mind interpreted the



<P>hall of the forest as a charnel place.&nbsp; He believed



<P>for an instant that he was in the house of the



<P>dead, and he did not dare to move lest these



<P>corpses start up, squalling and squawking.&nbsp; In a



<P>second, however, he achieved his proper mind.



<P>He swore a complicated oath at himself.&nbsp; He



<P>saw that this somber picture was not a fact of



<P>the present, but a mere prophecy.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He heard then the noise of a fire crackling



<P>briskly in the cold air, and, turning his head, he



<P>saw his friend pottering busily about a small



<P>blaze.&nbsp; A few other figures moved in the fog, and



<P>he heard the hard cracking of axe blows.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Suddenly there was a hollow rumble of



<P>drums.&nbsp; A distant bugle sang faintly.&nbsp; Similar



<P>sounds, varying in strength, came from near and



<P>far over the forest.&nbsp; The bugles called to each



<P>other like brazen gamecocks.&nbsp; The near thunder



<P>of the regimental drums rolled.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The body of men in the woods rustled.&nbsp; There



<P>was a general uplifting of heads.&nbsp; A murmuring



<P>of voices broke upon the air.&nbsp; In it there was



<P>much bass of grumbling oaths.&nbsp; Strange gods



<P>were addressed in condemnation of the early



<P>hours necessary to correct war.&nbsp; An officer's



<P>peremptory tenor rang out and quickened the



<P>stiffened movement of the men.&nbsp; The tangled



<P>limbs unraveled.&nbsp; The corpse-hued faces were



<P>hidden behind fists that twisted slowly in the eye



<P>sockets.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth sat up and gave vent to an enormous



<P>yawn.&nbsp; "Thunder!" he remarked petulantly.



<P>He rubbed his eyes, and then putting up his hand



<P>felt carefully of the bandage over his wound.



<P>His friend, perceiving him to be awake, came



<P>from the fire.&nbsp; "Well, Henry, ol' man, how do



<P>yeh feel this mornin'?" he demanded.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth yawned again.&nbsp; Then he puckered



<P>his mouth to a little pucker.&nbsp; His head, in truth,



<P>felt precisely like a melon, and there was an un-



<P>pleasant sensation at his stomach.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh, Lord, I feel pretty bad," he said.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Thunder!" exclaimed the other.&nbsp; "I hoped



<P>ye'd feel all right this mornin'.&nbsp; Let's see th'



<P>bandage--I guess it's slipped."&nbsp; He began to



<P>tinker at the wound in rather a clumsy way until



<P>the youth exploded.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Gosh-dern it!" he said in sharp irritation;



<P>"you're the hangdest man I ever saw!&nbsp; You



<P>wear muffs on your hands.&nbsp; Why in good



<P>thunderation can't you be more easy?&nbsp; I'd rather



<P>you'd stand off an' throw guns at it.&nbsp; Now, go



<P>slow, an' don't act as if you was nailing down



<P>carpet."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He glared with insolent command at his



<P>friend, but the latter answered soothingly.



<P>"Well, well, come now, an' git some grub," he



<P>said.&nbsp; "Then, maybe, yeh'll feel better."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At the fireside the loud young soldier



<P>watched over his comrade's wants with tender-



<P>ness and care.&nbsp; He was very busy marshaling



<P>the little black vagabonds of tin cups and pour-



<P>ing into them the streaming, iron colored mixture



<P>from a small and sooty tin pail.&nbsp; He had some



<P>fresh meat, which he roasted hurriedly upon a



<P>stick.&nbsp; He sat down then and contemplated the



<P>youth's appetite with glee.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth took note of a remarkable change



<P>in his comrade since those days of camp life upon



<P>the river bank.&nbsp; He seemed no more to be con-



<P>tinually regarding the proportions of his personal



<P>prowess.&nbsp; He was not furious at small words that



<P>pricked his conceits.&nbsp; He was no more a loud



<P>young soldier.&nbsp; There was about him now a



<P>fine reliance.&nbsp; He showed a quiet belief in



<P>his purposes and his abilities.&nbsp; And this in-



<P>ward confidence evidently enabled him to be



<P>indifferent to little words of other men aimed



<P>at him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth reflected.&nbsp; He had been used to



<P>regarding his comrade as a blatant child with an



<P>audacity grown from his inexperience, thought-



<P>less, headstrong, jealous, and filled with a tinsel



<P>courage.&nbsp; A swaggering babe accustomed to strut



<P>in his own dooryard.&nbsp; The youth wondered



<P>where had been born these new eyes; when his



<P>comrade had made the great discovery that



<P>there were many men who would refuse to be



<P>subjected by him.&nbsp; Apparently, the other had



<P>now climbed a peak of wisdom from which he



<P>could perceive himself as a very wee thing.&nbsp; And



<P>the youth saw that ever after it would be easier



<P>to live in his friend's neighborhood.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His comrade balanced his ebony coffee-cup on



<P>his knee.&nbsp; "Well, Henry," he said, "what d'yeh



<P>think th' chances are?&nbsp; D'yeh think we'll wal-



<P>lop 'em?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth considered for a moment.&nbsp; "Day-



<P>b'fore-yesterday," he finally replied, with boldness,



<P>"you would 'a' bet you'd lick the hull kit-an'-



<P>boodle all by yourself."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His friend looked a trifle amazed.&nbsp; "Would



<P>I?" he asked.&nbsp; He pondered.&nbsp; "Well, perhaps I



<P>would," he decided at last.&nbsp; He stared humbly at



<P>the fire.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth was quite disconcerted at this sur-



<P>prising reception of his remarks.&nbsp; "Oh, no, you



<P>wouldn't either," he said, hastily trying to re-



<P>trace.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But the other made a deprecating gesture.



<P>"Oh, yeh needn't mind, Henry," he said.&nbsp; "I be-



<P>lieve I was a pretty big fool in those days."&nbsp; He



<P>spoke as after a lapse of years.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was a little pause.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"All th' officers say we've got th' rebs in



<P>a pretty tight box," said the friend, clearing



<P>his throat in a commonplace way.&nbsp; "They all



<P>seem t' think we've got 'em jest where we



<P>want 'em."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I don't know about that," the youth replied.



<P>"What I seen over on th' right makes me think



<P>it was th' other way about.&nbsp; From where I was,



<P>it looked as if we was gettin' a good poundin'



<P>yestirday."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"D'yeh think so?" inquired the friend.&nbsp; "I



<P>thought we handled 'em pretty rough yestir-



<P>day."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Not a bit," said the youth.&nbsp; "Why, lord,



<P>man, you didn't see nothing of the fight.&nbsp; Why!"



<P>Then a sudden thought came to him.&nbsp; "Oh!



<P>Jim Conklin's dead."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His friend started.&nbsp; "What?&nbsp; Is he?&nbsp; Jim



<P>Conklin?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth spoke slowly.&nbsp; "Yes.&nbsp; He's dead.



<P>Shot in th' side."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yeh don't say so.&nbsp; Jim Conklin. . . . poor



<P>cuss!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>All about them were other small fires sur-



<P>rounded by men with their little black utensils.



<P>From one of these near came sudden sharp



<P>voices in a row.&nbsp; It appeared that two light-



<P>footed soldiers had been teasing a huge, bearded



<P>man, causing him to spill coffee upon his blue



<P>knees.&nbsp; The man had gone into a rage and had



<P>sworn comprehensively.&nbsp; Stung by his language,



<P>his tormentors had immediately bristled at him



<P>with a great show of resenting unjust oaths.



<P>Possibly there was going to be a fight.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The friend arose and went over to them, mak-



<P>ing pacific motions with his arms.&nbsp; "Oh, here,



<P>now, boys, what's th' use?" he said.&nbsp; "We'll



<P>be at th' rebs in less'n an hour.&nbsp; What's th'



<P>good fightin' 'mong ourselves?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>One of the light-footed soldiers turned upon



<P>him red-faced and violent.&nbsp; "Yeh needn't come



<P>around here with yer preachin'.&nbsp; I s'pose yeh



<P>don't approve 'a fightin' since Charley Morgan



<P>licked yeh; but I don't see what business this



<P>here is 'a yours or anybody else."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, it ain't," said the friend mildly.&nbsp; "Still



<P>I hate t' see--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was a tangled argument.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, he--," said the two, indicating their



<P>opponent with accusative forefingers.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The huge soldier was quite purple with rage.



<P>He pointed at the two soldiers with his great



<P>hand, extended clawlike.&nbsp; "Well, they--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But during this argumentative time the de-



<P>sire to deal blows seemed to pass, although they



<P>said much to each other.&nbsp; Finally the friend re-



<P>turned to his old seat.&nbsp; In a short while the



<P>three antagonists could be seen together in an



<P>amiable bunch.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Jimmie Rogers ses I'll have t' fight him



<P>after th' battle t'-day," announced the friend as



<P>he again seated himself.&nbsp; "He ses he don't



<P>allow no interferin' in his business.&nbsp; I hate t' see



<P>th' boys fightin' 'mong themselves."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth laughed.&nbsp; "Yer changed a good



<P>bit.&nbsp; Yeh ain't at all like yeh was.&nbsp; I remember



<P>when you an' that Irish feller--"&nbsp; He stopped



<P>and laughed again.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No, I didn't use t' be that way," said his



<P>friend thoughtfully.&nbsp; "That's true 'nough."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, I didn't mean--" began the youth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The friend made another deprecatory gesture.



<P>"Oh, yeh needn't mind, Henry."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was another little pause.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Th' reg'ment lost over half th' men yestir-



<P>day," remarked the friend eventually.&nbsp; "I thought



<P>a course they was all dead, but, laws, they kep'



<P>a-comin' back last night until it seems, after all,



<P>we didn't lose but a few.&nbsp; They'd been scattered



<P>all over, wanderin' around in th' woods, fightin'



<P>with other reg'ments, an' everything.&nbsp; Jest like



<P>you done."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"So?" said the youth.



<P>

<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <TITLE>chapter16</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XVI.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A SPUTTERING of musketry was always to be



<P>heard.&nbsp; Later, the cannon had entered the dis-



<P>pute.&nbsp; In the fog-filled air their voices made a



<P>thudding sound.&nbsp; The reverberations were con-



<P>tinued.&nbsp; This part of the world led a strange,



<P>battleful existence.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth's regiment was marched to relieve



<P>a command that had lain long in some damp



<P>trenches.&nbsp; The men took positions behind a curv-



<P>ing line of rifle pits that had been turned up, like



<P>a large furrow, along the line of woods.&nbsp; Before



<P>them was a level stretch, peopled with short,



<P>deformed stumps.&nbsp; From the woods beyond



<P>came the dull popping of the skirmishers and



<P>pickets, firing in the fog.&nbsp; From the right came



<P>the noise of a terrific fracas.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The men cuddled behind the small embank-



<P>ment and sat in easy attitudes awaiting their



<P>turn.&nbsp; Many had their backs to the firing.&nbsp; The



<P>youth's friend lay down, buried his face in his

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>154



<P>arms, and almost instantly, it seemed, he was in a



<P>deep sleep.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth leaned his breast against the



<P>brown dirt and peered over at the woods and up



<P>and down the line.&nbsp; Curtains of trees interfered



<P>with his ways of vision.&nbsp; He could see the low



<P>line of trenches but for a short distance.&nbsp; A few



<P>idle flags were perched on the dirt hills.&nbsp; Behind



<P>them were rows of dark bodies with a few heads



<P>sticking curiously over the top.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Always the noise of skirmishers came from



<P>the woods on the front and left, and the din on



<P>the right had grown to frightful proportions.



<P>The guns were roaring without an instant's pause



<P>for breath.&nbsp; It seemed that the cannon had come



<P>from all parts and were engaged in a stupendous



<P>wrangle.&nbsp; It became impossible to make a sen-



<P>tence heard.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth wished to launch a joke--a quota-



<P>tion from newspapers.&nbsp; He desired to say, "All



<P>quiet on the Rappahannock," but the guns refused



<P>to permit even a comment upon their uproar.



<P>He never successfully concluded the sentence.



<P>But at last the guns stopped, and among the



<P>men in the rifle pits rumors again flew, like birds,



<P>but they were now for the most part black



<P>creatures who flapped their wings drearily near



<P>to the ground and refused to rise on any wings of



<P>hope.&nbsp; The men's faces grew doleful from the



<P>interpreting of omens.&nbsp; Tales of hesitation and



<P>uncertainty on the part of those high in place and



<P>responsibility came to their ears.&nbsp; Stories of



<P>disaster were borne into their minds with many



<P>proofs.&nbsp; This din of musketry on the right, grow-



<P>ing like a released genie of sound, expressed and



<P>emphasized the army's plight.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The men were disheartened and began to



<P>mutter.&nbsp; They made gestures expressive of the



<P>sentence: "Ah, what more can we do?"&nbsp; And it



<P>could always be seen that they were bewildered



<P>by the alleged news and could not fully compre-



<P>hend a defeat.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Before the gray mists had been totally ob-



<P>literated by the sun rays, the regiment was march-



<P>ing in a spread column that was retiring carefully



<P>through the woods.&nbsp; The disordered, hurrying



<P>lines of the enemy could sometimes be seen down



<P>through the groves and little fields.&nbsp; They were



<P>yelling, shrill and exultant.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At this sight the youth forgot many personal



<P>matters and became greatly enraged.&nbsp; He ex-



<P>ploded in loud sentences.&nbsp; "B'jiminey, we're



<P>generaled by a lot 'a lunkheads."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"More than one feller has said that t'-day,"



<P>observed a man.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His friend, recently aroused, was still very



<P>drowsy.&nbsp; He looked behind him until his mind



<P>took in the meaning of the movement.&nbsp; Then he



<P>sighed.&nbsp; "Oh, well, I s'pose we got licked," he



<P>remarked sadly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth had a thought that it would not be



<P>handsome for him to freely condemn other men.



<P>He made an attempt to restrain himself, but the



<P>words upon his tongue were too bitter.&nbsp; He



<P>presently began a long and intricate denunciation



<P>of the commander of the forces.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Mebbe, it wa'n't all his fault--not all to-



<P>gether.&nbsp; He did th' best he knowed.&nbsp; It's our



<P>luck t' git licked often," said his friend in a weary



<P>tone.&nbsp; He was trudging along with stooped



<P>shoulders and shifting eyes like a man who has



<P>been caned and kicked.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, don't we fight like the devil?&nbsp; Don't



<P>we do all that men can?" demanded the youth



<P>loudly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He was secretly dumfounded at this sentiment



<P>when it came from his lips.&nbsp; For a moment his



<P>face lost its valor and he looked guiltily about



<P>him.&nbsp; But no one questioned his right to deal in



<P>such words, and presently he recovered his air



<P>of courage.&nbsp; He went on to repeat a statement



<P>he had heard going from group to group at the



<P>camp that morning.&nbsp; "The brigadier said he



<P>never saw a new reg'ment fight the way we



<P>fought yestirday, didn't he?&nbsp; And we didn't do



<P>better than many another reg'ment, did we?



<P>Well, then, you can't say it's th' army's fault, can



<P>you?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In his reply, the friend's voice was stern.&nbsp; "'A



<P>course not," he said.&nbsp; "No man dare say we



<P>don't fight like th' devil.&nbsp; No man will ever dare



<P>say it.&nbsp; Th' boys fight like hell-roosters.&nbsp; But



<P>still--still, we don't have no luck."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, then, if we fight like the devil an'



<P>don't ever whip, it must be the general's fault,"



<P>said the youth grandly and decisively.&nbsp; "And I



<P>don't see any sense in fighting and fighting and



<P>fighting, yet always losing through some derned



<P>old lunkhead of a general."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A sarcastic man who was tramping at the



<P>youth's side, then spoke lazily.&nbsp; "Mebbe yeh



<P>think yeh fit th' hull battle yestirday, Fleming,"



<P>he remarked.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The speech pierced the youth.&nbsp; Inwardly he



<P>was reduced to an abject pulp by these chance



<P>words.&nbsp; His legs quaked privately.&nbsp; He cast a



<P>frightened glance at the sarcastic man.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Why, no," he hastened to say in a concili-



<P>ating voice, "I don't think I fought the whole



<P>battle yesterday."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But the other seemed innocent of any deeper



<P>meaning.&nbsp; Apparently, he had no information.



<P>It was merely his habit.&nbsp; "Oh!" he replied in the



<P>same tone of calm derision.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth, nevertheless, felt a threat.&nbsp; His



<P>mind shrank from going near to the danger, and



<P>thereafter he was silent.&nbsp; The significance of the



<P>sarcastic man's words took from him all loud



<P>moods that would make him appear prominent.



<P>He became suddenly a modest person.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was low-toned talk among the troops.



<P>The officers were impatient and snappy, their



<P>countenances clouded with the tales of misfor-



<P>tune.&nbsp; The troops, sifting through the forest,



<P>were sullen.&nbsp; In the youth's company once a



<P>man's laugh rang out.&nbsp; A dozen soldiers turned



<P>their faces quickly toward him and frowned with



<P>vague displeasure.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The noise of firing dogged their footsteps.



<P>Sometimes, it seemed to be driven a little way,



<P>but it always returned again with increased



<P>insolence.&nbsp; The men muttered and cursed,



<P>throwing black looks in its direction.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In a clear space the troops were at last halted.



<P>Regiments and brigades, broken and detached



<P>through their encounters with thickets, grew



<P>together again and lines were faced toward the



<P>pursuing bark of the enemy's infantry.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>This noise, following like the yellings of eager,



<P>metallic hounds, increased to a loud and joyous



<P>burst, and then, as the sun went serenely up the



<P>sky, throwing illuminating rays into the gloomy



<P>thickets, it broke forth into prolonged pealings.



<P>The woods began to crackle as if afire.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Whoop-a-dadee," said a man, "here we are!



<P>Everybody fightin'.&nbsp; Blood an' destruction."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I was willin' t' bet they'd attack as soon as



<P>th' sun got fairly up," savagely asserted the



<P>lieutenant who commanded the youth's company.



<P>He jerked without mercy at his little mustache.



<P>He strode to and fro with dark dignity in the



<P>rear of his men, who were lying down behind



<P>whatever protection they had collected.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A battery had trundled into position in the



<P>rear and was thoughtfully shelling the distance.



<P>The regiment, unmolested as yet, awaited the



<P>moment when the gray shadows of the woods



<P>before them should be slashed by the lines of



<P>flame.&nbsp; There was much growling and swearing.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Good Gawd," the youth grumbled, "we're



<P>always being chased around like rats!&nbsp; It makes



<P>me sick.&nbsp; Nobody seems to know where we go



<P>or why we go.&nbsp; We just get fired around from



<P>pillar to post and get licked here and get licked



<P>there, and nobody knows what it's done for.&nbsp; It



<P>makes a man feel like a damn' kitten in a bag.



<P>Now, I'd like to know what the eternal thunders



<P>we was marched into these woods for anyhow,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>THE RED BADGE OF COURAGE 161

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>unless it was to give the rebs a regular pot shot



<P>at us.&nbsp; We came in here and got our legs all



<P>tangled up in these cussed briers, and then we



<P>begin to fight and the rebs had an easy time of it.



<P>Don't tell me it's just luck!&nbsp; I know better.&nbsp; It's



<P>this derned old--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The friend seemed jaded, but he interrupted



<P>his comrade with a voice of calm confidence.



<P>"It'll turn out all right in th' end," he said.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh, the devil it will!&nbsp; You always talk like a



<P>dog-hanged parson.&nbsp; Don't tell me!&nbsp; I know--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At this time there was an interposition by the



<P>savage-minded lieutenant, who was obliged to



<P>vent some of his inward dissatisfaction upon his



<P>men.&nbsp; "You boys shut right up!&nbsp; There no



<P>need 'a your wastin' your breath in long-winded



<P>arguments about this an' that an' th' other.



<P>You've been jawin' like a lot 'a old hens.&nbsp; All



<P>you've got t' do is to fight, an' you'll get plenty 'a



<P>that t' do in about ten minutes.&nbsp; Less talkin' an'



<P>more fightin' is what's best for you boys.&nbsp; I never



<P>saw sech gabbling jackasses."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He paused, ready to pounce upon any man



<P>who might have the temerity to reply.&nbsp; No words



<P>being said, he resumed his dignified pacing.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"There's too much chin music an' too little



<P>fightin' in this war, anyhow," he said to them,



<P>turning his head for a final remark.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The day had grown more white, until the sun



<P>shed his full radiance upon the thronged forest.



<P>A sort of a gust of battle came sweeping toward



<P>that part of the line where lay the youth's regi-



<P>ment.&nbsp; The front shifted a trifle to meet it square-



<P>ly.&nbsp; There was a wait.&nbsp; In this part of the field



<P>there passed slowly the intense moments that pre-



<P>cede the tempest.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A single rifle flashed in a thicket before the



<P>regiment.&nbsp; In an instant it was joined by many



<P>others.&nbsp; There was a mighty song of clashes and



<P>crashes that went sweeping through the woods.



<P>The guns in the rear, aroused and enraged by



<P>shells that had been thrown burlike at them,



<P>suddenly involved themselves in a hideous alter-



<P>cation with another band of guns.&nbsp; The battle



<P>roar settled to a rolling thunder, which was a



<P>single, long explosion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In the regiment there was a peculiar kind of



<P>hesitation denoted in the attitudes of the men.



<P>They were worn, exhausted, having slept but lit-



<P>tle and labored much.&nbsp; They rolled their eyes



<P>toward the advancing battle as they stood await-



<P>ing the shock.&nbsp; Some shrank and flinched.&nbsp; They



<P>stood as men tied to stakes.



<P>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">
