<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER II.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>THE next morning the youth discovered that



<P>his tall comrade had been the fast-flying messen-



<P>ger of a mistake.&nbsp; There was much scoffing at



<P>the latter by those who had yesterday been firm



<P>adherents of his views, and there was even a lit-



<P>tle sneering by men who had never believed the



<P>rumor.&nbsp; The tall one fought with a man from



<P>Chatfield Corners and beat him severely.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth felt, however, that his problem was



<P>in no wise lifted from him.&nbsp; There was, on the



<P>contrary, an irritating prolongation.&nbsp; The tale



<P>had created in him a great concern for himself.



<P>Now, with the newborn question in his mind, he



<P>was compelled to sink back into his old place as



<P>part of a blue demonstration.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>For days he made ceaseless calculations, but



<P>they were all wondrously unsatisfactory.&nbsp; He



<P>found that he could establish nothing.&nbsp; He final-



<P>ly concluded that the only way to prove himself



<P>was to go into the blaze, and then figuratively to

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>18



<P>watch his legs to discover their merits and faults.



<P>He reluctantly admitted that he could not sit



<P>still and with a mental slate and pencil derive an



<P>answer.&nbsp; To gain it, he must have blaze, blood,



<P>and danger, even as a chemist requires this, that,



<P>and the other.&nbsp; So he fretted for an opportunity.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Meanwhile he continually tried to measure



<P>himself by his comrades.&nbsp; The tall soldier, for



<P>one, gave him some assurance.&nbsp; This man's se-



<P>rene unconcern dealt him a measure of con-



<P>fidence, for he had known him since childhood,



<P>and from his intimate knowledge he did not see



<P>how he could be capable of anything that was



<P>beyond him, the youth.&nbsp; Still, he thought that



<P>his comrade might be mistaken about himself.



<P>Or, on the other hand, he might be a man here-



<P>tofore doomed to peace and obscurity, but, in



<P>reality, made to shine in war.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth would have liked to have discov-



<P>ered another who suspected himself.&nbsp; A sympa-



<P>thetic comparison of mental notes would have



<P>been a joy to him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He occasionally tried to fathom a comrade



<P>with seductive sentences.&nbsp; He looked about to



<P>find men in the proper mood.&nbsp; All attempts



<P>failed to bring forth any statement which looked



<P>in any way like a confession to those doubts



<P>which he privately acknowledged in himself.



<P>He was afraid to make an open declaration of



<P>his concern, because he dreaded to place some



<P>unscrupulous confidant upon the high plane of



<P>the unconfessed from which elevation he could



<P>be derided.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In regard to his companions his mind wa-



<P>vered between two opinions, according to his



<P>mood.&nbsp; Sometimes he inclined to believing them



<P>all heroes.&nbsp; In fact, he usually admitted in secret



<P>the superior development of the higher qualities



<P>in others.&nbsp; He could conceive of men going very



<P>insignificantly about the world bearing a load of



<P>courage unseen, and although he had known



<P>many of his comrades through boyhood, he be-



<P>gan to fear that his judgment of them had been



<P>blind.&nbsp; Then, in other moments, he flouted these



<P>theories, and assured himself that his fellows



<P>were all privately wondering and quaking.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His emotions made him feel strange in the



<P>presence of men who talked excitedly of a pro-



<P>spective battle as of a drama they were about to



<P>witness, with nothing but eagerness and curiosity



<P>apparent in their faces.&nbsp; It was often that he sus-



<P>pected them to be liars.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He did not pass such thoughts without severe



<P>condemnation of himself.&nbsp; He dinned reproaches



<P>at times.&nbsp; He was convicted by himself of many



<P>shameful crimes against the gods of traditions.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In his great anxiety his heart was continually



<P>clamoring at what he considered the intolerable



<P>slowness of the generals.&nbsp; They seemed content



<P>to perch tranquilly on the river bank, and leave



<P>him bowed down by the weight of a great prob-



<P>lem.&nbsp; He wanted it settled forthwith.&nbsp; He could



<P>not long bear such a load, he said.&nbsp; Sometimes



<P>his anger at the commanders reached an acute



<P>stage, and he grumbled about the camp like a



<P>veteran.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>One morning, however, he found himself in



<P>the ranks of his prepared regiment.&nbsp; The men



<P>were whispering speculations and recounting the



<P>old rumors.&nbsp; In the gloom before the break of



<P>the day their uniforms glowed a deep purple



<P>hue.&nbsp; From across the river the red eyes were



<P>still peering.&nbsp; In the eastern sky there was a yel-



<P>low patch like a rug laid for the feet of the com-



<P>ing sun; and against it, black and patternlike,



<P>loomed the gigantic figure of the colonel on a



<P>gigantic horse.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>From off in the darkness came the trampling



<P>of feet.&nbsp; The youth could occasionally see dark



<P>shadows that moved like monsters.&nbsp; The regi-



<P>ment stood at rest for what seemed a long time.



<P>The youth grew impatient.&nbsp; It was unendurable



<P>the way these affairs were managed.&nbsp; He won-



<P>dered how long they were to be kept waiting.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As he looked all about him and pondered



<P>upon the mystic gloom, he began to believe that



<P>at any moment the ominous distance might be



<P>aflare, and the rolling crashes of an engagement



<P>come to his ears.&nbsp; Staring once at the red eyes



<P>across the river, he conceived them to be grow-



<P>ing larger, as the orbs of a row of dragons ad-



<P>vancing.&nbsp; He turned toward the colonel and saw



<P>him lift his gigantic arm and calmly stroke his



<P>mustache.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At last he heard from along the road at the



<P>foot of the hill the clatter of a horse's galloping



<P>hoofs.&nbsp; It must be the coming of orders.&nbsp; He



<P>bent forward, scarce breathing.&nbsp; The exciting



<P>clickety-click, as it grew louder and louder,



<P>seemed to be beating upon his soul.&nbsp; Presently a



<P>horseman with jangling equipment drew rein be-



<P>fore the colonel of the regiment.&nbsp; The two held



<P>a short, sharp-worded conversation.&nbsp; The men in



<P>the foremost ranks craned their necks.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As the horseman wheeled his animal and gal-



<P>loped away he turned to shout over his shoulder,



<P>"Don't forget that box of cigars!"&nbsp; The colonel



<P>mumbled in reply.&nbsp; The youth wondered what a



<P>box of cigars had to do with war.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A moment later the regiment went swinging



<P>off into the darkness.&nbsp; It was now like one of



<P>those moving monsters wending with many feet.



<P>The air was heavy, and cold with dew.&nbsp; A mass



<P>of wet grass, marched upon, rustled like silk.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was an occasional flash and glimmer



<P>of steel from the backs of all these huge crawl-



<P>ing reptiles.&nbsp; From the road came creakings and



<P>grumblings as some surly guns were dragged



<P>away.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The men stumbled along still muttering specu-



<P>lations.&nbsp; There was a subdued debate.&nbsp; Once a



<P>man fell down, and as he reached for his rifle a



<P>comrade, unseeing, trod upon his hand.&nbsp; He of



<P>the injured fingers swore bitterly and aloud.&nbsp; A



<P>low, tittering laugh went among his fellows.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Presently they passed into a roadway and



<P>marched forward with easy strides.&nbsp; A dark



<P>regiment moved before them, and from behind



<P>also came the tinkle of equipments on the bodies



<P>of marching men.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The rushing yellow of the developing day



<P>went on behind their backs.&nbsp; When the sunrays



<P>at last struck full and mellowingly upon the



<P>earth, the youth saw that the landscape was



<P>streaked with two long, thin, black columns



<P>which disappeared on the brow of a hill in front



<P>and rearward vanished in a wood.&nbsp; They were



<P>like two serpents crawling from the cavern of the



<P>night.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The river was not in view.&nbsp; The tall soldier



<P>burst into praises of what he thought to be his



<P>powers of perception.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Some of the tall one's companions cried with



<P>emphasis that they, too, had evolved the same



<P>thing, and they congratulated themselves upon



<P>it.&nbsp; But there were others who said that the tall



<P>one's plan was not the true one at all.&nbsp; They per-



<P>sisted with other theories.&nbsp; There was a vigorous



<P>discussion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth took no part in them.&nbsp; As he



<P>walked along in careless line he was engaged



<P>with his own eternal debate.&nbsp; He could not hin-



<P>der himself from dwelling upon it.&nbsp; He was de-



<P>spondent and sullen, and threw shifting glances



<P>about him.&nbsp; He looked ahead, often expecting to



<P>hear from the advance the rattle of firing.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But the long serpents crawled slowly from



<P>hill to hill without bluster of smoke.&nbsp; A dun-col-



<P>ored cloud of dust floated away to the right.



<P>The sky overhead was of a fairy blue.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth studied the faces of his compan-



<P>ions, ever on the watch to detect kindred emo-



<P>tions.&nbsp; He suffered disappointment.&nbsp; Some ardor



<P>of the air which was causing the veteran com-



<P>mands to move with glee--almost with song--



<P>had infected the new regiment.&nbsp; The men began



<P>to speak of victory as of a thing they knew.



<P>Also, the tall soldier received his vindication.



<P>They were certainly going to come around in



<P>behind the enemy.&nbsp; They expressed commisera-



<P>tion for that part of the army which had been



<P>left upon the river bank, felicitating themselves



<P>upon being a part of a blasting host.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth, considering himself as separated



<P>from the others, was saddened by the blithe and



<P>merry speeches that went from rank to rank.



<P>The company wags all made their best endeav-



<P>ors.&nbsp; The regiment tramped to the tune of



<P>laughter.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The blatant soldier often convulsed whole



<P>files by his biting sarcasms aimed at the tall one.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>And it was not long before all the men seemed



<P>to forget their mission.&nbsp; Whole brigades grinned



<P>in unison, and regiments laughed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A rather fat soldier attempted to pilfer a horse



<P>from a dooryard.&nbsp; He planned to load his knap-



<P>sack upon it.&nbsp; He was escaping with his prize



<P>when a young girl rushed from the house and



<P>grabbed the animal's mane.&nbsp; There followed a



<P>wrangle.&nbsp; The young girl, with pink cheeks and



<P>shining eyes, stood like a dauntless statue.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The observant regiment, standing at rest in



<P>the roadway, whooped at once, and entered



<P>whole-souled upon the side of the maiden.&nbsp; The



<P>men became so engrossed in this affair that they



<P>entirely ceased to remember their own large war.



<P>They jeered the piratical private, and called



<P>attention to various defects in his personal ap-



<P>pearance; and they were wildly enthusiastic in



<P>support of the young girl.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>To her, from some distance, came bold advice.



<P>"Hit him with a stick."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There were crows and catcalls showered



<P>upon him when he retreated without the horse.



<P>The regiment rejoiced at his downfall.&nbsp; Loud



<P>and vociferous congratulations were showered



<P>upon the maiden, who stood panting and regard-



<P>ing the troops with defiance.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At nightfall the column broke into regimental



<P>pieces, and the fragments went into the fields to



<P>camp.&nbsp; Tents sprang up like strange plants.



<P>Camp fires, like red, peculiar blossoms, dotted



<P>the night.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth kept from intercourse with his



<P>companions as much as circumstances would



<P>allow him.&nbsp; In the evening he wandered a few



<P>paces into the gloom.&nbsp; From this little distance



<P>the many fires, with the black forms of men pass-



<P>ing to and fro before the crimson rays, made



<P>weird and satanic effects.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He lay down in the grass.&nbsp; The blades



<P>pressed tenderly against his cheek.&nbsp; The moon



<P>had been lighted and was hung in a treetop.



<P>The liquid stillness of the night enveloping him



<P>made him feel vast pity for himself.&nbsp; There was



<P>a caress in the soft winds; and the whole mood



<P>of the darkness, he thought, was one of sympathy



<P>for himself in his distress.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He wished, without reserve, that he was at



<P>home again making the endless rounds from the



<P>house to the barn, from the barn to the fields,



<P>from the fields to the barn, from the barn to the



<P>house.&nbsp; He remembered he had often cursed the



<P>brindle cow and her mates, and had sometimes



<P>flung milking stools.&nbsp; But, from his present point



<P>of view, there was a halo of happiness about each



<P>of their heads, and he would have sacrificed all



<P>the brass buttons on the continent to have been



<P>enabled to return to them.&nbsp; He told himself that



<P>he was not formed for a soldier.&nbsp; And he mused



<P>seriously upon the radical differences between



<P>himself and those men who were dodging imp-



<P>like around the fires.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As he mused thus he heard the rustle of grass,



<P>and, upon turning his head, discovered the loud



<P>soldier.&nbsp; He called out, "Oh, Wilson!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The latter approached and looked down.



<P>"Why, hello, Henry; is it you?&nbsp; What you do-



<P>ing here?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh, thinking," said the youth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The other sat down and carefully lighted his



<P>pipe.&nbsp; "You're getting blue, my boy.&nbsp; You're



<P>looking thundering peeked.&nbsp; What the dickens



<P>is wrong with you?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh, nothing," said the youth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The loud soldier launched then into the sub-



<P>ject of the anticipated fight.&nbsp; "Oh, we've got



<P>'em now!"&nbsp; As he spoke his boyish face was



<P>wreathed in a gleeful smile, and his voice had



<P>an exultant ring.&nbsp; "We've got 'em now.&nbsp; At



<P>last, by the eternal thunders, we'll lick 'em



<P>good!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"If the truth was known," he added, more



<P>soberly, "THEY'VE licked US about every clip up to



<P>now; but this time--this time--we'll lick 'em



<P>good!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I thought you was objecting to this march



<P>a little while ago," said the youth coldly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh, it wasn't that," explained the other.&nbsp; "I



<P>don't mind marching, if there's going to be fight-



<P>ing at the end of it.&nbsp; What I hate is this getting



<P>moved here and moved there, with no good com-



<P>ing of it, as far as I can see, excepting sore feet



<P>and damned short rations."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, Jim Conklin says we'll get a plenty of



<P>fighting this time."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"He's right for once, I guess, though I can't



<P>see how it come.&nbsp; This time we're in for a big



<P>battle, and we've got the best end of it, certain



<P>sure.&nbsp; Gee rod! how we will thump 'em!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He arose and began to pace to and fro excit-



<P>edly.&nbsp; The thrill of his enthusiasm made him



<P>walk with an elastic step.&nbsp; He was sprightly,



<P>vigorous, fiery in his belief in success.&nbsp; He



<P>looked into the future with clear, proud eye, and



<P>he swore with the air of an old soldier.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth watched him for a moment in



<P>silence.&nbsp; When he finally spoke his voice was as



<P>bitter as dregs.&nbsp; "Oh, you're going to do great



<P>things, I s'pose!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The loud soldier blew a thoughtful cloud of



<P>smoke from his pipe.&nbsp; "Oh, I don't know," he



<P>remarked with dignity; "I don't know.&nbsp; I s'pose



<P>I'll do as well as the rest.&nbsp; I'm going to try like



<P>thunder."&nbsp; He evidently complimented himself



<P>upon the modesty of this statement.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"How do you know you won't run when the



<P>time comes?" asked the youth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Run?" said the loud one; "run?--of course



<P>not!"&nbsp; He laughed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well," continued the youth, "lots of good-



<P>a-'nough men have thought they was going to do



<P>great things before the fight, but when the time



<P>come they skedaddled."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh, that's all true, I s'pose," replied the



<P>other; "but I'm not going to skedaddle.&nbsp; The



<P>man that bets on my running will lose his money,



<P>that's all."&nbsp; He nodded confidently.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh, shucks!" said the youth.&nbsp; "You ain't



<P>the bravest man in the world, are you?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No, I ain't," exclaimed the loud soldier in-



<P>dignantly; "and I didn't say I was the bravest



<P>man in the world, neither.&nbsp; I said I was going to



<P>do my share of fighting--that's what I said.&nbsp; And



<P>I am, too.&nbsp; Who are you, anyhow.&nbsp; You talk as



<P>if you thought you was Napoleon Bonaparte."



<P>He glared at the youth for a moment, and then



<P>strode away.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth called in a savage voice after his



<P>comrade: "Well, you needn't git mad about it!"



<P>But the other continued on his way and made no



<P>reply.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He felt alone in space when his injured com-



<P>rade had disappeared.&nbsp; His failure to discover



<P>any mite of resemblance in their view points



<P>made him more miserable than before.&nbsp; No one



<P>seemed to be wrestling with such a terrific per-



<P>sonal problem.&nbsp; He was a mental outcast.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He went slowly to his tent and stretched him-



<P>self on a blanket by the side of the snoring tall



<P>soldier.&nbsp; In the darkness he saw visions of a thou-



<P>sand-tongued fear that would babble at his back



<P>and cause him to flee, while others were going



<P>coolly about their country's business.&nbsp; He admit-



<P>ted that he would not be able to cope with this



<P>monster.&nbsp; He felt that every nerve in his body



<P>would be an ear to hear the voices, while other



<P>men would remain stolid and deaf.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>And as he sweated with the pain of these



<P>thoughts, he could hear low, serene sentences.



<P>"I'll bid five."&nbsp; "Make it six."&nbsp; "Seven."



<P>"Seven goes."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He stared at the red, shivering reflection of



<P>a fire on the white wall of his tent until, ex-



<P>hausted and ill from the monotony of his suf-



<P>fering, he fell asleep.



<P>

<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER III.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>WHEN another night came the columns,



<P>changed to purple streaks, filed across two pon-



<P>toon bridges.&nbsp; A glaring fire wine-tinted the



<P>waters of the river.&nbsp; Its rays, shining upon the



<P>moving masses of troops, brought forth here and



<P>there sudden gleams of silver or gold.&nbsp; Upon



<P>the other shore a dark and mysterious range of



<P>hills was curved against the sky.&nbsp; The insect



<P>voices of the night sang solemnly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After this crossing the youth assured himself



<P>that at any moment they might be suddenly and



<P>fearfully assaulted from the caves of the lowering



<P>woods.&nbsp; He kept his eyes watchfully upon the



<P>darkness.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But his regiment went unmolested to a camp-



<P>ing place, and its soldiers slept the brave sleep



<P>of wearied men.&nbsp; In the morning they were



<P>routed out with early energy, and hustled along



<P>a narrow road that led deep into the forest.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It was during this rapid march that the regiment

<BR>lost many of the marks of a new com-



<P>mand.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The men had begun to count the miles upon



<P>their fingers, and they grew tired.&nbsp; "Sore feet



<P>an' damned short rations, that's all," said the



<P>loud soldier.&nbsp; There was perspiration and grum-



<P>blings.&nbsp; After a time they began to shed their



<P>knapsacks.&nbsp; Some tossed them unconcernedly



<P>down; others hid them carefully, asserting their



<P>plans to return for them at some convenient



<P>time.&nbsp; Men extricated themselves from thick



<P>shirts.&nbsp; Presently few carried anything but their



<P>necessary clothing, blankets, haversacks, canteens,



<P>and arms and ammunition.&nbsp; "You can now eat



<P>and shoot," said the tall soldier to the youth.



<P>"That's all you want to do."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was sudden change from the ponderous



<P>infantry of theory to the light and speedy infantry



<P>of practice.&nbsp; The regiment, relieved of a burden,



<P>received a new impetus.&nbsp; But there was much



<P>loss of valuable knapsacks, and, on the whole,



<P>very good shirts.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But the regiment was not yet veteranlike in



<P>appearance.&nbsp; Veteran regiments in the army



<P>were likely to be very small aggregations of men.



<P>Once, when the command had first come to the



<P>field, some perambulating veterans, noting the



<P>length of their column, had accosted them thus:



<P>"Hey, fellers, what brigade is that?"&nbsp; And when



<P>the men had replied that they formed a regiment



<P>and not a brigade, the older soldiers had laughed,



<P>and said, "O Gawd!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Also, there was too great a similarity in the



<P>hats.&nbsp; The hats of a regiment should properly



<P>represent the history of headgear for a period of



<P>years.&nbsp; And, moreover, there were no letters of



<P>faded gold speaking from the colors.&nbsp; They were



<P>new and beautiful, and the color bearer habitu-



<P>ally oiled the pole.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Presently the army again sat down to think.



<P>The odor of the peaceful pines was in the men's



<P>nostrils.&nbsp; The sound of monotonous axe blows



<P>rang through the forest, and the insects, nodding



<P>upon their perches, crooned like old women.



<P>The youth returned to his theory of a blue dem-



<P>onstration.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>One gray dawn, however, he was kicked in



<P>the leg by the tall soldier, and then, before he



<P>was entirely awake, he found himself running



<P>down a wood road in the midst of men who were



<P>panting from the first effects of speed.&nbsp; His can-



<P>teen banged rhythmically upon his thigh, and his



<P>haversack bobbed softly.&nbsp; His musket bounced



<P>a trifle from his shoulder at each stride and made



<P>his cap feel uncertain upon his head.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He could hear the men whisper jerky sen-



<P>tences: "Say--what's all this--about?"&nbsp; "What



<P>th' thunder--we--skedaddlin' this way fer?"



<P>"Billie--keep off m' feet.&nbsp; Yeh run--like a cow."



<P>And the loud soldier's shrill voice could be



<P>heard: "What th' devil they in sich a hurry for?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth thought the damp fog of early



<P>morning moved from the rush of a great body



<P>of troops.&nbsp; From the distance came a sudden



<P>spatter of firing.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He was bewildered.&nbsp; As he ran with his com-



<P>rades he strenuously tried to think, but all he knew



<P>was that if he fell down those coming behind



<P>would tread upon him.&nbsp; All his faculties seemed



<P>to be needed to guide him over and past obstruc-



<P>tions.&nbsp; He felt carried along by a mob.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The sun spread disclosing rays, and, one by



<P>one, regiments burst into view like armed men



<P>just born of the earth.&nbsp; The youth perceived



<P>that the time had come.&nbsp; He was about to be



<P>measured.&nbsp; For a moment he felt in the face of



<P>his great trial like a babe, and the flesh over



<P>his heart seemed very thin.&nbsp; He seized time to



<P>look about him calculatingly.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But he instantly saw that it would be impossi-



<P>ble for him to escape from the regiment.&nbsp; It in-



<P>closed him.&nbsp; And there were iron laws of tradi-



<P>tion and law on four sides.&nbsp; He was in a moving



<P>box.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As he perceived this fact it occurred to him



<P>that he had never wished to come to the war.



<P>He had not enlisted of his free will.&nbsp; He had



<P>been dragged by the merciless government.&nbsp; And



<P>now they were taking him out to be slaughtered.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The regiment slid down a bank and wallowed



<P>across a little stream.&nbsp; The mournful current



<P>moved slowly on, and from the water, shaded



<P>black, some white bubble eyes looked at the men.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As they climbed the hill on the farther side



<P>artillery began to boom.&nbsp; Here the youth forgot



<P>many things as he felt a sudden impulse of curi-



<P>osity.&nbsp; He scrambled up the bank with a speed



<P>that could not be exceeded by a bloodthirsty



<P>man.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He expected a battle scene.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There were some little fields girted and



<P>squeezed by a forest.&nbsp; Spread over the grass and



<P>in among the tree trunks, he could see knots and



<P>waving lines of skirmishers who were running



<P>hither and thither and firing at the landscape.



<P>A dark battle line lay upon a sunstruck clearing



<P>that gleamed orange color.&nbsp; A flag fluttered.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Other regiments floundered up the bank.&nbsp; The



<P>brigade was formed in line of battle, and after a



<P>pause started slowly through the woods in the



<P>rear of the receding skirmishers, who were con-



<P>tinually melting into the scene to appear again



<P>farther on.&nbsp; They were always busy as bees,



<P>deeply absorbed in their little combats.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth tried to observe everything.&nbsp; He



<P>did not use care to avoid trees and branches,



<P>and his forgotten feet were constantly knocking



<P>against stones or getting entangled in briers.



<P>He was aware that these battalions with their



<P>commotions were woven red and startling into



<P>the gentle fabric of softened greens and browns.



<P>It looked to be a wrong place for a battle field.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The skirmishers in advance fascinated him.



<P>Their shots into thickets and at distant and



<P>prominent trees spoke to him of tragedies--hid-



<P>den, mysterious, solemn.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Once the line encountered the body of a dead



<P>soldier.&nbsp; He lay upon his back staring at the sky.



<P>He was dressed in an awkward suit of yellowish



<P>brown.&nbsp; The youth could see that the soles of his



<P>shoes had been worn to the thinness of writing



<P>paper, and from a great rent in one the dead foot



<P>projected piteously.&nbsp; And it was as if fate had



<P>betrayed the soldier.&nbsp; In death it exposed to his



<P>enemies that poverty which in life he had perhaps



<P>concealed from his friends.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The ranks opened covertly to avoid the corpse.



<P>The invulnerable dead man forced a way for him-



<P>self.&nbsp; The youth looked keenly at the ashen face.



<P>The wind raised the tawny beard.&nbsp; It moved as



<P>if a hand were stroking it.&nbsp; He vaguely desired



<P>to walk around and around the body and stare;



<P>the impulse of the living to try to read in dead



<P>eyes the answer to the Question.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>During the march the ardor which the youth



<P>had acquired when out of view of the field rapidly



<P>faded to nothing.&nbsp; His curiosity was quite easily



<P>satisfied.&nbsp; If an intense scene had caught him with



<P>its wild swing as he came to the top of the bank,



<P>he might have gone roaring on.&nbsp; This advance



<P>upon Nature was too calm.&nbsp; He had opportunity



<P>to reflect.&nbsp; He had time in which to wonder



<P>about himself and to attempt to probe his sensa-



<P>tions.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Absurd ideas took hold upon him.&nbsp; He



<P>thought that he did not relish the landscape.



<P>It threatened him.&nbsp; A coldness swept over his



<P>back, and it is true that his trousers felt to him



<P>that they were no fit for his legs at all.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A house standing placidly in distant fields



<P>had to him an ominous look.&nbsp; The shadows of



<P>the woods were formidable.&nbsp; He was certain that



<P>in this vista there lurked fierce-eyed hosts.&nbsp; The



<P>swift thought came to him that the generals did



<P>not know what they were about.&nbsp; It was all a



<P>trap.&nbsp; Suddenly those close forests would bristle



<P>with rifle barrels.&nbsp; Ironlike brigades would ap-



<P>pear in the rear.&nbsp; They were all going to be



<P>sacrificed.&nbsp; The generals were stupids.&nbsp; The



<P>enemy would presently swallow the whole com-



<P>mand.&nbsp; He glared about him, expecting to see



<P>the stealthy approach of his death.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He thought that he must break from the ranks



<P>and harangue his comrades.&nbsp; They must not all



<P>be killed like pigs; and he was sure it would



<P>come to pass unless they were informed of these



<P>dangers.&nbsp; The generals were idiots to send them



<P>marching into a regular pen.&nbsp; There was but one



<P>pair of eyes in the corps.&nbsp; He would step forth



<P>and make a speech.&nbsp; Shrill and passionate words



<P>came to his lips.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The line, broken into moving fragments by the



<P>ground, went calmly on through fields and woods.



<P>The youth looked at the men nearest him, and



<P>saw, for the most part, expressions of deep inter-



<P>est, as if they were investigating something that



<P>had fascinated them.&nbsp; One or two stepped with



<P>overvaliant airs as if they were already plunged



<P>into war.&nbsp; Others walked as upon thin ice.&nbsp; The



<P>greater part of the untested men appeared quiet



<P>and absorbed.&nbsp; They were going to look at war,



<P>the red animal--war, the blood-swollen god.&nbsp; And



<P>they were deeply engrossed in this march.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As he looked the youth gripped his outcry at



<P>his throat.&nbsp; He saw that even if the men were



<P>tottering with fear they would laugh at his warn-



<P>ing.&nbsp; They would jeer him, and, if practicable,



<P>pelt him with missiles.&nbsp; Admitting that he might



<P>be wrong, a frenzied declamation of the kind



<P>would turn him into a worm.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He assumed, then, the demeanor of one who



<P>knows that he is doomed alone to unwritten re-



<P>sponsibilities.&nbsp; He lagged, with tragic glances at



<P>the sky.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He was surprised presently by the young lieu-



<P>tenant of his company, who began heartily to



<P>beat him with a sword, calling out in a loud and



<P>insolent voice: "Come, young man, get up into



<P>ranks there.&nbsp; No skulking'll do here."&nbsp; He mend-



<P>ed his pace with suitable haste.&nbsp; And he hated



<P>the lieutenant, who had no appreciation of fine



<P>minds.&nbsp; He was a mere brute.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After a time the brigade was halted in the



<P>cathedral light of a forest.&nbsp; The busy skirmish-



<P>ers were still popping.&nbsp; Through the aisles of



<P>the wood could be seen the floating smoke from



<P>their rifles.&nbsp; Sometimes it went up in little balls,



<P>white and compact.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>During this halt many men in the regiment



<P>began erecting tiny hills in front of them.&nbsp; They



<P>used stones, sticks, earth, and anything they



<P>thought might turn a bullet.&nbsp; Some built com-



<P>paratively large ones, while others seemed con-



<P>tent with little ones.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>This procedure caused a discussion among the



<P>men.&nbsp; Some wished to fight like duelists, believ-



<P>ing it to be correct to stand erect and be, from



<P>their feet to their foreheads, a mark.&nbsp; They said



<P>they scorned the devices of the cautious.&nbsp; But



<P>the others scoffed in reply, and pointed to the



<P>veterans on the flanks who were digging at the



<P>ground like terriers.&nbsp; In a short time there was



<P>quite a barricade along the regimental fronts.



<P>Directly, however, they were ordered to with-



<P>draw from that place.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>This astounded the youth.&nbsp; He forgot his



<P>stewing over the advance movement.&nbsp; "Well,



<P>then, what did they march us out here for?" he



<P>demanded of the tall soldier.&nbsp; The latter with



<P>calm faith began a heavy explanation, although



<P>he had been compelled to leave a little protection



<P>of stones and dirt to which he had devoted much



<P>care and skill.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When the regiment was aligned in another



<P>position each man's regard for his safety caused



<P>another line of small intrenchments.&nbsp; They ate



<P>their noon meal behind a third one.&nbsp; They were



<P>moved from this one also.&nbsp; They were marched



<P>from place to place with apparent aimlessness.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth had been taught that a man be-



<P>came another thing in a battle.&nbsp; He saw his sal-



<P>vation in such a change.&nbsp; Hence this waiting



<P>was an ordeal to him.&nbsp; He was in a fever of im-



<P>patience.&nbsp; He considered that there was denoted



<P>a lack of purpose on the part of the generals.



<P>He began to complain to the tall soldier.&nbsp; "I



<P>can't stand this much longer," he cried.&nbsp; "I



<P>don't see what good it does to make us wear



<P>out our legs for nothin'."&nbsp; He wished to return



<P>to camp, knowing that this affair was a blue



<P>demonstration; or else to go into a battle and



<P>discover that he had been a fool in his doubts,



<P>and was, in truth, a man of traditional courage.



<P>The strain of present circumstances he felt to be



<P>intolerable.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The philosophical tall soldier measured a sand-



<P>wich of cracker and pork and swallowed it in a



<P>nonchalant manner.&nbsp; "Oh, I suppose we must go



<P>reconnoitering around the country jest to keep



<P>'em from getting too close, or to develop 'em, or



<P>something."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Huh!" said the loud soldier.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well," cried the youth, still fidgeting, "I'd



<P>rather do anything 'most than go tramping 'round



<P>the country all day doing no good to nobody and



<P>jest tiring ourselves out."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"So would I," said the loud soldier.&nbsp; "It ain't



<P>right.&nbsp; I tell you if anybody with any sense was



<P>a-runnin' this army it--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Oh, shut up!" roared the tall private.&nbsp; "You



<P>little fool.&nbsp; You little damn' cuss.&nbsp; You ain't had



<P>that there coat and them pants on for six months,



<P>and yet you talk as if--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Well, I wanta do some fighting anyway,"



<P>interrupted the other.&nbsp; "I didn't come here to



<P>walk.&nbsp; I could 'ave walked to home--'round an'



<P>'round the barn, if I jest wanted to walk."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The tall one, red-faced, swallowed another



<P>sandwich as if taking poison in despair.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But gradually, as he chewed, his face became



<P>again quiet and contented.&nbsp; He could not rage



<P>in fierce argument in the presence of such sand-



<P>wiches.&nbsp; During his meals he always wore an air



<P>of blissful contemplation of the food he had swal-



<P>lowed.&nbsp; His spirit seemed then to be communing



<P>with the viands.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He accepted new environment and circum-



<P>stance with great coolness, eating from his haver-



<P>sack at every opportunity.&nbsp; On the march he



<P>went along with the stride of a hunter, object-



<P>ing to neither gait nor distance.&nbsp; And he had



<P>not raised his voice when he had been ordered



<P>away from three little protective piles of earth



<P>and stone, each of which had been an engineer-



<P>ing feat worthy of being made sacred to the name



<P>of his grandmother.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In the afternoon the regiment went out over



<P>the same ground it had taken in the morn-



<P>ing.&nbsp; The landscape then ceased to threaten the



<P>youth.&nbsp; He had been close to it and become



<P>familiar with it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When, however, they began to pass into a



<P>new region, his old fears of stupidity and in-



<P>competence reassailed him, but this time he dog-



<P>gedly let them babble.&nbsp; He was occupied with



<P>his problem, and in his desperation he concluded



<P>that the stupidity did not greatly matter.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Once he thought he had concluded that it



<P>would be better to get killed directly and end



<P>his troubles.&nbsp; Regarding death thus out of the



<P>corner of his eye, he conceived it to be noth-



<P>ing but rest, and he was filled with a momen-



<P>tary astonishment that he should have made an



<P>extraordinary commotion over the mere matter



<P>of getting killed.&nbsp; He would die; he would go



<P>to some place where he would be understood.



<P>It was useless to expect appreciation of his pro-



<P>found and fine senses from such men as the lieu-



<P>tenant.&nbsp; He must look to the grave for compre-



<P>hension.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The skirmish fire increased to a long chatter-



<P>ing sound.&nbsp; With it was mingled far-away cheer-



<P>ing.&nbsp; A battery spoke.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Directly the youth would see the skirmishers



<P>running.&nbsp; They were pursued by the sound of



<P>musketry fire.&nbsp; After a time the hot, dangerous



<P>flashes of the rifles were visible.&nbsp; Smoke clouds



<P>went slowly and insolently across the fields like



<P>observant phantoms.&nbsp; The din became crescendo,



<P>like the roar of an oncoming train.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A brigade ahead of them and on the right



<P>went into action with a rending roar.&nbsp; It was



<P>as if it had exploded.&nbsp; And thereafter it lay



<P>stretched in the distance behind a long gray wall,



<P>that one was obliged to look twice at to make



<P>sure that it was smoke.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth, forgetting his neat plan of getting



<P>killed, gazed spell bound.&nbsp; His eyes grew wide



<P>and busy with the action of the scene.&nbsp; His



<P>mouth was a little ways open.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Of a sudden he felt a heavy and sad hand laid



<P>upon his shoulder.&nbsp; Awakening from his trance



<P>of observation he turned and beheld the loud



<P>soldier.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"It's my first and last battle, old boy," said



<P>the latter, with intense gloom.&nbsp; He was quite



<P>pale and his girlish lip was trembling.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Eh?" murmured the youth in great aston-



<P>ishment.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"It's my first and last battle, old boy,"



<P>continued the loud soldier.&nbsp; "Something tells



<P>me--"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"What?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"I'm a gone coon this first time and--and I



<P>w-want you to take these here things--to--my--



<P>folks."&nbsp; He ended in a quavering sob of pity for



<P>himself.&nbsp; He handed the youth a little packet



<P>done up in a yellow envelope.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Why, what the devil--" began the youth



<P>again.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But the other gave him a glance as from the



<P>depths of a tomb, and raised his limp hand in a



<P>prophetic manner and turned away.



<P>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>CHAPTER XVII.</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>THIS advance of the enemy had seemed to the



<P>youth like a ruthless hunting.&nbsp; He began to fume



<P>with rage and exasperation.&nbsp; He beat his foot



<P>upon the ground, and scowled with hate at the



<P>swirling smoke that was approaching like a phan-



<P>tom flood.&nbsp; There was a maddening quality in



<P>this seeming resolution of the foe to give him no



<P>rest, to give him no time to sit down and think.



<P>Yesterday he had fought and had fled rapidly.



<P>There had been many adventures.&nbsp; For to-day he



<P>felt that he had earned opportunities for contem-



<P>plative repose.&nbsp; He could have enjoyed portraying



<P>to uninitiated listeners various scenes at which he



<P>had been a witness or ably discussing the pro-



<P>cesses of war with other proved men.&nbsp; Too it was



<P>important that he should have time for physical



<P>recuperation.&nbsp; He was sore and stiff from his ex-



<P>periences.&nbsp; He had received his fill of all exer-



<P>tions, and he wished to rest.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But those other men seemed never to grow



<P>weary; they were fighting with their old speed.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>163



<P>He had a wild hate for the relentless foe.&nbsp; Yester-



<P>day, when he had imagined the universe to be



<P>against him, he had hated it, little gods and big



<P>gods; to-day he hated the army of the foe with



<P>the same great hatred.&nbsp; He was not going to be



<P>badgered of his life, like a kitten chased by boys,



<P>he said.&nbsp; It was not well to drive men into final



<P>corners; at those moments they could all develop



<P>teeth and claws.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He leaned and spoke into his friend's ear.&nbsp; He



<P>menaced the woods with a gesture.&nbsp; "If they



<P>keep on chasing us, by Gawd, they'd better watch



<P>out.&nbsp; Can't stand TOO much."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The friend twisted his head and made a calm



<P>reply.&nbsp; "If they keep on a-chasin' us they'll drive



<P>us all inteh th' river."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth cried out savagely at this state-



<P>ment.&nbsp; He crouched behind a little tree, with his



<P>eyes burning hatefully and his teeth set in a cur-



<P>like snarl.&nbsp; The awkward bandage was still about



<P>his head, and upon it, over his wound, there was



<P>a spot of dry blood.&nbsp; His hair was wondrously



<P>tousled, and some straggling, moving locks hung



<P>over the cloth of the bandage down toward his



<P>forehead.&nbsp; His jacket and shirt were open at the



<P>throat, and exposed his young bronzed neck.



<P>There could be seen spasmodic gulpings at his



<P>throat.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>His fingers twined nervously about his rifle.



<P>He wished that it was an engine of annihilating



<P>power.&nbsp; He felt that he and his companions were



<P>being taunted and derided from sincere convic-



<P>tions that they were poor and puny.&nbsp; His knowl-



<P>edge of his inability to take vengeance for it made



<P>his rage into a dark and stormy specter, that pos-



<P>sessed him and made him dream of abominable



<P>cruelties.&nbsp; The tormentors were flies sucking in-



<P>solently at his blood, and he thought that he would



<P>have given his life for a revenge of seeing their



<P>faces in pitiful plights.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The winds of battle had swept all about the



<P>regiment, until the one rifle, instantly followed by



<P>others, flashed in its front.&nbsp; A moment later the



<P>regiment roared forth its sudden and valiant re-



<P>tort.&nbsp; A dense wall of smoke settled slowly down.



<P>It was furiously slit and slashed by the knifelike



<P>fire from the rifles.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>To the youth the fighters resembled animals



<P>tossed for a death struggle into a dark pit.&nbsp; There



<P>was a sensation that he and his fellows, at bay,



<P>were pushing back, always pushing fierce on-



<P>slaughts of creatures who were slippery.&nbsp; Their



<P>beams of crimson seemed to get no purchase upon



<P>the bodies of their foes; the latter seemed to evade



<P>them with ease, and come through, between,



<P>around, and about with unopposed skill.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When, in a dream, it occurred to the youth



<P>that his rifle was an impotent stick, he lost sense



<P>of everything but his hate, his desire to smash



<P>into pulp the glittering smile of victory which he



<P>could feel upon the faces of his enemies.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The blue smoke-swallowed line curled and



<P>writhed like a snake stepped upon.&nbsp; It swung its



<P>ends to and fro in an agony of fear and rage.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The youth was not conscious that he was erect



<P>upon his feet.&nbsp; He did not know the direction of



<P>the ground.&nbsp; Indeed, once he even lost the habit



<P>of balance and fell heavily.&nbsp; He was up again



<P>immediately.&nbsp; One thought went through the



<P>chaos of his brain at the time.&nbsp; He wondered if



<P>he had fallen because he had been shot.&nbsp; But the



<P>suspicion flew away at once.&nbsp; He did not think



<P>more of it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He had taken up a first position behind the lit-



<P>tle tree, with a direct determination to hold it



<P>against the world.&nbsp; He had not deemed it possi-



<P>ble that his army could that day succeed, and



<P>from this he felt the ability to fight harder.&nbsp; But



<P>the throng had surged in all ways, until he lost



<P>directions and locations, save that he knew where



<P>lay the enemy.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The flames bit him, and the hot smoke broiled



<P>his skin.&nbsp; His rifle barrel grew so hot that ordi-



<P>narily he could not have borne it upon his palms;



<P>but he kept on stuffing cartridges into it, and



<P>pounding them with his clanking, bending ram-



<P>rod.&nbsp; If he aimed at some changing form through



<P>the smoke, he pulled his trigger with a fierce



<P>grunt, as if he were dealing a blow of the fist with



<P>all his strength.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When the enemy seemed falling back before



<P>him and his fellows, he went instantly forward,



<P>like a dog who, seeing his foes lagging, turns and



<P>insists upon being pursued.&nbsp; And when he was



<P>compelled to retire again, he did it slowly, sul-



<P>lenly, taking steps of wrathful despair.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Once he, in his intent hate, was almost alone,



<P>and was firing, when all those near him had ceased.



<P>He was so engrossed in his occupation that he



<P>was not aware of a lull.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He was recalled by a hoarse laugh and a sen-



<P>tence that came to his ears in a voice of contempt



<P>and amazement.&nbsp; "Yeh infernal fool, don't yeh



<P>know enough t' quit when there ain't anything t'



<P>shoot at?&nbsp; Good Gawd!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He turned then and, pausing with his rifle



<P>thrown half into position, looked at the blue line



<P>of his comrades.&nbsp; During this moment of leisure



<P>they seemed all to be engaged in staring with



<P>astonishment at him.&nbsp; They had become specta-



<P>tors.&nbsp; Turning to the front again he saw, under



<P>the lifted smoke, a deserted ground.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He looked bewildered for a moment.&nbsp; Then



<P>there appeared upon the glazed vacancy of his



<P>eyes a diamond point of intelligence.&nbsp; "Oh," he



<P>said, comprehending.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He returned to his comrades and threw him-



<P>self upon the ground.&nbsp; He sprawled like a man



<P>who had been thrashed.&nbsp; His flesh seemed strange-



<P>ly on fire, and the sounds of the battle continued



<P>in his ears.&nbsp; He groped blindly for his canteen.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The lieutenant was crowing.&nbsp; He seemed



<P>drunk with fighting.&nbsp; He called out to the youth:



<P>"By heavens, if I had ten thousand wild cats like



<P>you I could tear th' stomach outa this war in



<P>less'n a week!"&nbsp; He puffed out his chest with



<P>large dignity as he said it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Some of the men muttered and looked at the



<P>youth in awe-struck ways.&nbsp; It was plain that as



<P>he had gone on loading and firing and cursing



<P>without the proper intermission, they had found



<P>time to regard him.&nbsp; And they now looked upon



<P>him as a war devil.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The friend came staggering to him.&nbsp; There



<P>was some fright and dismay in his voice.&nbsp; "Are yeh



<P>all right, Fleming?&nbsp; Do yeh feel all right?&nbsp; There



<P>ain't nothin' th' matter with yeh, Henry, is there?"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"No," said the youth with difficulty.&nbsp; His



<P>throat seemed full of knobs and burs.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>These incidents made the youth ponder.&nbsp; It



<P>was revealed to him that he had been a barbarian,



<P>a beast.&nbsp; He had fought like a pagan who de-



<P>fends his religion.&nbsp; Regarding it, he saw that it



<P>was fine, wild, and, in some ways, easy.&nbsp; He had



<P>been a tremendous figure, no doubt.&nbsp; By this



<P>struggle he had overcome obstacles which he



<P>had admitted to be mountains.&nbsp; They had fallen



<P>like paper peaks, and he was now what he called



<P>a hero.&nbsp; And he had not been aware of the pro-



<P>cess.&nbsp; He had slept and, awakening, found him-



<P>self a knight.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>He lay and basked in the occasional stares of



<P>his comrades.&nbsp; Their faces were varied in de-



<P>grees of blackness from the burned powder.



<P>Some were utterly smudged.&nbsp; They were reek-



<P>ing with perspiration, and their breaths came



<P>hard and wheezing.&nbsp; And from these soiled ex-



<P>panses they peered at him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Hot work!&nbsp; Hot work!" cried the lieu-



<P>tenant deliriously.&nbsp; He walked up and down,



<P>restless and eager.&nbsp; Sometimes his voice could



<P>be heard in a wild, incomprehensible laugh.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When he had a particularly profound thought



<P>upon the science of war he always unconsciously



<P>addressed himself to the youth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There was some grim rejoicing by the men.



<P>"By thunder, I bet this army'll never see another



<P>new reg'ment like us!"



<P>"You bet!"

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp; "A dog, a woman, an' a walnut tree,



<P>&nbsp;&nbsp; Th' more yeh beat 'em, th' better they be!

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>That's like us."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Lost a piler men, they did.&nbsp; If an' ol' woman



<P>swep' up th' woods she'd git a dustpanful."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>"Yes, an' if she'll come around ag'in in 'bout



<P>an' hour she'll git a pile more."

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The forest still bore its burden of clamor.



<P>From off under the trees came the rolling clatter



<P>of the musketry.&nbsp; Each distant thicket seemed a



<P>strange porcupine with quills of flame.&nbsp; A cloud



<P>of dark smoke, as from smoldering ruins, went



<P>up toward the sun now bright and gay in the



<P>blue, enameled sky.



<P>
